The large sale for EUMENTHOL JUJUBES
has led to a number of imitations being manu-
factured under the name of menthol and other
jujubes. It is important, therefore, when buying
to be sure and get HUDSON’S EUMENTHOL
JUJUBES. You may be asked to buy other
jujubes, and be told they are “just as good”
as Hudson’s Eumenthol Jujubes. This proves
that these imitations and substitutes have no
reputation of their own, and can only be sold
,on the good repute and high character of Hud-
son's Eumenthol Jujubes. Sold by all chemists.

MOSES JUNIOR.
Fiction
J. D. Beresford can be interesting: in fact
his reader sometimes comes to the conclu-
sion that J. D. Beresford deliberately makes
up his mind to be interesting. These
Lynnekers (Cassell) is a solid book
about solid people, solidly written. These
Lynnekers are not exactly exciting people,
and nothing much really happens to them,
but they are very real people. The quite
legitimate, question whether such people are
worth writing about, whether, indeed, there
is any necessity for them to exist at all, is
answered by the fact that J. D. Beresford
can make them interesting in spite of their
dullness. And for Australians there is an
added interest in the joyful fact that these
Lynnekers arc practically extinct in this
continent. It was a household where “diffi-
culties were debated, but it was rare for any-
one to take action. The average trouble,
whatever it was, righted itself in time.
Something always happened sooner or later.”
But, having as a family been decorously in-
distinguished for generations, the Lynneker
blood was running a little thin. Intellec-
tually the Lynnekers were dropping behind
their own times; their Conservatism was fall-
ing into senile decay. But the head of the
family, a rector, “took his own family as
the standard of English culture, and deplored
not the Lynneker loss of vitality, but the
growing vulgarity of the new generation.”
And into this family of inertia a son was
born who actually purposed doing things.
Naturally the family was shocked: an elder
brother once even went to the extent of pas-
sionately upsetting the salt-cellar and slam-
ming the door behind him as he went out.
“He had done as much as any Lynneker
could in the face of great opposition. The
utter abandonment implied by his spilling of
the salt must surely show that he was
intensely in earnest.” But Dick had imme-
diately forgotten the scene; he was too
much occupied in his own problems. He goes
into a bank, and ultimately gets a good posi-
tion as the secretary of a famous financier;
but after 300 pages he had not yet settled
what he wants to do in life, or even met the
girl that he is to marry. (This is fair
warning: an author who forgets the girl for
300 pages takes risks.) True, there is the
elopement of the other “outsider” in the
family, Dick’s sister, who clears with the
village carpenter; but she is only an inci-
dent. Dick’s efforts to understand politics,
to settle for himself the question of religion,
and, above all, to settle the question of him-
self, is all the story, with, of course, that
tremendous background of the Lynneker
inertia.
“They accepted and glorified the past.
They referred to it as to an ideal that might
and should be reconstructed. His father’s
test began: Tn my days’.... that comfort-
able day in which, it seemed now, there was
never anything new and disturbing Step
by step they might be followed back to some
primitive ancestor who had preferred flint to
the new-fangled bronze weapon that had
driven his tribe westward. But always they
had been driven forward. Some power,
invisible and indefinable, had always over-
come their inertia, tumbled them one step
onward, and left them to deplore the won-
derful past out of which they had been
mercilessly thrust —and to congratulate
themselves on the fact that in spite of almost
insupprable difficulties they had risen to the
occasion.”
The charm of this deliberately interesting
book lies in the realism with which this
quaint family is depicted. At the end of the
novel you have got to know these Lynnekers
quite well. Whether anyone would care to
know them in real life is beyond the ques-
tion. Evidently J. D. Beresford did.
May Sinclair, the greatest living English
woman novelist, fails in her latest book
Tasker Jevons (Hutchinson, per Dymock’s
Library) only because she is a woman. The
Three Sisters was a work of art: Tasker
Jevons is a highly entertaining story. May
Sinclair is never dull. But the flaw is her
pretence that the narrator is a man. She
writes in the first person, but the figure of
a man that she projects is too obviously the
sort of man she would be if she weren’t a
woman. In fact, he is a woman, first cousin
to W. J. Locke's later impersonations.
This is curious, since May Sinclair’s picture
of Jevons rings horribly true, even seen
through the eyes of her semi-masculine obser-
ver. If Jevons isn’t moulded on Arnold
Bennett, then she must have had under
microscopic observation a disciple of Arnold
Bennett. But there can’t be many of those
about. The next novel by Bennett ought to
have May Sinclair in it.
Tasker Jevons is meant to be “the real
story” of a successful author. “I know I
am Writing about a man whom many people
still consider a great novelist and a great
playwright. But to me what he has written
matters so little; it has no interest for me
except. as his vehicle, the vehicle in which
he arrived.... He wanted most awfully to
arrive. How far he took himself seriously
nobody will ever know. . . .He was too clever,
I think, ever to have quite convinced himself.
.... His first novel, he told me, was calcu-
lated, deliberately, to startle and arrest; to
hit the public, rather unpleasantly, in the
eye. That, he said, was the way to be remem-
bered. It wouldn’t sell. He didn’t want it
to sell. _ What he wanted first was to gain
a position; then to consolidate it; then to
build. His second novel would be designed
to counteract the disagreeable effects of his
first.... If he went on being disagreeable,
he’d alienate the very sections of the public
he most wanted to gain. It was in his third
novel that he meant, still deliberately, to
come into his kingdom. His third novel, he
declared, would sell: and it would be his
best. On that utterly secure and yet ele-
vated basis he could build afterwards pretty
much as he pleased.... That third novel was
to start his big sales. The worst of a big
sale was this, that when you’d caught your
public you were bound to go on giving them
the sort of thing you’d caught them with;
therefore, he’d be jolly careful to start ’em
with the sort of thing he happened to like
himself, otherwise he’d have to spend the
rest of his life knuckling under to them. He
could get a cheaper glory if he chose to try
for it; but a cheaper glory wouldn’t satisfy
him.... Besides, he wanted his glory and his
pay quick. He couldn’t afford to wait a
month longer than his third novel. As for
the different quality in the glory it would
be years before anybody but himself could
tell the difference, and by the time they
spotted him he’d be at another game. A
game in which he defied anybody to catch
him out. He’d be writing plays.”
(Arnold Bennett’s first novels were: A
Man from the North, The Grand Babylon
Hotel, and Anna of the Five Towns. The
Old Wives’ Tale, his greatest novel, did not
come till 10 years after his start.)
“There’s no good trying to express such a
phenomenon as Jevons in terms of literature.
You can only think about him in terms of
action, every book of his being an onslaught
by which he laid his public 10w.... He had
studied the public he set out to capture. He
presented the life it knew —the moving,
changing, fantastically adventurous life of
the middle classes. Until Jevons rushed on
them and forced their eyes open, you may say
at the point of the bayonet, the. middle
classes didn’t know they were moving and
changing and being adventurous. Nobody
knew. It was Jevons’s discovery.”
May Sinclair even discloses his secret.
“ ‘You can do anything,’ he said, ‘if you’re
only funny enough.’ ” The whole thing was
as calculable as any successful deal on the
Stock Exchange. “When you asked him: ‘Then
why can’t other people do it?’ he said: ‘God
knows why. They must be precious fools if
they want to do it and don’t find out how.
I’ve had to find out.’ ”
But it is the real Jevons, the little, kindly,
almost lovable bounder in private life, that
May Sinclair has to do with. She analyses
his personality with the cold skill of a scien-
tist, but without that deep sympathy that
she gave to the poet in The Divine Fire.
There is his amazing marriage with the
wonderful Viola, after their innocent esca-
pade in Belgium, about which May Sinclair
worries overmuch. There are the effects of
his growing popularity and wealth- —the
period when the little man felt that he need
no longer keep a jealous watch upon his
aitches and other vulgar habits —about which
May Sinclair grows rather unnecessarily
annoyed. There is his ignorance l 'of furniture
—according to May Sinclair, this is the
seventh of the deadly sins. To this page a
wrong assortment of “periods” in furniture
is hardly a crime. The cult of furniture is
one of the 10 commandments of English
society that the war seems most likely to
abrogate.
One turns with relief from these false
fastidities of May Sinclair to the comedy she
extracts from Jevons’s infatuation for his new
motor-car. It was such a wonderful car that
Jevons felt it a sacrilege to take it out. It
might get a speck of dust or a spot of rain
on its immaculate surface. All Jevons did
was to worship it in its altar, the garage.
But the war came and found a use for the
car. In fact, May Sinclair seems to have
herself declared war on Germany just for the
purpose of providing a background for Jevons
and his car. The adventures of Jevons and
of the nice young man who follows Jevons
to Belgium with the novelist’s wife, set
against that awful background, have the air
of foolish comedy. The wild rush of the two
motor-cars over the landscape is ludicrously
incongruous: one very nearly comes to the
conclusion that Belgium had been invaded on
behalf of Jevons and his car. The little man
dominates the situation, instead of being
swallowed up by that tragedy of the destruc-
tion of a nation. And Viola herself seems to
imagine that the war is merely a new hobby
for herself. But she learns the truth from her
exasperated husband: “If you think you’re
any more comfort to the wounded than you
are to me I can tell you you’re mistaken.
There was a poor devil at Lokeren the other
day with a bullet in his stomach who told
me he didn’t mind his wounds, and he didn’t
mind the Germans; what worried him was
the lady being there when he wasn’t able
to defend her... .We all think you heroic,
and you are sometimes useful. I think you
adorable in a tunic and breeches, and
General Roubaix agrees with me. But there
isn’t a thing you’ve done yet that a man
can’t do better.... And when you’re in the
lines you’ve a moral effect equal to about 10
17-inch guns. If the men see you hovering
about their trenches they’re so jumpy they
can hardly hold their rifles." And Viola
remarks, "What about Joan of Arc?”
May Sinclair’s malicious study of an
Arnold Bennett type is delightful; but she
gives away her case when she adds: “Don’t
ask me what would have happened to Jevons
and his wife if there hadn’t been a war.”
The fatal flaw in the book, as a work of art,
is that May Sinclair deliberately makes use
of the war in order to settle up the domestic
differences between a husband and his (of
course) childless wife. It is like hiring an
elephant to decide, with his foot, a dispute
between two ants.
Australian
William X., who signs himself Jas. A.
Ross, is politely requested to supply an
explanation of his drama, The Midnight
Sun (Sands, Sydney). Apparently it means
something. This page learns that it is the
third of a trilogy, and it is referred “for
further explanation” to The Daum, the
curtain-raiser to The Midnight Sun. But
as The Dawn is not included, this page is
still in darkness. The persons represented
include Kato (King of Diamonds),
Orphanus (Prince of Peace), Carlyle, Eros,
Kitchener, Tageta (a Maori Princess), The
Pleiades, The Little Baby Stars, The After-
glow, Twigg (a curate), Venus, Ghizeh and
Jentel Lamb (a millionaire). The scenes
take place in the Blue Mountains, chiefly at
Lcura Baths, which are called “Leurasjem,”
and the dramatist asks us to note that this
word contains the same letters as Jerusalem!
Here is the brilliant climax to the first
act. The characters left are Twigg, the
curate, and Eros, dressed as a girl.
Ekos: Are you a shade?
Twigg : No, young lady, I’m the curate!
Ekos : What do you cure ? Fish!
Twigg : Demented ! Bacon !
Ekos : What’s demented Bacon?
Twigg : Shakespeare!
Ekos: Do you mean Orphanus?
Twigg : No! I’ve never heard of him!
Ekos: Strange! Do you eat grubs?
Twigg : No, the grubs eat me!
Eros : Poor grubs! How sad ! Etc.
But the final tableau is more interesting.
Orphanus and other characters are seated
upon a golden disc on a square marble base.
“At the foot of the throne steps is Venus
lying at the feet of Britannia, who is
guarded on either side by John Bull and
Kitchener.” In front are The Baby Stars
and the Afterglow. “Between these and the
outer circle of the women is a circle of
white everlasting daisies and a pink rose
cross at the further side separating the
words
WILL X I AM
CHRIST.
This is the shield of Orphanus.... In gold
letters on the white base of the throne is
seen Will-iam X. (meaning Will: I am
Christ). It is the stroke of the pen that
converts the name of William 11. to that
taken up by Christ; hence the meaning of
the words of Hamlet in The Dawn, when
pointing to the red cross on the white
screen, ‘lt is written ! Thy will be done.’ ”
After pointing out that the bloke’s name
is Wilhelm, not William, this page retires
baffled.
A lordly tribute to the genius of the late
J. J. Hilder, Australia’s finest water-colorist,
is presented by a group of his friends in
the form of J. J. Hilder, Water-Dolorist, a
charming book of color reproductions of his
most characteristic work, with appreciations
of the man and his pictures, published by
Tyrrella, Sydney, in a limited edition of
500 copies at ss. The book is typographic-
ally a credit to any country, and the color
blocks, with the exception of one block made
at the Government Printing Office, Sydney,
reach a level that must be near the highest
yet reached in Australia. The other blocks
were etched by Hartland and Hyde, and
the printing was done by Arthur McQuitty,
both of Sydney. As the profits go to the
painter’s widow, it is a matter for congratu-
lation that the whole edition is entirely
disposed of. Hilder died of tuberculosis at
Hornsby last April, at the early age of 34.
From the biography by Bertram Stevens
we learn that he was born in Queensland,
and left school to become a clerk in the Bank
of N.S.W., Sydney. Once again in Aus-
tralian art-history, it was Julian* Ashton
who put this youth, without any previous
artistic training, on the right track. The
Hilder panel at the 1907 exhibition of the
Sydney Society of Artists created a sensa-
tion. So successful was his work that he
was soon afterwards enabled to leave the
Bank and look to his art for a living. Julian
Ashton says: “In his earlier work he had
a gem-like method of mosaic, which placed
accurately-drawn forms of brilliant color
in juxtaposition with an effect akin to
jewellery. Later, he broadened and har-
monised and simplified, until in the best
examples of his work, Hilder, as a land-
scapist, is not only unsurpassed here, but I
make bold to say would take the highest
place in any collection of modern water-
colors.” Lionel Lindsay says that “within
the boundaries of a few square inches of
paper ililder became a little master. “His
command of blue, the most difficult of colors
ou the palette to manage, was astonishingly
sure. Lindsay finds in Hilder’s very early
works a certain hardness, giving them the
effect of little posters; but his middle period
represents his period of greatest originality,
when, “his work resembles no predecessor’s,
and his undoubted originality is a distinct
contribution to the art of his time.” Later,
his manner broadened; his touch became
more facile and less precious. “Hilder is
our first romantic painter His fame is
already assured.” This confident prophecy is
amply confirmed by the exquisite examples
of his work reproduced in this book, and the
wonderful memorial exhibition of his work
now open in Sydney.
“The Olives,” who is “preparing a paper
on Australian Poetry to be read at a meet-
ing of the local Poetry Society,” expects us
to do his job for him. He wants detailed
information on the following points: When
did The Bulletin “School of Poets” have
its origin? Why did it have an origin?
Who are the scholars of that school? How
many copies of the works of each scholar
have been sold? What is the finest verse
produced in that school? And, finally,
“Could you mention any matters of parti-
cular interest in dealing with this subject?”
The last question is the only one we need
answer. There is no Bulletin school of
poetry. Poetry is Poetry, and most of the
poetry written by Australian poets has
appeared in The Bulletin. This paper
has encouraged the writing of Australasian
poetry by publishing the stuff and paying
for it. But it does not tell poets to write
any particular brand of poetry—because
there are no particular brands of poetry.
“Chink” demui;ely asks what is The
Bulletin’s attitude toward the split infini-
tive, guilelessly pointing out that this page
“would stretch a point to avoid dividing the
infinitive, whereas the lapse often occurs in
the general columns and even in ‘Plain Eng-
lish’ on occasions.” “Chink” instances this
page’s use of the phrase: “He fails quite to
understand,” where the sense might be taken
in two different ways. Presumably he means
that the sentence might mean either“He
quite fails to understand,” or “He fails to
quite understand.” The answer to “Chink”
is that if the sentence meant the former, it
would have been plainly written that way.
But as to the general attitude to the
“splintive,” this page prefers deliberately not
to split if it can be prevented; but it means
to blanky well split whenever added empha-
sis can be given by that means. And on the
other pages of this paper the writers doubt-
less also use the “splintive” as a servant
and not a master. There is a dislike to split-
ting on the part of formal grammarians, their
theory being that, for example, the two
words, to and split, are really one word, being
the infinitive form of a verb, and that there-
fore no word should come between them.
But the writer can take that rule, like other
grammarians’ rules, merely for his guid-
ance. Where greater ease or greater empha-
sis is necessary, you may split if you please.
Your own sense of style should be your best
guide. But if “Chink” wants this page
definitely to ban the “splintive,” that is a
thing that this page means to blanky well
refuse.
Modern Horse Management, by R. S.
Timmis, of the Royal Canadian Dragoons
(Cassell; 12s. 6d.), is a new encyclopaedia of
the moke. In this big book the author prof-
fers information of everything concerning the
horse, from the psychology of the horse to
the anatomy and use of the horse’s tail, from
the crime of docking horses’ tails to the
bacteriology of his diseases, from the theory
of grooming to the principles of stable con-
struction, from ladies riding astride to the
final humane destruction of the moke. The
book contains 500 photographs and diagrams
illustrating all possible practical points, and
reproduces many pictures and statues of the
horse in art, as well as photographic por-
traits of most of the famous horses in the
world. As there are several blokes on this
continent who are more interested in horses
than in motor-cars, the book should find
purchasers. Incidentally, the author repro :
duces the worst poem upon the horse that has
been written. As the horse is said to possess
an extraordinary imagination this poem may
have been written by a moke. The poem
ends thus:—
The friend of King, of Prince, or Tsar,
But does not like a motor-ear.
With champing steel and restless paw,
He waits outside his lordship’s door;
Eager to start with dashing speed,
The cruel whip he does not need.
When kindness greets his listening ear,
He’ll safely take you far and near.
With mournful pride and pluming crest.
He takes us to our final rest.
DORN-MAWN.
“Not a word about dawn-morn. That controversy
is dead.” —Red Page, 8/6/’16.
I have for you the frabjeous scorn,
That noble midnight feels for dawn!
Why were you critics ever born ?
Bah! Go and put your head in pawn!
Do you expect the Muse to fawn,
Sweet singers grow projag, forlorn,
Because the Red Page says that “sawn”
Is but an oompty rhyme for “thorn.”
My poet’s license I have torn,
No more I swink till graveyards yawn.
I write no more. To-morrow morn
I go to work concocting brawn.
N.F.S.

The STRAIGHT TIP
HERE are cogent reasons why everyone

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Love —and What Then?
Faith betrayed, and all that pother,
Tears and torment... .oh, let be!
If you choose to love another,
What is that to me?
If you stoon to dupe and trick me,
Why should I, forlorn, repine?
Love’s keen shaft can wound and prick me
Only while you’re mine.
So, dear lady, if you hold me
Lightly, if deceit's your trade,
Other arms will soon enfold me....
Get you gone, slim jade!
MX. Eixeeslie Grey.
Fallen Petals.
Hot-heart and flaringrcyes,
Where are you now?
Do you stay safe at home,
Keeping some vow?
Are you a wanderer
Under the sky,
Restless, unsatisfied,
Even as I?
Gipsy-one, petulant.
Haunting me yet,
Oh, the mad mouth of you,
Who could forget?
1 Ghost of a summer gone,
Mocking my sleep,
Holding white hands to me
I cannot keep!
Wilful and passionate,
Whom do you love?
What has lie won from you,
Rose or a glove?
Do you remember me?
How that you said:
‘‘Sweetheart, I worship you!”
Is it all dead?
Laughing-heart, sunny-eyes,
Have you learnt pain,
Sorrow or loneliness,
Hunger in vain?
Wiitched all your old beliefs
One by one die?
Tasted Gethsemane?
Dear, so have I!
Child-woman, heart o’ me!
Be where you may,
Weeping for broken toys,
Laughing at play,
Out of my thoughts for you
This one is best—
After your journeyings
There will come rest!
N.S.W.
MALCOLM ARNOLD.
The Threshold.
Just as the first glad brightness in the skies
Shows in the east to say the'day has come,
I saw a taking wonder in your eyes;
Your lips were parted, though your voice
was dumb,
Moved by a breath like that sweet breeze
that seems
To waken earth to passion from its dreams.
And as the earth in waking seems to stir,
Your breast with restless heavings warmly
moved.
O girl of passion! Innocent you were,
Glad in the thought of loving—being loved !
And all the world seemed, like-the dawn, to
sing,
When love was pure and fresh—and every-
thing!
Dreams are more true than anything we
know,
And so vision I shall truly hold,
Though with the years , the day’s first
wonders go,
And freshness stales, and passion’s fires
grow cold.
My faithful heart the work of time denies;
I saw the dawn of love within your eyes!
N.S.W.
HAMER.
Morning.
All the world was morning sweet.
All the ways were bright with song.
Laughter, straying round our feet,
Trailed an echo faint and long—
Yellow hair and dancing feet.
Morning turned you to a song.
Music, as when earth was young.
Trembled down between the trees;
Dust of dreams was lightly flung
On the sighings of the breeze;
All your breath was music flung
Lightly to the sighing trees.
Laughter, music, dream and song—
Earth arose the sun to kiss
With a pulse too full and strong,
Morning’s wedded joy to miss.
Girl, the wine of dreams is strong;
Morning turned you to a kiss!
N.S.W.
D. M. W.
I Heard My Heart.
I heard my heart like some old engine
thumping
Deep in my chest, and Journey’s End seemed
near,
As when at sea some wearied piston bump-
ing
Calls out for rest to some grey engineer.
How many miles? How many years of
steaming
Across blue seas and oceans black as
jet?. . . .
Unanswered all the queries of my dream-
ing!
Beat on ! Beat true ! We’ll find the Harbor
yet!.
N.S.W.

THURSDAY, JULY 27, 1916.
THE SOLDIER AND THE JOB.
It is generally conceded tliat if the obliga-
tions of Empire threw upon Australia the
responsibility of fighting the German, the
obligations of ordinary decency demand that
the fair thing be done by the men who take
up arms. To some extent these have been
met by (1) paying the army more liberally
than any army was ever paid before; (2)
making more liberal provision by way of
allowances and pensions than any other
country engaged in the disturbance; and
(;5) generously subscribing for the susten-
ance and treatment of the wounded and
for the amusement and entertainment of
the unwounded. But all this has discharged
tally part of our responsibilities. There
remain others. Among them are those con-
nected with the problem of “repatriation.
Up to the present we have not got beyond
the stage of passing resolutions and talking
in a vague, clamorous way about doing
something, and the difficulties to be faced,
and the need for action and all. the rest of
it. But what is going to be done and by
whom and how and when is by no means
as clear as it ought to be. Indeed, there is so
much vagueness about it that “repatriation”
threatens to become merely a shibboleth, or
an excuse for consulting Cohen and making
horrified remarks about the labor market.
It seems to The Bulletin that after two
years of war. and with thousands of men
already back from the Front, the time is
about ripe to definitely understand this
business. It has been messed with long
enough. And it is not unreasonable to
inquire when “difficulties” are mentioned to
what extent these have been manufactured
for the occasion, and whether they have
anything to do with the war, or whether
they did not exist prior to the departure of
the troops and will remain in existence long
after the army has left off khaki and got into
moleskins.
The responsibilities of the people of
Australia towards the army are heavy and
definite in several directions. The widows
of the fallen, the fatherless children, the
men who are sent home permanently
maimed, those who return partially disabled
and all who come back sick or in any way
incapacitated are and must be the nation s
care. And the extent to which justice is
done to these victims of the war will be the
measure of the national sense of honor.
Every good Australian would have these
responsibilities discharged to the utmost
farthing. The Bulletin believes they will
be; Australia’s form of government makes
it certain that in this direction the Common-
wealth will perpetrate no mistakes. 1 he
liability will be easily expressed m terms
ot £ s. d., and the bill will be on the table
for public inspection. It is going to be no
small bill either. The toll exacted on our
tioops at Gallipoli was great. In all human
probability the toll taken in France and
Flanders will be equally heavy. Our men
are fighting no mean enemy, and the task
before the armies is stupendous. All this
means a big final reckoning—a big burden
for Australia to bear. Australia carries a
good solid weight already. This one added
is going to try the nation’s strength as it
has never been tried before. And because
this is so. and because the burden must be
carried, there are very substantial reasons
for being exceedingly careful that Australia
is not stampeded into projects that will
have the double effect of increasing the load
and decreasing the national ability to carry
it. By hare-brained schemes of land-resump-
tion and getting more deeply into debt the
two things can be accomplished with prompt-
ness and despatch.
So far as it lias gone the talk about
“repatriation” has been built upon a theory
that men coming back from the war will
experience great difficulty in obtaining
employment —that Australia, having de-
nuded herself of, say, 300,000 of her
strongest, most active men, will be unable,
without causing chaos or something, to take
them back. If this theory rests on a sound
foundation it is a frightful condemnation
of past Australian statesmanship. But this
paper refuses to believe that there is nothing
for them to do in Australia, and resolutely
declines to agree that in order to find them
something to do more of the public credit
must be pawned to buy out landowners, or
that there must be further trafficking with
Cohen to “develop our great national
resources,” which have already been
developed at staggering cost to everyone
concerned. At a conference of Common-
wealth and State Governments held at Mel-
bourne in May someone announced that he
“shuddered to think what might happen if
200,000 or 250,000 men were to_ return and
try to get back into their old niches or try
to* find an opportunity to make a livelihood,
unless there was some thorough organisa-
tion in view of their arrival.” W hat is
there to shudder at —the arrival of the
men, an apprehension that the weary old
borrowing imposture is about played out,
or a suspicion that Australia is going to
insist as hitherto on sending work to
foreigners instead of doing it on _ the
premises? Of course a quarter of a million
men are not going to be dumped down in
one spot to run around like ants looking for
a job. These men have been mustered from
a continent three million square miles in
area. They will return gradually to the
districts they came from, will be sprinkled
over the landscape from the Leeuwin to
Carpentaria, and it is an idle fear to appre-
hend that tlie vast majority will find them-
selves in respect to employment in any
worse position than they were before they
went away. But there is no idle fear in
the apprehension that Australian politicians
may use the talk about “repatriation” as a
screen to cover more borrowing, more going
into debt, so that they may hold up the
ruinous institution they coll “prosperity.”
Somebody in a moment of transport
promised that men going to the Front would
on their return to Australia be “given
farms.” This has boiled down to a more
or less vague arrangement to expedite land
settlement. Once brought face to face with
this question politicians realise that here
Australia has on hand a most unholy mess.
Two hundred millions have been spent in
railroading a country with a population of
five millions, and every mile of railway built
has made it more expensive for people to get
land. The effect of the borrowing and the
building has been to put millions into pri-
vate pockets, to put up the price of land
against the people and reduce the State to
the madness first of borrowing money to
inflate land values and then borrowing
again to rescue land for public purposes or
for occupation by another settler. The evil
of this lunatic policy can be glaringly seen
now when various agitated Ministries assert
that soldiers cannot be provided with land
for settlement unless more millions are
spent on railways—and this with existing
railways running through unpeopled wilder-
nesses, and scores of thousands of holdings
yielding a mere minimum of production!
Of course this paper has nothing to say
against Australian soldiers being given
opportunity to settle on land. The idea is a
good one. The more settlement there is the
better. But we must have a settlement
that does not put added millions into pri-
vate pockets—a scheme that does not merely
fling the settler into a bog of debt.
* * * *
To provide a source from which advances
may be made to soldier settlers is one of the
main purposes of the Commonwealth
Repatriation Fund, and The Bulletin
hopes to see this fund liberally subscribed
to. So far as it is applied to men who take
up selections on lands served by existing
railways, the investment will be a sound
one. But if recourse is to be had by the
States to a system of borrowing for the pur-
pose of acquiring land, then the consequences
will be the enrichment of Fat, the impover-
ishment of the settlers, and the perpetuation
of a practice which has proved a gross fraud
on the community wherever it lias been
tried. Treasurer I liens raised his voice at
the Melbourne Conference in warning
against land purchase for settlement; and.
following the same line, it is to be hoped
that he will protest against our soldiers
being made an excuse for dragging the
Australian people further into debt and
sale-tracking the questions that are vital to
the successful occupation of the land of this
country. Even if Governments are afraid
to make the speculative landholder loosen
his clutches, that is no reason why either
the Australian public or the Australian
soldier should be fleeced again. ‘•Repatria-
tion” is a word that rolls easily off the
tongue. “Providing farms for soldiers ’ is
a phrase that sounds well. Both may cover
patriotic intention and genuine solicitude
for the men who have fought Australia s
battles. But behind word and phrase the
political shirker can find cover for a lot of ad-
ditional shirk. Were the nation’s tariff tackled
with a resolute desire to make it what it
should long ago have been, there would be
employment for many more men than are
going'to return to Australia from Flanders
and Egypt. There would then be an oppor-
tunity also for the investment of many
millions, and any superfluous millions that
might be still lying about could possibly be
used in bringing a place called the Northern
Territory closer to Australia. There is
room for a lot of people up there without
buying someone else out.
WHY NOT EXTEND THE CENSOR-
SHIP?
Among the peculiar weaknesses that
modern progress has evolved is the prying
curiosity which desires to peep into all
corners and be informed upon all facts. No
word can be said against the desire for
higher and nobler knowledge. The study of
Plato —as Oxford University lias proved, in
defiance of other seats of learning—is a valu-
able addition to the soul’s outfit, while too
much stress cannot be laid upon the import-
ance of a thorough acquaintance with the
Greek and Latin classics and the higher
branches of mathematics. The desire to
know, where the knowledge applies to things
academic and outside the practical sphere,
is to be highly commended; but no con-
demnation can be sufficiently strong for the
extension of the spirit of petty curiosity to
the realm of affairs. The gossip of the local
ale-house and the tittering tale-bearing over
the back fence have in our crowded com-
munities developed into the news and
comment of the newspapers; and only by
the severest restrictions on the part _ of
those in authority can the inquisitive spirit
of the community be now 7 kept in check. The
press censorship during the present war has
done good w 7 ork, but it requires to be con-
siderably extended before the value of its
efforts for the public good can be appreci-
ated to the full. With certainty it may be
asserted that never before in the course of
history has the moral and ethical value of
a blue pencil been so effectively shown: but,
with the results already achieved to point
the way, there seems no reason why green,
red and even yellow pencils should not also
be employed in the great cause of national
self-restraint and that faith in authority
which is the true basis of all docile and
unperturbed citizenship.
Already, under the wise control exercised
over printed matter, certain most im-
portant actions of Federal Ministers may
ba withheld from criticism. This, of
course, is as it should be. With a great war
on hand and a mighty continent to control, it
is above all things necessary that the men
of outstanding genius and towering intellect
who have charge of Commonwealth a H airs
at home and abroad should be allowed to live
and w r ork in an atmosphere of perfect calm,
far removed from the babble of the voices
of the crowd. To harass them with com-
ment, criticism, condemnation or advice is
to lessen their peace of mind and restrict
the sweep of their action; and the censor-
ship which guards them from the cold blast
of outer thought and feeling must be counted
among our national blessings. But the con-
stitution of our Commonwealth is such that
blessings of the kind cannot be confined to
Ministers in the Federal sphere without rais-
ing the question of State rights. Why should
the censorship protect one class of Ministers
only, while another that would derive equal
benefit from such protection is subject to
even severer criticism from the press ?
Under the guardianship of a blue pencil of
sufficient magnitude State Ministers could
really get busy and show 7 what they are
capable of. There is no telling to what splen-
did heights of achievement a Minister like
Arthur Griffith might soar if all facts,
suggestions and comment with regard to his
administrative acts were rigidly suppressed
in all newspapers. Without such protection,
it must be admitted, a statesman’s hands are
tied. To any right-thinking person it must
be abundantly clear that an extension of the
censorship to matters relating to State Min-
istries is among the great needs of the hour
and should have a foremost place on any
programme of reform.
* * * *
Take as merely one example the question
of borrowing. How hampered must Wil-
liam Holman often feel himself by the com-
ments of the newspapers and of the poli-
ticians whom the newspapers report? Yet
Holman says that borrowing is essen-
tial for the development of the country.
He declares it a national necessity. More-
over, it is the policy of the country: a Gov-
ernment which practised it appealed to the
people.and w 7 ns elected triumphantly. How
much easier it would be to carry out the
thing that is necessary for the development
of the country if unpatriotic people who dis-
approve of borrowing were silenced! Bor-
rowing being the national policy, and
declared by the people to be necessary for the
country’s salvation, it seems to us obvious
that the state Government might properly
ask that no statement should be made which
would prejudice the raising of one single
bean. This would put an end to all the
tittle-tattle w 7 e hear from time to time about
tiie waste of money on political works, and
any other unpatriotic talk that might lead
some person with a hundred pounds to put
it in the bank instead of passing it over to
the Treasurer of the State.
But to stop the development of the censor-
ship when it has merely afforded sanctuary
to Federal and State Ministries would be
a decided mistake. In the cause of national
organisation we should go much farther, lhe
affairs of our municipal councils are in
some respects of equal importance to those
of the States; «nd the time has come when
the interference of public curiosity should
be eliminated with regard to them. hhe
tierce light which beats about the seat or
W. A. Holm ax or his deputy beats with
equal brilliancy around the chair of R- *- ■
Meagher in his job as head of the city ot
Sydney, and narrows and restricts his efforts
in a way that largely prevents him from
coming to liis own. Again, the various
Boards, Trusts and other official organisa-
tions are too freely subjected to the petty
tyranny of the public eye, and might wen
be safeguarded from such inquisition, lhe
police, too, who labor under many disac -
vantages, might have a little consideration
shown them. The publication of stories ot
crime and subsequent Court proceedings
leads to much fatigue and the loss of a gien
deal of leisure. Were these things properly
blue-pencilled from all printed matter little
light and brightness might be imported into
the notoriously unhappy lot of the worth}
and too-often unappreciated guardians ot the
law. Further, it must be noted that all
extensions of the censorship are in the nature
ot' davlight-saving reform. The amount ot
time wasted by the general public m repeat-
ing the news concerning official actions pub-
lished in the newspapers and making suitable
comment upon them is dead loss to the
community. No war-time economy .could
be greater than that which might be effected
by turning off these torrents of speech and
setting the people free to employ their s iaie
time in growing cabbages in their back
yards, to the great benefit_ of the national
health as w r ell as of the national pocket.

The Relation of Doves to Sausages.
It is at least possible that Prussian mili-
tarism and the Kaiser may get the knock-out
blow from somebody not in the least con-
nected with the huge Allied forces that are
now hammering at the German hues ol
defence. The stories of hunger riots in Ber-
lin and other cities have come through with
some regularity, and the cumulative evi-
dence is to the effect that llans is seri-
ously pining for his sausage and very angry
indeed with the people who threw it awaj
on a. pretence of finding him a place in the
sun. llans was persuaded that his bosses
knew their job, and that they really could
make things more glorious for him by the
use of explosives. Now he knows better, or
if he doesn’t know he is rapidly learning, lie
sees nothing but hunger ahead, with more
hunger to follow, and plenty of graves in
between: and the chances are that, as tie
cables say, he is fighting for a loat m
Leipzig with quite as much enthusiasm as
his brother fights for a yard of French trench
near Verdun. One interesting point about
his change of view is the bearing it may have
on the peace proposals when the war-teast
reaches the course where the dove comes in
with olive garnishings. To dictate terms
to a broken Kaiser is one matter; to demand
The Englishman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Back where the border of Time recedes
From the chronicled years oi: inch.
My coracle stirred the grating i’eeds
And the slime of the shoreless ten ;
Wild, in a wild thing’s pelt, I eked
Harsh living from wood and Sea *
The wicker crackled, the captives shrieked,
The Druid chanted, the god-stone reeked,
And the hut-folk fled from me.
There, ere our tally of time began
I was an Englishman!
West, where my short-necked spearmen
strode, ,
The men of the Severn railed;
North on a ribbon of Roman road
My glittering legions trailed.
Grandeur of speech and the fighting lust.
These were the gifts I gave—
Order from chaos and sweat and dust.
Late, when a gibbering Tyneman s thrust
Under my gorget drave, .
I laughed as I died! Did my heart divine
That the English blood was mine.'
After, I drove my Frisian keel
On the dunes of the Wessex shore,
Titled my fief with a pen of steel,
Mated and fought and bore
Till, when the Dragon at Stamford shone,
And the spear ripped Tostig’s side.
I spurred for the south with Godwinsso.n,
And, charging the Norman gonfalon,
At Harold’s feet I died!
England, I gave to you bone and tliew —
I who was English too!
Harrying up to the sorry Forth,
Foraying down to the sea,
Scored I the furrows from south to north,
With the harrow of Normandy.
Squire was I of the old Romance,
Heavy of hand and heel,
Yet with the fire of coastward France
Edged I the blundering Saxon lance,
The slotlilier Saxon steel.
This you had missed had I passed you by,
For I am English, I!
So have I drawn to me (whence have I
not?)
Men on the swan-path since—-
Norsemen and Fleming, Jew and Scot,
Burgher, beggar and prince.
Huguenot zealot and broken Russ —-
The sixteen winds and the sea
(As once to the city of Romulus)
Bore them to weld and to wed, and thus
Fashion my nationry ;
Never a nook of the world but owns
Englishmen living or English bones.
Paladin oft-times, plunderer oft.
Henchman of pride and sloth.
Bullying, god-like, stern and soft,
Liegeman and laggard both ;
Nations shall bourgeon, aiftl ripe, and rot,
Yet shall the peoples know
As in the gaunt years unforgot,
The call of my race-line polyglot —
Slow waking, to ire slow,
The splintering shock of my strength at
spate.
Merciless, chivalrous, all-but-late!

The Advantages of Doing Things as
One People.
Tue opening of 14 Houses of Parliament
each year is a solemn circumstance, and will
apparently endure for a long time yet. The
present system is. however, untidy ; and with
a view to getting a start with the business
of co-ordination, this paper suggests that a
Conference of Premiers should be held imme-
diately after Hughes returns, in order to
systematise the opening of the multitudinous
Chambers. The advantages of having the cere-
monial performed simultaneously throughout
the Commonwealth are obvious. It would be
nationally impressive. It would be convenient
to the Cocked Hats. It might even suggest
Empire. It could be made the occasion of a
public holiday and great social rejoicing.
The public would certainly get a better hang
of the beautiful machine they support. If
The Day were fixed the rest would be easy.
Early in the morning the public could be
aroused by the roar of cannon. Getting an
early start they could rush towards the
Houses of Parliament. Then the arrival of
the local Cocked Hat, surrounded by an
armed guard and pursued by motor-cars,
could be heralded by more cannon and loud
applause, and the entry of the official party
could be made with appropriate dignity. The
Speech could be standardised, and throughout
the length and breadth of the land the public
would know that the same thing had been
done and said at seven different places—that
Black Rods had whistled several hundred
persons together; that Cocked Hats, sur-
rounded by millinery, had in firm, clear
voices taken pieces of paper out of their
pockets and proceeded to make speeches
which were not speeches but recitations. If
the recitations were to this effect they would
about meet the bill:—
Honorable Gentlemen of tiie Legislative Council
and Gentlemen of the Legislative Assembly:
(1) It is with great pleasure that I summon you
to make a pretence of doing something.
(2) The war finished several years ago, and it is
exceedingly gratifying to my advisers to be able to
announce that it is not going on.
(3) Rain fell the day before yesterday, and the
result should be to make the grass grow.
(4) The Government has continued to inquire into
various matters requiring attention.
Gentlemen of the Legislative Council :
(5) You will be pleased to learn that you will be
asked to accept allowances at the rate of £lOOO per
head per annvgn.
(6) My Government has decided to aslc your
acceptance of life railway passes for all members of
our families and relatives by marriage.
Honorable Gentlemen of the Legislative Council
and Gentlemen of tiie Legislative Assembly:
(7) You will be asked to consider Bills dealing
with important proposals relating to
(a) Getting more cash.
(i>) Spending more cash.
(c) Promising more cash.
(d) Developing our great national resources.
(8) I invite your earnest consideration of the
matters proposed as the subjects of your delibera-
tions, and pray that under the guidance of Divine
Providence your labors may conduce to -the well-
being and prosperity of the party in power.
This having been worked off, the decorative
effects could clatter away on fiery chargers
and. the bills be circulated for public infor-
mation, while 14 Speakers proceeded to adorn
themselves with 14 wigs, and 14 Maces were
laboriously borne forth, and the concentrated
intellect of the nation applied itself to the
consideiation of how host to lay more bird-
lime for Cohen.
More Howls for Shutting;’ Things Up.
()N the day last week that N.S.W. Bung
got final notice to turn off the beer supply at
G o clock a deputation rushed a member of
the Ministry with urgent appeals to “stop
prize fights.” The appeals were made on
what the deputationists called “moral and
national grounds,” these being (1) that
boxmg contests at the Stadium are brutal:
(_) that the attendance of people thereat
wounds the susceptibilities of individuals
with friends and relatives at the Front;
(b) that instead of looking on at these con-
tests men should join the army; (4) that
the imported png is an undesirable immi-
grant; (o) that while war rages the com-
munity ought to bo made to recognise its
obligations; and (0) that the whole thing
is a scandal. The Bulletin is not going to
the Stadium is a centre of
that from it there is wafted through
the community a fine ethical influence. Nor
should it be necessary at this stage for
this paper to say that it regards the non-
performance of military duty by pugs and
by “fans” as a gross scandal—none surely
has said so more plainly. But it takes
the same iiew of the draper and the poet
and the chauffeur and the actor and the
lawyer. The trouble is not that the Stadium
is open and that boxing matches take place,
but that there is no general definition of
social responsibility. If there was compul-
sory military service where would the objec-
tion to the glove-fight be? To call it brutal
is absurd. A conflict between Darcy and
McGooriv. for instance, is a long way less
brutal than a Rugby scrum. And there is
the further circumstance that a large pro-
portion of the people are not fit for military
service. Are their liberties to see an ath-
letic display to be restricted because a
few individuals are not in the army’ Is
the returned soldier to be prevented from
seeing a glove-fight because Ginger Mick
or Exquisite Percy didn’t get into khaki?
The sober truth is that the war is being
put forward as an excuse for censorious
interference with every citizen of the Com-
monwealth and for making a penal offence
of enjoyment of any kind except such as
may be approved by deacons and church-
wardens.
Whisky by Measure.
Ladies and Gentlemen, Brother Fax will
now address you :
One of Melbourne’s licensed victuallers lately re-
belled against the local brewery combine. The rebel
had offended the powers that beer by refusing to
raise his price for bottled spirits, imported by, him-
self, up to “the trade’s” standard. He was content
with a smaller- profit than other retailers exacted
from their victims; and the Combine, being the
bloated capitalist at the back of many such retailers,
took measures to reduce the rebel to submission.
He was denied further supplies of Australian beer,
whereupon he retaliated by retailing imported spirits
and ale and stout in fourpenny doses, which was an
awful innovation on the trade’s established rule.
Thus he gave away the fact that the public has been
grossly overcharged by the trade in general since
war prices came into force. When, in December,
1914, the duty on spirits was raised, the wholesale
price for “brands” was raised as a matter of business
necessity. It went up mostly 15s. a case. Hotel-
keepers thus had to pay another 15d. per bottle. To
preserve their large margin of profit they would have
needed only to ask 7d. a glass for the whisky and
soda they had previously sold at 6d., seeing that each
bottle is reckoned to yield 15 drinks at the counter.
Many hotelkeepers estimate a bottle at 10 drinks, the
average customer, especially in the morning, being a
very moderate nipper, who nips only to oblige his
friend or keep a clients in countenance. Nevertheless
the Melbourne retailers conspired to raise the charge
for “brands” to 9d. They stuck 3d. on the drink
where Id. would have sufficed. They did as the
grocers, drapers and other sorts of shopkeepers have
been doing. They demanded an exorbitant profit on
the extra amount they paid for the goods.
“Derrink” being, in a wowser way of speaking, an
accursed thing which everybody would be better
without, Government regulation of whisky prices is
not to be expected. Nobody can plead that the wel-
fare of the community demands a reduction in the
cost of the liquor that makoth glad the heart of
man by stealing away his brains. Besides, the
drinkers themselves are primarily responsible for the
imposition which is now practised upon them. They
have accepted the silly old American custom of help-
ing themselves from the bottle, which is expected to
average 15 drinks to the vendor. But the rebel who
lately introduced fourpenny drinks to notice doled
them out by measure, in English fashion, and this is
what should be the rule everywhere. A reasonable
citizen wants no more of a nip than the publican
can afford to sell him for 4d. What the boozer wants
is another matter. Let him pay extra for a larger
dose, even as a woman in a 3d. icecream shop has the
option of paying 6d. for a double quantity.
Another absurdity in the hotel trade was the cus-
tom of giving the customer a right to demand
aerated water with his whisky, as though it cost no
more than water from the tap. For the continuation
of this custom the Trades Hall was accountable.
When the hotelkeepers last year proposed to make a
fixed distinction in price between whisky straight
and whisky with soda, the Trades Hall protested that
it would be injurious to the aerated-water trade, and
so the old absurdity was retained.
It is the moderate drinker, with a preference for
plain water with his spirit, who has all these years
been paying for the “pass-the-bottle” and “extra
sodas free” anomalies. He is the profit where the
immoderate customer is a loss to the publican. He
suffers in his pocket in order that the greedy boozer
may not be charged according to a fair rule. It is
time for the reasonable citizen to revolt. If he wants
fourpen’orth of his fancy brand of whisky he should
be able to get it—by measure. A concerted demand for
the reform would bring the supply. And the cor-
morant publicans and soakers, together with the
autocratic brewery combine, would be euchred.
I.W.W.
Dear Bulletin, —Of course, tlie I.W.W.
people are quite mad. Most of them know
they are. The newspapers can be depended
upon not to let them forget it. But the daily
yell at these people for being mad is not going
to make them sane. Their particular form of
madness is catching, and you can take it
from one who has no party gods to propitiate
that the disease is spreading. And what is
more, it is going to spread. But when we
have yelled at 1.W.W.-ism and the S. M.
Herald recovers from its next paroxysm,
and W. Hughes has assailed the I.W.W.
with fiery jaw, I want to ask you as a man
and a brother, what we propose to do about
it. Suggestions for putting the I.W.W. in
gaol and suppressing its spruikers and other-
wise jumping on it are clearly inadequate.
They merely add fuel to.the fire. There is
the possibility of putting the owners of
I.W.W. principles on the noxious-weed list,
but unless W. Hughes and the Argus care
to propose that, it looks as if the casting out
of devils will not be complete. Meanwhile it
occurs to me that there is a tremendous
volume of yell being poured into the wrong
ear. 1.W.W.-ism is the doctrine of the dis-
ordered digestion, the unstable mentality, the
wild eye and the delirious phrase. It is the
doctrine of despair—nothing else. And the
reason why it has got foothold—and a
stronger one than the newspapers care to
admit—is that Labor is despairing, that the
rank and file have something more than a
suspicion that they are being fooled. Are
they wrong or right in that? Let it be
granted that 1.W.W.-ism leads to the Devil.
Well, where does ScADDAN-ism lead and 110 -
MAN-ism? What are Hughes and Tudor
and Pearce offering? Australian men and
women have been very patient. They have
been very trustful. But they are growing
impatient and mistrustful now and intolerant
of carrots. The spread of I.W.W. theories is
the result of the failure of the men who
called themselves the leaders of Labor and
are now wandering about with chloroform
bottles. Is it conceivable that the Australian
democracy can be argued or bludgeoned into
having their political choice limited to two
awful political alternatives—the Liberalism
of Joe Cook and Co. on one side and a loan-
mongering travesty of Labor’s ideals on the
other? To me the idea is fantastic.
The truth is that our party system has hope-
lessly broken down, that the people are being
fleeced by politicians, and that the mental
bankruptcy of the official Lib. and Lab. and
his camp-follower is pitiable. Everyone of
them is quaking in his shoes. It is not the
fanatic on the soap-box who is making con-
verts to the I.WAV. The base betrayal of
principle by the other fellow is making them
by the hundred. And that brings np the old
question again—-well, what about it? Is no
one going to offer the toiling mob a fair share
of the earth and the fruits thereof, or must
he. for ever take £l, which K Ninas accuses Of
• being a disguise for 14s. 6d., and thank
Cohen he is still alive?— Wm. Tracker.

The Struggle Against a Secret Distri-
bution of Disease.
Even if it be admitted that there is room
for difference of opinion in these things,
what force is there in the argument ad-
vanced by N.S.W. Chief Secretary George
Black against making venereal disease com-
pulsorily notifiable? The indisputable facts
are that the disease is rampant; that it is
the cause of widespread misery, inefficiency
and human decay; that it is easily distri-
buted. That is stating the case in ihe very
mildest terms. And Black's objection to
making this disease notifiable in the same
way as smallpox: and plague and tubercu-
losis and other contagious and infectious
horrors is that the result would be to
“drive people suffering from the disease to
quack doctors and chemists.” If this' is
his only objection the Minister has not a
leg to stand on. Notification of leprosy
and diphtheria and scarlet fever does not
drive people to quacks. Why should it be
supposed that there would be an exception
in the case of venereal affliction? And can-
not quacks be rooted out of their holes and
corners? The fact is that ignorance and
fear and the lunatic conspiracy of hush
under which the spread of this disease has
been cloaked have more than anything else
been the cause of making sufferers look to
the impostor for relief. The character of
the disease has not been understood by
men and women; its frightfully insidious
nature has not been realised. The news-
papers and the pulpits and the politicians
have all conspired to impress the public
mind with the tragic idea that syphilis and
gonorrhoea must not be even spoken of —
that they are visitations from the Most
High, the consequences of Sin, and that
anyone suffering therefrom had about him
something that should be kept secret be-
tween him and his Maker. Therefore
the rogue with the pill and the ointment
has flourished exceedingly. Even the
medical-schools’ course of instruction in the
treatment of venereal disease was until
quite recent years a perfunctory and more-
or-less unpleasantly jocular circumstance.
Unless bubonic plague was a notifiable
disease, how could the thing be grappled
with? And how can any other communi-
cable disease be adequately fought? If
quacks have to be fought as well, good
enough ! But in this case what has to be
wrestled with most strenuously is ignorance,
and notification is the best of all methods
of rousing the public to recognise that there
•is a ravening monster at large. Under a
sane system of warfare against venereal
affection the State would keep track of the
system of treatment pursued in the case of
individuals. How could this be done with-
out notification?

Sydney’s Underground Railway.
llolman says the construction of the Sydney
city railway isn’t postponed—only put off
owing to the difficulty of getting material in
war-time.. The difference is rather too fine
for the ordinary intelligence, but the state-
ment itself seems to call for a question or
two. Does Holman imply that the material
for this railway must be imported as well
as the cash or credit to construct it? Is
there any material in a railway of this sort
that cannot be obtained in Australia?
Timber, bricks, metal and everything else
required for making ordinary holes in the
earth through which trains can run would
appear to be obtainable in the Common-
wealth ; but possibly there may be a stick
of sealing-wax or a blotting-pad required
for the office before things can begin to
move. Anyhow, seeing that there is such a
delay and that whether “postponed” or “put
off” the underground railway isn’t going to
be made at once, Holman might give a
little thought to the alternative proposal of
decentralisation by the construction of
much less expensive railways to some of our
closed ports, for which the material is
certainly all to be obtained in Australia-
even if it is war time. With two or three
other trade outlets created, Sydney could
get along very decently for some time to
come with the existing traffic arrangements,
and the whole. State would benefit tremen-
dously. Of course, the congestionists, who
would like to build the city up 20 storeys
high all over, want as much railway as they
can get below their feet; but these people
aren’t exactly profitable to the community
and have had a- pretty good innings. To
set them gnashing their teeth would be a
fine' stroke of policy, and every gnash would
be a testimonial to Holman’s integrity of ’
purpose. But there is little hope that any
such exhibition will be seen; for the con-
gestionist’s smile is set, and he trusts
llolman to gild his Sydney possessions bv
piling all the people and trade he can into
the space between Darling Harbor and
AVoolloomooloo Bay. Meanwhile, with
wheat rotting and city storage limited, the
bloat policy becomes a national calamity.
Peace at Any Price.
I" 1 ’ is about time somebody started a new
Peace Society with a gun stamped on its
official notepaper. The thing that now
raves its faith in brotherly kindness in spite
°f the horrors of Belgium, Servia and the
baby-killing raids on the British coast is too
insane a proposition to be allowed the copy-
right of a very good and satisfactory name.
\Wien the old gag “Peace at any price” was
being hurled against Gladstone in the
happy days when a Phoenix Park murder
was the biggest enemy offensive in sight,
some heckled statesman is supposed do have
replied. “Yes, _ peace at any price, because
the highest price ever paid for peace is less
than the lowest price paid for war.” In a
general sort of way that statement is true •
but the real trouble is that the Peace Society
folk behind their disastrous umbrellas are
never willing to pay the price of peace v The
real price of peace is the cost of keeping
strong enough in a military sense to make
the other fellow afraid to tackle you. In
the actual .event of war, as at present, the
price of peace is the cost of sufficient armies
and munitions to thrash the enemy to a
finish. Looking at the matter with as little
prejudice as possible, it seems as if the
utmost price that Britain could have paid
in the past in order to be equal to any call
to arms must, even in the aggregate, have
been less than the cost of this war; and the
fact that millions of lives would have been
thus saved from death or misery must
weigh down the balance very heavily in
favor of planking down the necessary cash
before the crisis arrives. By a fluke—which
fluke is represented by the stubborn valor
of Frenchmen, Belgians and Servians—Bri-
tain has been allowed enough time to raise
armies which can give an account of them-
selves before the finish ; but the thing was
an immense gamble, and gambling is sup-
posed to be abhorrent to the souls of Peace
Society people. War preparation is simply
decent insurance against the madness of
somebody else with a gun. The idea of a
lot of people in Australia, that we are quite
safe, as long as Britain can muddle
through, must be given up forever. When
the present trouble is over—and as
much before that date as possible—the
Commonwealth must face this matter of
insurance of her own national ideals and
the lives of her own people in grim serious-
ness. Because there is no immediate danger
threatening, the organisation of that insur-
ance can be carried out with some thorough-
ness. Had England so organised about the
time when W. T. Stead and Alexander
111. and the Hague Conference were mixed
up in a wild whirl of doves’ leaves, olive
feathers and Christmas carols, there would
be no war going on now. If there had been
a Avar in 1914 it would have stopped Short
with a heavy jolt somewhere about Liege or
Namur.
“X.Y.”: The production of synthetic dyes
is one of .the most intricate in the whole
range of industrial chemistry. The chemist
has not only to produce a given color or
shade, but he must produce it of a standard
tinctorial capacity. This is a most difficult
matter, as different batches of the same
stock of coal-tar derivative, put through
exactly the same process, with similar re-
agents, will vary in tinctorial power
between very wide limits. As textile manu-
facturers demanded that dyes of the same
name should be uniform in color and tinc-
torial capacity, the German chemists stan-
dardised them. The manufacturer, there-
fore, had only to weigh out the same quan-
tity of dye to obtain a uniform result in
shade and dyeing power. It was variability
in the products, as regards coal-tar colors,
which killed the English trade. The Eng-
lish workmen had not the capacity for taking
pains into which the German chemical indus-
trial operative had been trained, and the result
was that the manufacturers bought the stan-
dard German dyes even at a higher price. It
is sad and annoying, but it is true, and wo
will have to admit the fault and remedy
it if we are to be independent of llans
after the war. In peace or war the only
way to beat the enemy is to do better than
him, and you can’t depend on even your
mother to buy your inferior dye for
ever. She may do it for a little while out
of natural love and affection; but her
patience is going to give out some time.
Therefore—Go thou and do better, or at
least as well as the enemy.

“Barcoo” : That most deplorable country,
the Northern Territory, has once more
laughed at its traducers. On June 8, at tin'
London sales, wool from Avon Downs, Barkly
Tableland (N.T.), brought up to 45d.
per lb. for scoured fleece, and the whole clip
of 368 bales averaged 42d. During the last
20 years this wool has more than once when
sold in Australia either topped the sales oi-
lmen thereabouts, even when treated on the
station in a primitive way. Avon Downs was
taken up by Tom Guthrie, of Geelong
(lie.), 30-odd years ago. His initial mis-
take was that he stocked with sheep of the
Tasmanian type, and they did not acclima-
tise for a long period. The Western N.SAV.
type are the sheep for the Territory. The
Avon Downs merino, however, has now
'veil accommodated itself to its conditions.
On several occasions wethers have walked
into Hergott Springs, between 600 and 700
miles, taken the train to pastures near Adel-
aide, and after three or four weeks’ spell
have topped the markets. The Tableland,
where these good beasts grow, is only a small
portion of identical country which runs right
through to Kimberley (W.A.), and it has
been estimated to have a carrying eapacitv
of 10,000.000 merinos. This part’s time wiil
come, and its settlement is going to start
inland first, sheep displacing cattle, as they
have in Central and Western Queensland,
where two districts that 40-odd years ago
, WS cattle-run now depasture
9,000,000 of the heaviest-cutters and highest-
priced wool-growers i n Australia.

Billjim is right up against it again, and
the last Tiie Buixetin heard of him he
was locked in a desperate struggle with the
Ilun for possession of a village on the
Allies’ line of advance. It is only the little
village of Pozieres that no Australian had
ever heard of or wanted to hear of until
the other day. But it is a name that will
henceforth be burned into the memory, for
on this French hillock the - troops of
the Commonwealth have had their first big
collision with the German, and it is there
that many an Australian soldier-son and
brother will find a grave. What the losses
among our lads have been cannot yet be
known. But we must steel our hearts for
sad news. It is no use thinking that there
can be participation in such terrific fighting
without heavy suffering. Each day makes
it clearer that the job of rooting the enemy
out of the places where he has fortified
himself is an undertaking of stupendous
difficulty; and whether the Allies are going
to speedily succeed in the job they started
on three weeks ago is still hanging in the
balance. What is certain already is that
they are now making the German fight as
he has never had to fight before, and
bleed as he never bled before. But
it is certain, too, that to achieve a decisive
victory the Allies have a lot yet to over-
come ; and anything short of decisive-
ness will not have been worth the loss of
life involved. Meanwhile the one com-
forting feature about the summer campaign-
ing is that on both Fronts the initiative
has passed from the German to his oppo-
nents, and there is no sign of the position
being reversed. The great question of the
immediate present is: How long can the
Allies keep the Western push going? If
they can maintain the shove as long and
intensely as the Russian is keeping his
shoulder behind the wheel, the Kaiser will
be a much worried person when the autumn
leaves commence to fall. The persistence
of the effort is, of course, mainly a question
of reserves. And the question of reserves
is mainly a matter of the individual’s
answer to the nation’s call for help.

From a man in the middle of it; —
The Federal Government has “requisitioned” all
rabbit-skins, but at prices that will administer a
severe shock to the business. ffest skins have le-
cently brought 2s. 3d. a lb. at auction, and even then
it is only in places where it is impossible to sell
carcases that skin-getting- is profitable enough to
follow. The Government top price of Is. Bd. is so
much less that many skin-trappers must throw up
the game. There is much more work about trapping
for skins than for carcases, and with the increase in
the number of freezing works in the country there is
a big incentive to get in touch with a factory. Con-
sequently the Government prices would seem almost
certainly calculated to very largely decrease the
supply of rabbit-skins sent in by trappers, and make
the shortage more acute. Probably the Government
will eventually have to turn its attention to the fur
exported on carcases, as it is thus that the bulk of
it leaves the country. Incidentally, skin-trapping
keeps rabbits in check in districts where trapping
for carcases is impossible.
George Meudell’s beautiful yarn from
’Frisco: —■
On March 8 a party of jolly Germans met in a
cafe here to sing songs and toast the success of the
Teuton armies. When everybody was very fu’, the
chairman, one Sigismund Vinterfeld, signed a post-
card on behalf of those present, addressed, in Ger-
man, to the “Crown Prince, Verdun, France,” read-
ing, “Go ahead, Fritz, we are all with you.” Like
the cat of lyrical fame, the postcard came back,
stamped Verdun, May 4, and across it in French,
“Not yet arrived at Verdun.”
In connection with the Federal Govern-
ment’s scheme for a picnic to America, the
present idea is that each of the dozen
revellers shall get £2 2s. per day “expenses,”
plus steamer and railway fares, while the
secretary and the journalist will get £8 per
week each, with exes, on the same scale.
What the Commission is to find out no one
seems to know. For instance, Tasmania is to
send two representatives to inquire into
paper making. Yet only the other day the
Tasmanian Government imported a Yankee
expert at a cost of about £lOOO to come and
report on the Speck’s chances in paper-
making, and was told she had none. As she
certainly doesn’t make any paper and never
has made any. it isn’t likely that she has
anybody who can study paper-making with
effect, anyway.

Last week at Melbourne Princess’s a new
and less sophisticated contingent was added
to the patrons of grand opera. The folk
who always “wait for ‘Faust’ ” came along
for the first time this season to hear the oid
familiar airs and rejoice in the fact that
one language is much the same as another
when you know the narrative by heart.
The Gonsalez Co. makes a sufficiently good

Cacialli (the Devil) is the least effective of
the principals, his basso being rather light
f. v full Satanic expression and his style ot
acting non-theatrical. Even in his descent
to Hell, with red fire to the left of
him and a crude vision of the kingdom of
Heaven stretched the full width of the stage
behind him —even then Cacialli failed to
thrill this observer. His red hair was
flagrantly unreal and Ills deportment didn t
reek of sin nncl wickedness. In the It&licin
opera version of “Faust” the Devil returns
alone to the place where the red fire comes
from. His pupil is saved by the grace of
Margherita. And Capelli plays the name
part as though he deserved to escape. He
is never light-hearted in his rejuvenation,
but always seriously in love and earnestly
desirous of “going straight” as regards
morals. He sings the music of the first
scene with particular sweetness, and is ten-
derly reverential in his rendering of Salve
Dimora, where a piercing high note to finish
with is usually supplied by tenors of the
louder and shriller order. Miss V isoni is
an animated Margherita—a bit stagey in
her sad, emotional scenes —whose voice
fairly meets its heavy responsibilith s.
Filippini’s versatile baritone achieves a
great success in the thankful part of Valen-
tino. Miss Russ dresses Siebel in flaxen
ringlets, a tunic and tights—like the act-
resses of long ago used to dress Rosalind
in the Forest of Arden —and her mezzo s
not nearlv so true and tuneful as that of
Mrs. Piccolo, in the little part of Marta.
The lately strengthened male chorus does
very well' in “Faust,” though not so well
as in the latest revivals of “Rigoletto” and
other operas. The scenery is good; but the
stage management has lapsed utterly into
the old Italian habit of lining up the chorus
in semi-circular form.
* * * *
“Favorita,” presented on Saturday night,
is not so musically iuviting as “Lucia,” the
only other enduring example of Donizetti’s
art. The opera contains many sweet pas-
sages for the leading tenor and his Leonora,
but even the tenderest lyric has an undertone
of pious melancholy in keeping with the re-
ligious atmosphere of the subject. In
“Favorita” the composer celebrates the power
and influence ofjdie Church. The ex-novice,
Fernando, who weds Leonora and becomes a
king’s favorite, can only think of drowning
his sorrows in holy water, when the crushing
discovery of his wife’s past with the King
makes her society impossible. Fernando goes
back to the fold over the corpse of Ins peni-
tent spouse, with the same recognition ot
the Church's authority that compels his fes-
tive monarch to part with Leonora and
become a respectable husband once again.
Donizetti gives liis richest orchestral dress-
ing to the salving of Fernando’s wayward
soul, and puts into the mouth of that emo-
tional youth a lovely parting song full ot
dissipated rapture and vain regret. Ca-
pelli poured all the feeling of a broken heart
into every note of the item on Saturday, and
elsewhere sang with an Italian’s passionate
exultation in the picturesque possibilities of
a romantic role. But Capelli’s erotic yearn-
ing was mostly in his beautiful voice. llis
bearing towards Leonora was guarded to the
verge of coolness, and two or three conven-
tional attitudes of a love-sick tenor had to
suffice to express the fire that was burning
within him. True, Miss Russ, as Leonora,
was not very encouraging. She never em-
braced her young man with any ardor, whilst
her depressing vibrato merely gave a sort ot
corrugated intensity to her message of love.
On the physical side, however, she had some
endearing voung charms, which apparently so
affected the King (Belloni) that he also
developed a vibrato when he had to glorify
the lady in song. Still he warbled with genu-
ine feeling, and in other ways made a most
impressive royalty. Cacialli’s useful bass
kept the whiskered monk Balthasar well in
the picture; while Mrs. Belloni in the minor
part of Inez revealed a small soprano seem-
ingly suffering from nervous prostration.
The" male chorus, cowled and otherwise, was
very agreeable, and the feminine part of the
support was only a little awkward in stage
behavior.
