Sydney James's Strollers (Tarts’ manage-
ment) had a rousing reception at the Palace
on Saturday night on their reappearance
after a long tour. There is practically no
change in the co. since it was in Sydney
last, and such minor alterations as there
have been neither strengthen nor weaken
the entertainment. So all that The
Bulletin formerly said about the show
holds good. Sydney James’s humor sparkled
particularly on Saturday, and his ventrilo-
quial turn with “Billy” was a gay and glad-
some item. Madeline Rossiter (James s
strongest support), G. W. Desmond, Gwen
Lewis, George Graystone (he used to earn
money for the J.C.W. firm), Connie Miln,
Arthur Frost and Cyril Northcote helped
to cheer up the congregation.
Numerous motor-cars without and a
mighty crowd within the walls of Melbourne
King’s last Saturday indicated that “Beg
o' My Heart” had found popularity waiting
for it. Expectation was mostly satisfied
yet partly disappointed by “Peg.” The
simple plot is suggestive of Little
Mary,” but it lacks the satire of Barries
clever comedy, and it is based on obvious
absurdities. Fofc instance, Christopher
Brent, the comparative villain of the piece,
is supposed to have come to a complete
understanding with the daughter of the
house of Cnichester. They are already
almost ripe for an elopement when the cur-
tain rises. Yet the oily Hebrew in the
opening scene talks of his distaste for his
wife and expresses his yearning for a “kin-
dred spirit” as though he were pressing his
illicit suit for the first time. And the hard-
as-nails young woman, whose voice would
strike cold to any bad man’s soul, argues the
point with Christopher as though it were
new to her. The situation and the char-
acters are equally unreal. For the rest of
the plot there is a mild-spoken young man
who disguises himself under the name of
Jerry, whilst dodging in and out of the
Chichester domicile as casually _as
Christopher aforesaid, until he reveals him-
self as Peg’s legally-appointed guardian;
also there is an alleged bank smash which
turns out to be a mere fracture, and a dog
fight behind the scenes, and a thwarted
elopement, and Peg.
* * * *
Peg is not so much a part of the plot as
the whole of the show. She is the Maggie
Moore of 40 years back, licensed to be just
as free and easy as she pleases. Peg hap-
pens into the dull burlesque of a high-toned
English domestic circle and proceeds to cast
Hibernian repartee at the icebergian women-
kind and the contemptible men. Her man-
ners range from the effusively winsome to
the intensely irritating. The greatest fool-
ishness of the Chichester women is in treat-
ing her as a dire calamity instead of a
trying joke, and the redeeming circum-
stance in the stupidity of Alaric, the family
idiot, is his explosion of delight when he
finds that Peg would rather be dead than
married to him. Her laugh and her pet
dog and her belief in her beloved father
would be terrible things for any perfidious
Saxon to have to live with. She entertains
her lover with bits of anti-English declama-
tion borrowed from the speeches of her
father, who has been a wandering Sinn Fein
agitator. Finally she figures as guardian
angel to icicle Ethel, and then, having taxed
two other men with the crime of offering
to marry her for her fortune, sfie proposes
to marry the disingenuous and almost in-
audible young man who had been carefully
concealing his knowledge of her wealth.
Practically he was the heiress-trapper in
disguise. But the plot of the little play
matters nothing to its chance of success.
Sara Allgood is not quite all that Maggie
Moore might have been in the name part in
olden days, nevertheless she is a magnetic
Peg, with a mellow Irish voice and a happy,
broguish way of playing the little divil.
Other members of the co. seem to have been
picked in England to suit their respective
types of character, but in no case is
type forcibly presented. A stage setting
which serves for the three acts is admirably
solid and tasteful,
“Our Miss Gibbs,” revived for oue brief
week of musical bargains, is now waking
old echoes at Her Majesty's, Sydney. The
Honorable Hughie and other ghosts walk
or prance again according to their habit,
work off the old gags and thoroughly con-
vince the stalled patron (who had some sus-
picion of it before) that nothing on this
earth looks quite as old as a revived musical
comedy. The fine and plentiful music covers
a multitude of makeshifts: but it can’t con-
ceal the ruinous old property-liat that Addie
Lennard sings a song about and tries to sell
to the Duchess of Minster. It looks like
some Napoleonic relic that was picked up
on the plains of Poland 100 years after a
cavalry charge; nevertheless the stage
Duchess has to regard the battered relic
seriously through spectacles mounted on a
pole. Phil Smith wears the right size in
parts as Timothy Gibbs, and Minnie Love
makes a winsome Cousin Mary. Leslie
Holland prances again as the Honorable
Hughie and sings the ancient “Yip-i-Addy
as if it had been invented only yesterday.

ODE TO MY OLD PAL BILLY, THE
VENTRILOQUIST’S DUMMY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
With the Strollers back at the old address
The pangs of the winter time grow less.
1 long no more for the old men’s home,
Xor sigh for the past when the pint-pot's foam
Spread round the clock till 11 p.m.
And there was no need of stratagem
To slake one’s thirst when the day was done—-
Alas, those days of the midnight son!
I snore no more in the movie halls,
Nor groan in the stock the-a-tre stalls
At the sweet soubrette of forty-four
(The one who writes to the quack drug store:
“Dear Sir, I have tried your Dead Shot Cure,
And the pains that I had to long endure,
Have left me quite. Yours thankfully”—
I wish the pains that she gives to me
Cor Id be charmed away by the chemist wight:
I’d buy the whole of his stock on sight.)
Hut, coming back to this other show,
It'll do for me and the rest can go—
I lose my grouch, and forgive my cold,
And forget for an hour that I’m growing old ;
I drop my cares and I smooth my frown
When the Royal Strollers come to town.
X.S.W.

this Saturday and next, and on the interven-
ing Wednesday, the Repertory co. plays “The

Adelaide Repertory Theatre has success-
fully played the uncomfortable “David
Ballard.” It is a true Rep. play. In the
theatre which depends on the box-office,
David, breaking away from his soulless
family to live the Higher Life, must have
come back as a success. Here, at the
uncommercial Rep., he is precisely the fail-
ure they have all foretold, and, but for the
easy solace of love, his finish is as hopeless
as his beginning. Wilfred Neill, who is
just enlisting, made a brilliant farewell as
David, and was well supported all round,
especially by James Anderson as the in-
effectual father, Wadmore as the cad
brother, and Ray Walsh as the humorous
minor character who at the crisis of the
drama turns philosopher and takes the
centre of the stage. Three fine character-
studies were given by Dorothea Jacob,
Lthelwyn Robin (a trifle too refined in her
shrewish part), and Zoe Reid, who had to
look 15 and contrived to do it. The play-
dragged a little at times; and, to the
mostly-feminine audience which the Rep.
Theatre now seems content to attract, the
scream of the evening came with the
entirely unheralded and unhinted-at appear-
ance of twins in the last act.

“First Old Man”: How these orators
draw. In the Vic. Legislative Assembly the
other day they were hard at it debating the
farcical Reply to the Governor's Speech.
I hey were, as usual, playing to the gallery,
and I hope they were satisfied with the
encouragement they received. I shouldn’t
have been, for the only “strangers” present
were three women, who had brought their
knitting, two men and a boy. The menioers
ot any crowd I’ve ever been connected with,
if they couldn’t ensure better business than
that, got their salaries cut down or were
given the sack!
Answered the call, at 62. Arthur Lissant
whose association with the Australian stage
dates back to the days of the first Gilbert
and Sullivan productions. He also
appeared in the “legit.,” notably as Canon
\v ealtliy in ’All Caine’s “Christian.”
Lissant was a capable actor, in his younger
days owned a decent baritone and was a
rapid “study,” but somehow he failed to
get to the top. He understudied George
Lauri and other comedians for many years
and did good work when lie got his chance
as the Dook of Plaza-Toro. Probably his
success was as .Pooh-Bah— which
isn’t surprising, for there was something of
that superior person about Arthur Lissant
in private life. Of late years he was in
control of the stalls staff at Her Maj.’s
(Melb.), and was wont to stand at the door
gazing severely over his pince-nez at those
who entered. Rut regular patrons who
knew him liked him and looked forward on
‘first nights” to his magnificent military
salute. Only a couple of months back he
made one of his periodical “returns” to play
Kenneth Mugg in “The Belle of New York,”
a part lie had made very much his own.
The Gonsalez Opera management is
strangely austere in its advertising methods.
Each new production is announced in a
casual way without any reference to the
cast. _ The day’s ad. merely states that a
certain performance will begin at a certain
time.. The first appearance of a new tenor
or prima donna is not specified and no par-
ticular inducement to attend any particular
show is offered. The programme, however,
is very communicative. It gives the com-
plete casts for a whole week—six nights
and two matinees—as well as the names and
dates of the operas for the week following.
Thus the Saturday nigliters can learn who’s
who until the next Friday, and what’s
going to happen for six days thereafter.
But never a word about the plots of the
operas, which were a feature of the pro-
gramme when Williamson ran an Italian
co. 15 years ago. Gonsalez leaves the story
of the libretto to be divulged on sixpenny
sheets by a speculator who bought the right
to sell them in the theatre.
Maorilander Ernest Parkes, who was
with the Dandies for so long, is now
strengthening a Williamson combination
with his rich baritone notes. Before his
professional days the name of Era. Parkes
on a Wellington concert programme was
sufficient to ensure a packet? Town Hall.
“Harrison O.” : After three emphatic suc-
cesses with “Jelf’s,” “Quinney’s” and
Searchlights,” it looks as if Vachell had
misjudged his public with “Pen,” a dramatic
version of Morley Roberts’s “Lady Pen-
elope,” which has just been done in darkest
London. Pen, the niece of a Duchess, has
tor lovers a blushful viscount, a Yankee
money-spinner, a muscular captain and a
Jewish poet. Declining wedlock, she makes
each promise that he will be more like a
friend than a husband. When it is announced
taat unt . o aer . a son is given all four—three
out of imbecile gallantry—claim parentage,
but it is finally proved that the honor of
authorship belongs to the blushing viscount.
The critics for the most part refused to be
shocked, and insisted that they were merely
bored. “There may have been some bright
design behind it,” wrote one in a tired tone;
“perhaps in another and a better world the
veil will be lifted.”
“Fax”: An actor’s first qualification for
hero parts in spectacular dramas such as
“Romance” is ' the ability to look young
and lovely. He needs a small, unintellectual
sort of head which will carry a lot of hair
—real or assumed —without appearing top-
heavy. He should have a tall, slim figure
that lends itself to poses befitting the lover
in an old Family Herald illustration. His
smile must be warranted to kill any young
woman at 10 paces. His clothes must be
smartly cut, if not actually worthy of a
tailor’s dummy. His motto should be
“Every move a picture,” no matter what
type of character he is impersonating. In
these pictorial respects Frank Harvey, of
the Williamson Co., is a most unsatisfac-
tory hero. He looks neither young nor
“interesting.” He might be a civil engineer
who is absorbed in his work and doesn’t
care tuppence for appearances. No gallery
girl would think of adoring him for hi's
lovely eyes, legs or anything. He’s only a
good actor with a matter-of-fact manner
and a commonplace personality. He aims
ar, suggesting real life in dramas where only
theatrical effectiveness is required. Nobody
should be natural in melodrama.
“Norbell”: Bilanderson’s stage manager
had sternly interdicted all gagging, and
warned the late Temple Harrison, who was
the greatest offender, that lie Would be fined
heavily if he spoke anything but the author.
One of the mummers bet that he would
make Temple break out. That night
Harrison was seated at a table trying hard
to look dignified as a senior military officer.
The pledged destroyer entered as an orderly
and saluted. “Despatches, Colonel.” “Put
them on the table there,” said the Colonel
in accordance with the script. The elderly
should have done so and retired, but he
stood there and said earnestly, “They’re
very important, sir.” “Well, leave them
there,” growled his chief. “But,” he per-
sisted, “they’re very urgent.” “Put them
there, sir, and go !” roared the Colonel. “But
—but—they won’t keep!” Then the goaded
Harrison let loose. “Won’t keep? Well,
dammit man, put ’em on the ice !”
“Silas”: I saw him in Flinders-street
(Melb.) the other night. His companion
had some educated canaries; he was doing
the painless-man act. He had two darning
needles through each cheek, one through the
lobe of each ear, and sharpened knitting
needles stuck through the muscles of his
arms and into the calves of his legs. He
bristled like a porcupine, yet he was not
coining money. “ ’Struth,” he said at last
in disgust at the size of the collection, “it
takes a lot to satisfy the amusement-lovin’.
public nowadays.” Amusement-loving!

The old Innamincka, once the crack
passenger boat on the coast, has at last got
her passport, and is off to the vague “East.”
Six months ago the vessel, which has fallen
below the inter-State standard, was sold
to foreign buyers. Alarmed at the scarcity
of tonnage, the Commonwealth Government
had declined to allow bottoms to leave
Australia, and the Innamincka was held up
in Port Jackson. Seven months passed;
then, as the owners refused to run her, and
the Government wouldn’t, the authorities
gave in, and the “Inkie Pinkie” takes the
long green track that leads from here to
there.
The latest syndicate offer to finance Tas-
mania was very much like the one with
which the Earle Ministry dallied for a while.
Ihe payment was to be in stock carrying
o per cent, interest, and issued at £9B* with
a currency of five years. Public works were
to be carried out either for a lump sum or at
schedule rates ; and it was suggested that
this would allow the Speck to start steel
works or cement works or any other old
thing. But the Ministry would not bite.
Maybe it dislikes sindicates in general;
maybe it dislikes this sindicate’s back-
breaking rate of 5J per cent, or thereabouts
in particular.
Hannah, M.L.A., doesn’t see why Hughes
should have all the ships, so he 'is asking
the V ictorian Parliament to co-operate with
lasmania in running a joint line of
steamers. Lee, the Speck’s Premier, was
asked what he thought about it, and being
a good Tasmanian, he said “Nothing.” So
the new r line is not going to arrive all at
onc-e—which, taking the present price of
ships into account, is just as well. But the
matter will have to be gone into presently.
Inter-State trade can be carried on amongst
rive states by public railways which don’t
look for dividends; and the State which can
only reach the others by means of a con-
veyance which is looking for dividends isn’t
getting a fair deal.
UTTER BARBARIANS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sig’nor Comparo came to stay
At our fine “residential.”
Some of the dwellers rose to say
He was not quite essential.
A tenor he from some far clime,
Girls praised beyond all reason;
But. all the men growled at the mime-
They said perhaps he sang in time,
But always out of season.
One morn we bribed the rouseabout
To beard the trilling boarder,
And in the names of all the rout
To call the wretch to order.
Sam went with an unruffled brow—
His smile was almost saintly.
He said: “The gents sez, here and now,
You gotter stop yer flamin’ row!”
The Signor promptly fainted.
We heard a running on the stairs,
The sounds of females grieving’,
And sundry muttered Dago swears—
It was the Signor leaving.
“The row!” he moaned. “I weel not stay!
Sapriste ! How they treat you !
Oncivilised ! Igo avay !
And quick I go, for som’ fine day
These savage tribes vould eat you ! ’

Huns, who wore a self-satisfied smile for

thought it would terrify der vorldt into an
early submission to Sliermany’s vill to con-
quer—these swanking aliens have lapsed
into silence and sadness. They are looking
grey and glum, sick and sorry. They have
shed their swagger and put on an apolo-

petic air. Their news from Europe grows
steadily vo.rse, evidently. The final declara-
tion of peace was to be the signal for every
German and pro-German abroad to pose as
a true prophet and resume friendly relations
with the undeceived Britishers and others
who had not estimated Sherman organisa-

From Gerard Williams, Oapt. Iv. la., who
came to Australia to look at the mines ot the
Commonwealth for the London Times and
stayed to manage Mt. Oxide (Q-) •
We’ve got the Anzacs near us now, and I've met
several. I knew what to expect, because I have
several Australians with me. Key, second in com-
mand of this co., was late chief engineer of the
British Broken Hill ;he is now a captain. Martin,
the sergeant of the attached motor cyclists, comes
from S Aus., and several of the sappers have lived
down under. Our G.S.O. is a South Australian—
Malcin, Wiltshire Regiment, who got a D.S.O. while
in command of all that was left of his battalion m
last year’s fighting at Festubert.
T see from The Bulletin that the Broken Hill
miner has been proving again what a poor creature
he is at bottom, striking when a war’s on, and I’ll
bet getting talked to quite prettily by the Govern-
ment. I’d like some of the able-bodied perishers
there to see the work the infantry do every day for
them —for Is. a day. By God, the Infantry are the
men! When I’m back in peace and mufti again I'll
raise" mv hat to every Infantry man I meet who
wears the war ribbon. The gunner and sapper do
a bit in the trenches, but they don’t live there;
they only work there. But the Infantry during
their period of duty live there. The sappers are
splendid, always working in unhealthy places ; the
gunners contemplate hostile artillery “activity ’
with the dispassionate regard of the real critic who
knows. But, day and night, the Infantry live through
Hell. There’s all sorts of fine men in the world,
but the British Infantryman is the finest. If there’s
anything finer than him it’s his officers. I’ll tell
you a story—it’s of one of the Highlanders,
who are in our Division. We have an arch called
“The Brickstacks,” and the gentle Hun strafes .it
with “Big Minnies.” Now a “Big Minnie” contains
about 1201 b. of high explosive, and it is fearsome—
when it arrives on the right spot. A “Minnie”
buried some men and a subaltern, and another lot
of men were digging them out. Another “Minnie”
arrived, killing lf». and wounding and half-burying
the young sub. His men rushed up to dig him out.
Pinned there, this British kid cheerfully promised
to shoot any man who didn’t obey his orders to
go back to cover. And so they left him. With the
next shell it was all over.
The Hun is a brute, a destroyer of ancient cities,
a murderer of children, a spoiler of woman but he
fights and fights stubbornly. It’s the only saying
grace he has—and alas, he can’t even fight fairly.
Still he isn’t a coward. I’d rather be the Hun
who deliberately fired Louvain’s priceless library,
who used women as a screen, who murdered civi-
lians, than be a man under 40 who funked. For at
least the Ilun fought for his country.
“X-Swords”: Billjim does
not always win in his en-
counters with the English offi-
cah. One Johnnie, conspicu-
ously affecting an eyeglass, had
been sent to supervise an Aus-
tralian fatigue party. The full-
raoon tickled Jimbill; and it
was not long before every man
had his identification disc set at
a quizzical angle in his eye, and
had discarded the politeness of
the Australian language for
“Don’tclier •• know,-old - chappie,
eli-what?” The officah stood it
for a while, and then, “Stand
fast! : ’ he barked. To a man
the disc-monocled crowd faced
the superior authority. “Look
heah, you bally blighters!” he
said very quietly, and extract-
ing the window-pane from his
eye. “See if you can do this!”
With that he took the monocle
between his thumb and fore-
finger, gave it a spin in the air,
and dexterously caught it in
his eye as it fell. That was
enough for Billjim and his
mates. They knew they had
struck a “sport.” and thenceforward the
chappie could wear 50 monocles if he
wanted to.
“W.1i.11.” : At last the English Tommy
has got some of his own back on the Anza.cs,
and everywhere in France and England
we are met with the query, “Any trench-
mortars to spare, Australians?” This cryptic
utterance has its origin in the careless
loss of two Stokes’ mortars, which the Huns
captured during a night _ raid on the
trenches of a certain battalion. The worst
of it is these guns were absolutely “it,” and
their construction is now no longer a secret
to the enterprising Bosch. You can bet
we’re so pleased.
Montefiore, the North Adelaide home of
the late Chief Justice AVay, is in the market
again. It was bought by Paul Joseph in
connection with his wild-cat “City Temple
scheme, now abandoned, and is remarkable
as being the one and only thing for which he
paid. The Melbourne firm which had an
amazingly lavish order for furnishing lias
hastily recovered such goods as it had
started to deliver, and whether the house
will realise the £6OOO it brought in Maich
is what Joseph’s creditors are most anxious
to find out.

Flocks of bird-optimists sang of the
glory of living in Trone Wood, I ranee.
They had seen the sweating Germans pass
by with dust-grimed faces at the end of 1914
and hadn’t been blown out of their nests,
and the 1915 eggs had been hatched out
without disturbance. “Why, war’s a bless-
ing!” they chirped amongst the leaves of
1910. “Look at the crumbs these soldiers
scatter —we never had such a time befoie .
Then, after 20 months’ preparation, the
British offensive began, and the artillery
stripped and blasted Trone Wood till it
was a red morass littered with leafless poles
and dead men. It’s been a poor world tor
optimists this last two years.

“Jack’s” observations: —
Aviators are the bravest men in the world,
doff my hat to them all. But I think the British
a no-els are the most daring. Day after day they
soar into the clouds above the enemy lines, all the
time being shelled like fury. Some of them adopt
that cheery English haw-haw mannali, vou-know, and
that Sunny-Jim eyeglass on a piece of black ri > on.
But they are the lads and, as I say, 1 doff my lid
to them. . , ,
I saw a ’plane come hurtling through the clou s.
Suddenly she turned over and over from a great
height. Down she same at an awful speed. I
felt myself praying that she would right herself, bul ,
no' she fell into a paddock with a sickening crash
When I reached the spot she was just a blackened
mass of rubbish. A couple of Frenchmen had man-
aged to drag the mangled remains of the pilot and
observer clear. My mate had thrown his coat
over one of the bodies, when someone called out.
“Look, look! Here comes another ’plane. ’ Sure
enough, there was a ’plane making towards us.
She circled round a couple of times and finally
landed gently a few yards away from the wreev.
Two men scrambled out and came running towards us,
calling out, “Are they killed?” I said, “Yes sir,
they are both dead.” The inquiring pilot did not
look more than 20. Just a boy. He said What
rotten luckl” turned round, ran back, climbed into
his wings and soared. I don’t know where he
went. I suppose to try his luck once more with
the Hun. „ ,
Gentlemen of the R.F.C., I salute you once moie.

THE GENTLEMAN SHE WAS WITH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The lady-help has got one gag—
“ The gentleman I was with” ;
On ev’ry yarn it is a tag
“The gentleman I was with” ;
He stalks along at her genteel side,
She mentions him with a lady’s pride.
But his splendid name she’ll always hide—
“ The gentleman I was with.”
In him at least there was no flaw—
“ The gentleman I was with” ;
She drags him in to overawe —
“The gentleman I was with” ;
The bravest dream of her life, ho leans
Upon liis stick like a sketch of Kean’s ;
He sold no soap and he hoed no beans -
“The gentleman I was with.”
No doubt he was a decent bloke —
“The gentleman 1 was with” ;
Ready a friendly pipe to smoke —-
“The gentleman I was with” ;
But now he’s dressed in a fine disguise,
Because lie’s a sentimental prize ;
And so I listen and d —n ins eyes
“The gentleman I was with” !

"ilusketoon”: One of the biggest
"shouts’' I remember was paid for by Davy
Hall, the Cold Tea Minister for Justice
in Holman’s Government. lie was running
for Werriwa, and Niels Nielsen for Yass.
and they met at Grabben Cullen. At the
close of the speeches, Nielsen called to the
crowd: “Come on, boys. Davy is going to
shout 1” Hall couldn’t well get out of it,
but it hurt, because close on 200 men
rolled up at the bar. Nielsen told me
the yarn himself, before he went to serve
the N. S. Wales taxpayer from the peaceful
obscurity of U.S.A.
* * * *
The other day a Victorian Supreme
Court Judge and two legal lights solemnly
argued for hours over a matter of a dog
and a turkey. It appeared that an up-
country citizen, having “permitted his dog
to worry an animal, to wit, a turkey,’’ was
lined 205., against which he appealed on two
grounds—(l) that he didn’t “permit,” and
(2) that a turkey isn't an animal within
the meaning of the Police Offences Act.
After pondering the matter for a fortnight
his Honor decided that (1) a turkey is an
animal, (2) that appellant didn’t “permit’’
his dog to worry the thing. He therefore
<1 uashed the conviction, with £l2 12s. costs.
It is to be hoped the matter will not be
allowed to rest here. One Judge may decide
that a turkey is an animal, but there is
no certainty that a Full Court won’t put
it down as a vegetable, while the High
Court may conceivably decide it to be a
mineral. As for “permitting,” doubtless the
citizen did not specifically give his mongrel
permission to molest the turkey; but, then,
the ordinary individual, when he tosses his
dog a devilled kidney, doesn’t usually in-
form him he may eat it. No doubt he mai/
expect him to stand off and admire it as
a work of art or a now fashion, but the
L’INGENUE.
"It’s awfully nice of you to say that you
will always remember to-night.”
"Oh, Major, I couldn't forget it — to-morrow
is my birthday!”
assumption is that, having placed the clog
where he can come in contact with the
devilled kidney, he expects him to worry it
and ultimately masticate it. So with the
turkey. As an important matter, open to
much argument, the question should be
settled, of course with costs. Still, if it goes
no further, it is at least satisfactory to
know a turkey is an animal. There are
times when a layman would imagine it a
disease or a bad dream.
“Machete” from the other side: —
The participators in that famous “Soldiers’ Strike”
at Sydney are beginning: to arrive, and are receiving
a welcome which astonishes them. Sergeant-majahs
have developed an extraordinary habit of detailing
them for unpopular jobs, and the old hands are of a
rude, not to say aggressive, habit. One of the “strikers”
was boasting the other day of his performance. Where-
upon a hard-doing Billjim got up and smote him
most unceremoniously. “Wha’s tha’ for?” wailed
the smitten hero rising to his feet. “F’r insultin’ me
sister.” “Yer sister?” roared the other; I don't
know yer sister. An’ I don’t want to know ’er.”
“She’s an Australian woman,” says Billjim, senior;
“an’ you blanks have insulted th’ whole
boilin’, so take that.” And he took “that,” and
being satisfied, stayed with it on the ground.

A while back, however,
one of him, making the

picked up several lights,
and threw out curried
remarks to the cook for
building the beverage
so strong. When he
got ashore he apologised
to the cook, but deliv-

about other people.
Since his last trip

lamps, and from out-
side no one could tell
one from the other.
Later on, the Naviga-
tion Department sent

he found something, to
make a row about, since
the old glim is to be
doused and an acetylene
flash installed. Inci-
dentally, if any land-
lubber wants to know
what the mariner thinks
about the double-banked
light let him mention
the electric advertise-
ments which flick
around Sydney harbor
to a ferry skipper. But
for the skipper’s sake
let it be done in a lonely spot where the
police won’t hear.

“Centurion’s” plain tale from the Desert:
The other day a Yeomanry regiment received an
unexpected call from a party of Turks. The camp had
lo be abandoned. Later, two cursing Light Horse
regiments rode into the camp after a 40-miles
trek. The Turks had by this time gone home,
leaving the place mueh as they had found it. For
three days, having no supplies of their own, the
hungry Australians lived on the rations found on the
spot. Then the Yeomanry 0.0. came back. He was
exceedingly distressed to find kawlonial soldiery had
wolfed the extras in the officers’ mess, and
made free with such of the carpets, beds, chairs, etc.,
as the Turks had not carried away. “They even took
the General’s things,” he wailed when reporting the
matter later. There was also a vast quantity of
letters, mostly from truly ladies and a collection of
photos, which—well, no matter. That ends the first
act. The second opens with a blasphemous Aus-
tralian brigadier perusing an official complaint of the
“looting,” along with a bill for £4OO. And it ends
with the same brigadier superintending the packing
of the letters, photos, and oddments for Divisional
Headquarters as a set-off to the £4OO bill. The
third act hasn’t opened yet, but we are all anxiously
waiting for it.
“Suez”: The true Anzacs who were ex-
cluded from the great memorial service in
the Abbey because they were under the regu-
lation height for stage heroes wouldn’t have
fared any better if they had been at Alex-
andria when the Kitchener Memorial Ser-
vice was being held. Some nursing sisters
who had neglected to provide them-
selves with tickets or coronets turned up at
the church an hour before the show started
and found waiting outside in the sun a dis-
consolate group of 12 wounded soldiers who,
like themselves, had been spurned from the
sacred doors. But officialdom did its lunatic
best. When all the seats had been packed
tight (mostly by ticketed, sightseers) it let
in the weary and wounded people who had
merely been. taking part in the war and
allowed them to stand on their own devoted
legs while the memory of their dead chief was
being honored.
■af
The ability to keep a level head and do
the wise thing at the right moment stood a
>St. Kilda ( Vic.) man in good stead recently.
Returning home he found his wife, a licensed
publican, had just returned from the races,
whither she had gone in a strange motor,
with the owner alongside. Him he ordered
off the premises and then had a heart-to-
heart talk with the lady. Later on he called
a servant and said his wife was choking;
but the servant declined to be interested
and stayed where he was —in bed. A second
appeal moved him to take notice, and he
found the wife choked, with a motor veil
wound round her gullet. The husband sent
for the police and told all he knew, and was
duly arrested for murder. But the post
mortem showed death by slow strangulation
without marks of violence, and other evi-
dence hinted at previous threats of suicide.
It was a close call, though. The least pre-
varication or any attempt to conceal or
twist the facts would have brought that
husband within sight of the gallows.
“Uncle Jim” on a Maoriland superstition:
Apropos the recent voyage of the Enterprise, I see
The Bulletin has thrown a bucketful of doubt on
the story that the Daphne recovered the General
Grant’s cargo of early-Victorian gold from the
ancient wreck on the Auckland Islands. How the
Daphne could have done the salving I don’t know,
for here’s her story. I was in Invercargill at the
time of her arrival on the outward voyage.
She was reported at the time to have been bought
by a Melbourne syndicate, and had called in at Inver-
cargill to refit prior to continuing her voyage south
to the Aucklands. She was a week or two in port,
and before sailing the captain shipped a young chap
named Robert Boyd as an ordinary seaman. Boyd
was about 20, and a native of Southland. The
Daphne sailed, and had been away a few months
when one morning she blew back to the Heads and
the pilot and his crew brought her in over the bar.
Only one man, Boyd, was found on board, and he
had a strange tale to tell. It appears that after
reaching the Aucklands the crew began to explore
the coast of the island for the place where the
General Grant was supposed to have been wrecked.
Some weeks were thus occupied in combing the
island, but no clue was found. It was then decided
to search the coastline round the whole of the island,
and two boats were prepared and provisioned for a
cruise. Boyd was the only one left on board the
vessel. AVeek after week went by and neither of the
boats returned, and it was not until after the sixtli
or seventh week had passed that Boyd decided to
slip the vessel’s anchor and endeavor to reach the
MX. mainland. The first favorable wind came up
soon after he had made up his mind to go, and after
great trouble he slipped the ship’s cable and stood
out to sea. Favored with a steady wind, the brigan-
tine sighted Stewart’s Island some days after, and
then Boyd steered for Invercargill Heads. The
Southland Government immediately sent its steamer,
the Southland, Captain Greig, down to the Auck-
lands. The vessel was away several weeks' and
thoroughly explored the island, but not a trace of
the crew or boats was discovered. I can’t account
for the Enterprise people’s yarn, but I can vouch
for mine.

“Try Fluke”: The most important and
ruiUts-dutied John Hop I know of is the
gent, on the Chatham Islands. There is only
one of him, and lie plays second harp only to
one other, the stipendiary magistrate from
Maoriland. Constable Pooh Bah runs the
Customs, inspects the fisheries, propels the
post office, jingles the savings bank, registers
births, marriages and deaths, collects statis-
tics, keeps the rain gauge, etc., and in
between times does police duty from Wai-
tangi to Kaore-he-Kianga. Not long since
he performed a marriage ceremony like a
seasoned bishop, and it came as a glad break
in the day’s round. The young things were
Maoris, and a Maori wedding in the
Chathams is something to remember. The
bride arrived in a bullock-dray, and when the
officiating policeman shut his book all hands
wired into the wedding breakfast. The
little snack began at 2 pan. and lasted till
2 a.m. next day. There was dancing in the
slab mansion and dancing on the grass.’ and
every hour the M.C. rang a bell and yelled
“Another sitting,” and the guests got to it
again. There were jew’s-liarps going, and
accordeons and mouth-organs, and the police
sky-pilot himself did something hilarious on
a cornet. And at each sitting the guests
toasted the full and happy bride and bride-
groom, who stood up with the crowd every
time and drank their own healths in panni-
kins of good M.L. beer.

Dear heart, the shadows of your hair
Cloud over me, and once again
1 hear you say, “I do not care.”
Ah, sweet, that memory is pain!
With us the lone trees and the moon,
Manuka’s scent, the mopoke’s croon,
A wisp of lace, a vagrant curl—
I wonder who you were, old girl ?

STOMACH MEDICINES ARE
DANGEROUS.
DOCTORS NOW ADVISE MAGNESIA.
Just how dangerous it is to indiscrimi-
nately dose the stomach with drugs and
medicines is often not realised until too late.
It seems so simple to swallow a dose of some
special mixture or to take tablets of some
so-called remedy or other after meals, and
the folly of this drugging is not apparent
until perhaps years afterwards. Regrets
are then unavailing ; it is in the early stages,
when indigestion, dyspepsia, heartburn, flatu-
lence, etc., indicates excessive acidity of the
stomach and fermentation of the food con-
tents, that precautions should be taken.
Drugs and medicines are unsuitable and
often dangerous—they have as a rule little
or no influence upon the harmful acid, and
that is why doctors are discarding them and
advising sufferers from digestive and stomach
trouble to get rid of the dangerous acid and
keep the food contents bland and sweet by
taking a little bisurated magnesia instead.
Bisurated magnesia is an approved antacid,
which can readily be obtained from the
chemist. It is practically tasteless, and half
a teaspoonful taken in a little warm or cold
water after meals will usually be found quite
sufficient to instantly neutralise excessive
acidity of the stomach and prevent all possi-
bility of the food fermenting.—Advt.
PERSONAL
ITEMS
Lyndhurst F. Giblin, who left the Speck
recently as a captain in the A.1.F., is
the most versatile politician Australia
has seen of recent years. A son of
one of the brainiest Judges the island has
produced, he did a brilliant mathematical
course at Cambridge, and from there went to
Canada to join a pal in a timber spec. The
business failed badly, and Giblin passed to
the Klondyke. But gold-chasing didn’t pan
out any better, and the Tasmanian had to
pick up a crust as a river-pilot on the Yukon.
Next he homed to the Speck and turned
cocky farmer on a selection near Hobart.
But the wanderlust again cropped up and
he blew off to the Solomon Islands. He got
away in one piece, returned to Tas., and
has since then been farmer, schoolteacher
"NORMAN LOGKYER, Interstate Commis-
sioner, busy lately trying to put the
military canteens on a proper footing.
and M.P. in the Sosh interest by turns.
When last general election came around he
declined to re-enter the House, expressing the
opinion that an able-bodied man was more
use just now at the Front. Of course
some people think he’s eccentric.
Charlie MacDowall, late member for Cool-
gardie, who died the other day, had the re-
putation of being the first man to wear a
tall hat in Coolgardie. An auctioneer from
Victoria, he took the wild and roaring city’s
breath by suddenly appearing in a frock
coat and belltopper at a time when a shirt
was an effeminate luxury and even trousers
not indispensable. His style of oratory was
a little pompous, and he used to address the
Legislative Assembly as if it were an auction
mart. Otherwise he was a good Australian,
a good Laborist, and an all-round good fel-
low.
Kitchener wasn’t an iceberg all the time.
When he first visited Maoriland, the rabbit-
skinned mayor of a southern town, accom-
panied by promi-
nent citizens, met
him on arrival.
The Mayor was
loaded with the
usual speech of
welcome, but at
ttte critical moment
stage-fright grip-
ped him and he
stood speechless.
After an embar-
rassing silence he
managed to mum-
ble, “Well, sir, I
must confess I
have entirely lost
my nerve.” “ That’s
all right, Mr.
Mayor!” responded
“ K. ” smilingly,
“It’s a splendid
thing to have a
nerve to lose.” The
kindly word
warmed the Mayor
so nicely that he
suddenly recovered
and worked off his
recitation after all.

(Clerk of the House of Reps.), 61 on 27th;
and Andy Fisher, 54 on 29th.
That William Clifford, of Tassy, who
becomes a Baron through the death of his
only brother, Lord Clifford of Chudleigb, is
58. He had six sisters. One of them is the
Countess of Denbeigh. another the Condessa
de Torre Diaz, and two in the long ago became
nuns. The name of the family sea,t is Ug-
brooke Park, Devon, and to a station pro-
perty in Marlborough (M.L.), now owned
by Dunstan Vavasour, whose mother was
William Clifford’s aunt, this name of Ug-
brooke was given when young Bill in the
early ’SO’s went out to be a sheep farmer. But
he wasn’t much of a farmer —not nearly so
successful, for instance, as another member
of the great Clifford family, Charles, who
runs racehorses in Maoriland and once owned
Flaxbourne station and still has Stonyhurst.
Baron Bill married when he was quite young
a fine, handsome daughter of Maoriland at
Blenhiem. His heir is about 30.
From “The Oldest Inhabitant”: —
A product of the Blue Mountains, Charles Abraham
Wilson, died at Burwood (N.S.W.) a few days ago,
aged 76, and was buried in thf cemetery at Lawson.
In the mid-thirties of last century Jacob Josephson,
founder of a wealthy Sydney family, lived at Emu
Ford, on the Nepean River, and built an inn on the
river bank. All the Mountain traffic then passed Emu
Ford. In 1835 Josephson’s relative, C. A. Wilson,
kept the inn. Later on, as the Mountains were opened
up, the Wilsons settled at Lawson, having a license
for the Blue Mountain Inn. When the railway first
came Lawson was the only stop between Emu Plains
and Mount Victoria, and had a refreshment-room
looked after by Miss Wilson —known to everyone as
“the Blue Mountain Parrot.” The Wilsons owned the
land at Lawson on which the straggling town has
been built. With the demolition of the old inn C. A.
Wilson went further afield and opened an inn at
Wentworth Falls, which in early coaching days was
the Weatherboard. A son still holds the license.
“Daybreak”: The toast is Jimmy Rya“»
Itoyal Humane Society bronze medallist.
This nine-year-olcl Tasmanian boy earned his
decoration at Trowutta in the summer ot
1915, when a bush fire raced before a sudden
gale for his father’s new selection. Had
tried to get back, but the flames cut lnm
off, and Jimmy, his mother and his seven-
year-old sister, having been first driven by
the fire into the little home and then out ot
it, decided to make a dash for the creek
about 10 chains distant. The mother s
clothes were set on fire by the rain of sparks
on the way, and she fell never to rise again.
While the children waited and called, the
little girl’s frock caught: but Jimmy beat it
out with his bare hands, and at last flung
his sister down and shielded her with his
own body, thrashing out the fire in his own
clothes as best he could. They found him
hours afterwards, utterly exhausted and
cruelly burnt, but still holding out. Gentle-
men, Jimmy Ryan! May he live to com-
mand a regiment of Anzacs!
Cabled that a son of E. P. Field, one-
time of Sydney, has been killed in France.
E. P. Field, who is the son of an English
major-general, came to Australia as a
master at Sydney Grammar School, and
next was a judge’s associate in Brisbane.
Early in the ’eighties he came into promi-
nence by winning a libel action against W. C.
Windeyer, of Mount Rennie. The action
arose out of remarks in a report of Sydney
School of Arts, of which Windeyer was
president. Shortly afterwards Field went to
the Bar, and had several scraps with his old
enemy, being compelled on one occasion to
apologise on pain of committal for contempt.
While a barrister, he got religion in an
aggravated form, and used to preach in
Sydney streets during the lunch hour. In
a way, he was the Chidley of his time. In
1595 he went to the Cold Country, where he
is still living, and became secretary of an
“Evangelical Alliance” to propagate his
brand of religion. Always litigious, he was
plaintiff in a number of cases, even suing
his own Alliance, and eventually the English
Courts had to declare him a thistle or some-
thing under the Vexatious Litigants Act.
J. A. Pattinson, who takes cricketing
parson Stacy Waddy’s place as headmaster
of The King’s School, Parramatta.
(N.S.W.), was associated with two Aus-
tralian archgaiters while they were still
very niinor clerics in England. When Arch-
bishop Clarke came to Melbourne in i 902,
Pattinson succeeded him in an honorary
billet connected with Anglican school coun-
cils in England; and Archbishop Wright, of
Sydney, was rector of an adjoining parish
in Manchester. Pattinson came to Bris-
bane in 1910 as Canon of the local Cathe-
dral, and last year was temporarily head
of the Anglican college at Sydney Univer-
sity.
When the war broke out, Keith Ramsay,
son of David, of the Commonwealth Public
Service Commissioner’s office, was on a sail-
ing vessel in a South American port. He
completed the voyage, and then hurried to
the nearest place where khaki was being
issued. After a strenuous training he was
given a commission in the Seventh King’s
Rifles, and, later, was killed in France while
making a gallant attempt to bring in
a wounded man. Only 25 when he finished
up, he had found time between jobs as an
A.B. on a windjammer—now sealing, then
to Buenos Aires—to pass for his mate’s
ticket. You can’t keep Australians of his
type from a man’s job.

Died at Hobart, aged 87, Tt. .T. Lucas, one
of the Speck’s oldest solicitors and politi-
cians. He was for many years a member of
the Executive Council, and sat in the Assem-
bly for Kingborough. In 3887 he was Attor-
ney-General ; but the Government fell and
Lucas’s reign only lasted five or six days.
The death at Adelaide of YV illiam Tamsh
recalls the voyage, many years ago, ot the
Prince Regent, on which Tainsh was a
sailor, from London to Adelaide. Drinking
water ran out, and for the last three weeks
passengers and crew had to absorb bottled
stout, which formed part of the cargo. Six
of the crew died of scurvy, and 13 others
were half over the border line when the
alcoholic Prince reached port and asked for
something without a head on it. The fight-
ing teetotallers should record the case for
campaigning uses.
Mrs. Wallach, of Kooyong (Vic.), the flower
whom Franz plucked out of the musical-
comedy garden a few years ago, is tired of
hearing echoes of the past resounding
through the shrouded drawing-room of the
family castle and is making a move. There
was a sale the other day in the absence ot
the disaffected Wallach, who is spending his
warlidays at Langwarrin, and the tribe of
Israel and the clan of Mac Dealer assembled
in large numbers around the remains—a
strangely-mixed collection of antiques, dread-
ful-pasts, wedding and other presents, futur-
ist knick-knacks and fine china. It may be
taken as an indication that the interned
Wallach has at last given up all hope of a
glorious resurrection in a German Australia.
HIS MISTAKE.
BLOGGS (to marooned Ansae in London):
“Van you direct vie to the Bank of
England, my man ?”
BILLJIM (suspiciously): "Wot’s the popula-
tion of this Are place?"
BLOGGS: "Oh, about seven million."
BILLJIM: “Then why the ’ell pick on me ?”
Apart from the bit he had in N. S. Wales,
old David Mitchell, whom wild rumor had
alleged to be a millionaire, was not quite a
third of a millionaire when he died. If
Rumor were not naturally unreasonable it
would never have accused Melba’s hard-
headed dad of being three times more
wealthy than he was, Dave Mitchell was
firstly " a builder and contractor, and he
made much money in that useful line of
business. But he didn’t concentrate all his
thoughts on amassing money by the one
means. Anyone could get him interested
almost up to the day of his death in a
speculation which promised to develop the
resources of the country. The few million-
aires in Australia’s past and present have,
been useless favorites of fortune as com-
pared with Dave Mitchell, who did things.
Being taken prisoner at Ivut-el-Amara is
not the only bit of hard luck which has
struck poor old General Townsliend. He
was heir to the title and estates of his
cousin the Marquis up to May 12, 1916.
Then a brand-new son must come in to
upset the pot.
G. P. Donnelly, one of the best-known
wool kings in Hawkes Bay (M.L.), is still
going strong, at the age of 70 or thereabouts.
Apart from his penchant for the society of
Governors, he has always been an aggres-
sive figure in politics and elsewhere. In the
early ’sixties he was a mere liorsedealer
in Auckland, but when, in 1877, he married
the daughter of the Maori chief Ivarauvia
he acquired a great acreage which he was
not slow in turning to account. The other
day the old man was sued by a neighboring
runholder for damage resulting from a grass
burn. He was indisposed, but, so that
nobody should be inconvenienced, he sent a
message to the Napier Courthouse offering a
fleet of motor-cars to transport magistrates,
police, court officers, witnesses, counsel and
reporters to his place at Otatara, where the
dispute could be heard.
MUMPS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Queen Willielmina of Holland has the mumps, and
is remaining in seclusion. —-Court news.
Mumps!
Her Majesty has got the mumps.
She sits in sad seclusion.
The whole Dutch Court is in the dumps.
Secure from all intrusion
The fair queen in her royal dwelling
Must nurse the unromantic swelling.
Mumps!
Mumps!
How one’s romantic feeling slumps
Perceiving so domestic
A trouble as plebeian mumps
On one of rank majestic!
The regal air must know curtailment,
Associated with this ailment —
Mumps 1
Mumps!
Queen Wilhelmina has the mumps—
Oh, had some artist snapped her!
To cure th’ inflammatory lumps
In flannel they have wrapped her.
She quite a horrid little fright is
"With aggravated parotitis.
Mumps!
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

At best the figures are simply a sort ot hint
to the people interested that things are going
well or otherwise. For years past there has
been a steady increase of profits. We re
doing all right,” general-manager Ralston has
said in effect—“and I think we’re a shade
better off than we were six months ago.
This half-year he says: “Well, taking one
thing with another, I don t think the Q-N.
is making quite as much as it did 12 months
ago.” It it were the Q.N. would have had a
phenomenally good year; for it would take a
lot of progress to counterbalance the addi-
tional taxation which the terrific conflict or
the past 12 months has made necessary. And
then there is the probable effect of the Com-
mon wealth Bank on this and all other banks
business and profit. The effect may be larger
on their profit than on their business, every-
thing has conspired to help the Common-
wealth Bank; and it hasn’t missed many
chances. So we have had good old I nvate
Enterprisers like the S. M. Herald pouring
out leading articles on this conspicuous bit
of Socialism and declaring that it has done
so well for Australia that it deserves, etc.
On the other hand, a yen' large part of
Queensland is quite convinced that the Q-A.
deserves very well of the Northern State;
and if Queensland does more for itself after
the war, as seems likely, the Q.N. will cer-
tainly get its share of tne improvement.
Things are not going to flourish at once
when peace arrives, except perhaps during
a very brief period of mafficking: but tne
Avar will certainly help us to get a decent
tariff, and these, “repatriation” funds may
help us to develop the country a bit more
rapidly. The possibilities of Queensland,
thus assisted, are tremendous; and if the
future of the Q.N. isn’t bright, then our out-
look altogether must be very blue.
The bank, it will be noted, still pays no
dividend, and can pay none till it has wiped
off an old debt to the depositors who rushed
the bank in the ’SO’s. When the smash came
private creditors got paper for los. m the
£ • the other ss. was to be paid out of profit,
and till it was paid there was to be no divi-
dend to shareholders. The management has
been in no hurry to pay out this money. _ It
could have been done, of course, by claiming
as profit every possible penny and distribut-
ing it. But general-manager Ralston had
the experience of Drury to warn him, and
he was strong enough to sit down patiently
and remake the bank regardless of the
natural thirst of shareholders for dividends.
On the whole, they have been a very patient
lot, too; and, unless something very _ serious
happens, they look as though they might get
their reward in the early ’2o’s. This shows
the recent progress in the matter of repay-
ments :—• _ .

But, to be quite frank about it, that s not
true. After the big smash the bank decided
to be a sort of ostrich and pretend that it
couldn’t see the worst of its bad debts. It
passed these over to an Assets Trust t >.
at face value. But it was itself actually
the Trust Co., because it guaranteed that
concern’s liabilities; and as the assets were
realised it reduced the awful hiatus between
the Trust’s assets and its debts by appro-
priations of profit. That is mostly where
the amounts shown as written off have
gone : the balance has been used to reduce
the book value of bank premises. iSow the
old assets have been almost got rid of; there
is Only £13,386 worth left in the balance-
sheet. 'Probably they may fetch a trifle more:
for general-manager Heron may have a con-
tingency fund somewhere. Even so, how-
ever, and in spite of all the 20 thousands
that have been voted out of current profits
to reinstate lost capital, there is still,
ing to the balance-sheet, about £72b,000 ot
capital unrepresented by assets. Hence
there are not any real reserves at all.

truthfully

So in seven years the bank, besides writing
down its premises account, has
lost capital to the extent of £342,0,18, and,
as mentioned above, there is, according t
the accounts, £716,252 still missing. That
would be enough to keep the bank busy foi
another 11 years or so provided it could
make the same profits as it has been making
since 1909. But here another consideration
comes in. For a few years before the war
the holders of the ordinary shares—who are
supposed to represent the old, original pro-
prietors of the bank —had been getting
hungry. They had had nothing since the
smash. For some years they hadn t com-
plained about that—they felt too grateful
for merely being alive. Imally, however,
the directors decided that it wasn t a tan
thing to ask them to wait until all the capi-
tal had been made good.. But they couldn t
decently pay the ordinaries a dividend until
the prefs. (who had received their shares
in exchange for old deposits) had got the
full 4 p.c. to which they are entitled as a
maximum; so 4 p.c. was paid on prefs. _ as
from Jan. 1, 1914. That calls for £21,000
more per annum, which of course is £21,000
less available now towards reinstating
capital. On the other hand, every little
helps; and even if the accumulated profit is
earning only 5 p.c. the bank should be mak-
ing £17,000 a year more now than it was
seven years ago. Certainly the table
doesn’t show that it is making that much
more. Still a bank doesn’t always tell all
the truth. If it actually isn’t making that
much more, then it is a fair thing for the
Board to ask itself and its g.m. why.
Whether it was a decent thing for the
bank’s management of that tiine to make
the bargain it did with the depositors needn t
be discussed now; certainly, as things have
turned out, it was an excellent arrangement
for the old shareholders. As a result the
bank has £2,117,350 of pref. capital, cumu-
lative at the outset only as to 2 p.c. and
thereafter as to 3 p.c., and never entitled
under any circumstances to more than 4
p.c. Moreover, the £lO pref. share has no
larger say in the control than the 10s. ordi-
nary share. On the strength of this pref.
capital the bank can reasonably take on
deposit and use for ordinary banking pur-
poses £10,000,000. If the bank put the capital
into public securities it could earn much
more interest than it has to pay; and the
profit on the public’s money deposited on
the strength of the pref. capital would all
be available for the ordinaries. For the
present, of course, the problem isn t so easy,
because more than half a million of the
pref. capital isn’t represented by anything
at all. But with that restored the Com-
mercial of Australia will be an easy propo-
sition to handle, and the ordinaries will
have a very soft thing on. Provided nobody
interferes. " But something will certainly
have to be done to disguise the corpulence
0 r their dividends. For it will take less than
£20,000 a year to pay them 20 p.c.; and
even 20 p.c. is dangerous these times.

Evidently the C. B. of A. isn’t a pessimist,
for it has added £738,050 to its advances
during the year. There is no reason why
the business shouldn’t be thoroughly good
better, in fact, than that taken three or tour
years ago when values were high and there
was no war roaring into every officer's ear
to be extra-careful. Possibly a good deal
of the money represents short loans —especi-
ally advances on farm products. Otherwise
one would be disposed to say that, on the
figures, the'bank isn’t in a position to take
much more business, its liquid assets being
no greater than they ought to be.

On the figures this lias been no fat thing so
far; though the dividend has been whittled
dov r n from 10 p.c. to 8 p.c., there has been
very little surplus profit to lay by. f ilings
thus far haven’t come up to early expecta-
tions. Originally this was the concern in
which J. P. Wright, of tlie old N.S.W. Arbi-
tration Court, was the principal. On his
death, it appears, the family hung on to the
N. S. Wales business, which is run by Taylor
Bros., Ltd., and promoted this co. —Taylor
Bros. Co-operative, Ltd. —to take over the
business outside N. S. Wales. It doesn t look
as though there was much to sell; and, any-
way, £3OOO was all that was asked for the
goodwill. But apparently though the co. s
business was small it was very lucrative; so
in the first year there was a net profit of
£6070, although the capital was then well
under £30.000, *ind although the new and
enlarged plant, etc., had only recently been
completed. Certainly some of the seasons
since then have been unfavorable to the jam-
boiler. Supplies have been irregular and
prices high. But, giving all that in, it looks
as if the co. had to fight for its larger
market; and that means heavy expense.
This, the unfavorable seasons and the fact
that the co. has had a good deal of money
out most of the time in partly-completed ex-
tensions and additions would account for the
comparatively small profit. It was little
more than nominally an old-established busi-
ness that the co. took over; and 8 p.c. is a
very good yield indeed in those circum-
stances. The initial 10 p.c. shouldn’t have
been paid, of course. No dividend should so
far have been beyond 7 p.c.
Present capital is £52,822, of which
£40,000 is ordinary and the bnlance 8 p.c.
cumulative pref. There is £7170 of this
preference capital unallotted; and as th e '
is certainly under-capitalised authority < *
been obtained to issue the balance.
then the co. will have to lean heayi ly P
the bank during part of each year. On May
it owed £41,663 on one account and anothii ,
and no doubt earlier in the year it owed sti
more. The co. has a factory m Hobart as
well as the Melbourne mill, and there is a
Brisbane business. The premises and pla
represent £32,939, stock £43,442 book debts
£15,108, and the balance is mostly shares in
other cos. and sundries.

The results continue to be very disappoint-
ing. especially when the boom price of
copper is taken into account. At the end
of May, 1915, 2070 tons on hand were
valued at the usual £55 per ton. That lot
brought £B9 10s. 2d. per ton. The whole of
the last six months’ production was sold at
an average of £9l 12s. 3d.; but 18/•) tons
unshipped is valued, pending realisation, at
£55. Thus the half-year has had the benefit
not only of the high price for the current
production, but also of the surplus realised
on 195 tons —the difference between the
stocks at the beginning and at the end of
the half-year; and that means over £6ooo.
Also £8535 came from investments, as
against under £3OOO in the earlier half.
On the other hand the past half-year has
been charged with about £15,000 more
income tax than the previous half, the total
charge under this heading for the term
being £24.74(3. The £118,138 profit shown
for the half-year is net, the amount written
off against depreciation being £21,951, and
that is also slightly in excess of the amount
struck off for the previous half. When all
allowances are made the difference between
the net profit in the two half-years, as
shown in the table, is thus probably exag-
gerated a little ; but that there was actually
a. very serious difference is shown by the
fact that on working account alone the
balance for the first half was £225,520 and
for the second half only £177,136.
Several factors were responsible; but
larger outgoings were the chief. Working
costs per ton of Mt. Morgan ore were 475.
lOfd. as against 425. 51d. in the previous
period and 40s. 3fd. in the corresponding
period of the previous year. This was due
to (1) advances in wages, freights and
stores; (2) more broken time and conse-
quent reduction in ore treated; and (3)
a lower grade of ore. (1) Dearer wages,
freights and stores, the directors _ say,
account for over £60,000; but that is for
the whole year. (2) The working days
numbered only 144 as against 155 in the
previous term, while the average output of
ore was 1218 tons per working day as
against 3290. (3) The grade of ore
treated was 0.35p.c. lower in copper and
2.osdwt. less in gold than in the previous
term. Yet there does not appear to be
much hope of improving the grade if
average-value ore is mined. “At the pre-
sent time,” the directors say, “the total
reserves of smelting and concentrating ore
are 4,333,600 tons, averaging 2.6 p.c. copper
and 6.lSdwt. gold.” The Mt: Morgan ore
smelted during the half-year was 112,389
tons, with 22,881 tons concentrates; and
the 22,071 tons concentrates produced dur-
ing the period were the product of 72,074
tons ore. Hence approximately 185,000
tons of Mt. Morgan ore were handled, and
the recovery was 3675.36 tons copper and
57,1080 z. gold; so the average extraction
was 1.99 p.c. copper and 6.lBdwt. gold. The
copper may thus be increased a trifle ; but
the gold extraction is likely to fall away.
Including that won from Many Peaks
flux and purchased ore the copper yield for
the year was 8307 tons, and the total expen-
diture, including depreciation (which is
mostly a set-off against necessary renewals,
etc.), was £978,011. That means £ll7 15s.
a ton nearly—a very heavy price to pay,
and of course quite impossible if every ton
of copper didn’t get the help of nearly 15oz.
of gold. The net profit for the year
(£286,781) was equal to £34 10s. 6d. per
ton of copper; and that with a copper
market averaging £B7 per ton! It is quite
-obvious that Mt. Morgan is liable to get
into Queer-street if it can’t do something
much better than this. Even if the market
stood up to £67 per ton, the 112,000 tons of
copper in the mine, provided it could all be
extracted, would only show £1.625.000
Certainly on. top of that there are liqird

Some of the figures in the last line are only
estimates, and six months hence will no
doubt have to be adjusted. The basis, like
that of other S.A. co-operative concerns, is
rotten, the shareholder being entitled to
promptly withdraw his capital, though it is
sunk in plant, property or stock. And as
time goes on more and more of it will have
to go into premises and plant for making
jams, preserves, etc. Even now substantial
additions are in progress, and more are ahead.
Jhe committee “has recently had to engage
storage space from other establishments,
entailing heavy cartage and rental. In the
interests of economy and efficiency it is
hoped,” the committee adds, “that sufficient
capital will be subscribed in the near future
to enable us to proceed with a building
scheme which will ensure sufficient space for
some years to come.” Present so-called capi-
tal is £18,017, and the co. has debts of one
sort and another totalling £9324. Assets
°f stock £16,609, plant and premises
£8364, book debts £3951 and a few odds and
ends. There, are no less than 956 share-
holders —practically all growers and grocers,
the idea being to cut out as far as possible
the middleman who takes a bite out of both
producer and consumer. The scheme is excel-
lent ; and nothing in the world but disloyalty
or. bad management can stop the co. from
being a great and growing success.

Edna Central wired its Melbourne office
last week that the stone in the make off No. 3
crosscut was worth 7205. Later on it was
found that a mistake had been made in the
message, and that the stuff was worth only
34s.—pennyweights, in short, instead of
ounces. Of course, as the market had bolted
on receipt of the first news, there is a row ;
and it isn’t to end in nothing at all like so
many other mining fusses. This time too
many fat pigs got their tails singed. For a
start, exit R. Williams, the manager. Maybe
he wasn t so much to blame as some people
SBy U make sure > ? he B toc k Exchanges
ought (1) to get the directors to investigate
farther, (2) to help the directors by having
an examination made of all orders carried
out and wires received by their members, and
*3) to ask the Westralian authorities to
supplement any such investigation. It is
certainly necessary to know who bought and
sold ; and on that we shall be able to judge
whether It. W illiams is the victim of a piece
of bad^luck —or something else.
the Bullfinch meeting in London,
1. Doolette, in his usual optimistic way,
reckoned that prospects were all right.
Developments at 510 ft. had justified the
deepening of the main shaft to that point,
and, although the grade of the reserves was
down wS. 6d. compared with the previous
>eai, he was confident that when develop-
ment had been done in the two bottom levels
the average would improve. All the aliens
in the mine had been cleared out.
this had led to a serious shortage of labor,
ynd. to make matters worse, the quality
was deteriorating, owing to the number of
oung men going to the war. Xhere were
many parts of the mine which would, when
further opened up, place it in a much-
improved position, but to do this would cost
about £12,000. The Board hoped to give
shareholders another div. before the end of
the year. After that they must not expect
much until the policy of development mapped
out had been completed and the value of the
mine at greater depth demonstrated. Mean-
time it was intended to test the main shoot
down - to 600 ft. by winzing, and thus avoid
the considerable expense that would be
entailed in shaft sinking and crosscutting on
the blind.
* * * *
Word has been received in the West from
Senator Pearce that the exempting of gold-
mines from the provisions of the JVar-time
Profits Bill will be considered when the
measure comes before Parliament.
Nullagine (W.A.) expects to start sluic-
ing for gold and diamonds within three
months. It has 530 acres, but only a bit of
this has been tested. Still, according to
Colin Fraser, there are about three-quarters
of a million yards of alluvial averaging
2s. lOd. to start on. The capacity of the
plant is estimated at from 20,000 to 25,000
yards per month. Several diamonds have
been found. One, valued at between £6O and
£7O, was presented to Bill Robinson and
taken by him to London to be cut.
* * * *
Melbourne broker Tricks has not yet got
liis Empress mine at Mount Magnet (W.A.)
going properly. Manager Hartrick left it
pretty sick, and it has just straggled on
under his successors. The last of those is
W. J. Rickard, who made a name for cheap
mining at New Chum Goldfields, Bendigo.
At the Empress he went south at the 335 ft.
level, and as he punched ahead values ran
at one time up to loz. With 4ft. of stone it
looked a monte that he woum make a splash,
but the battery refused to back up the pan-
nings. Rickard has enough optimism to feel
that a strong reef like that of the Empress
ought to have a decent trial, so he has got
his Board to give him a bit of cash to spend
on seeing whether the mine can stand up to
the collar.
* * * . *
There’s a row in Hobart over the State
Ministry’s intention to lease 50 acres at
Derwent Park to the Amalgamated Zinc Co.
Someone has found out that the land is
wanted for a model suburb and that a
wretched, zinc plant would spoil everything.
That gives the North its chance; it says
th'ere’s square miles of land to be had on
the Tamar, with a deep-water frontage and
24 hours’ steam nearer Broken Hill, where
the material for treatment is to come from.
* * * *
“D.F.”: Poor old J. H. Gillies is getting
it in _the neck again. According to the
Hobart Daily Post there’s a dark con-
spiracy against him on the part of Amalga-
mated Zinc and the Mount Lyell Co., who
are out to see that his process for treating
zinciferous ores doesn’t get a fair trial.
Amalgamated Zinc’s move in getting a power
contract with the Speck’s Ministry and
snapping up a site at Derwent Park, right
on the power-transmission line and handy
to Hobart, while the Gillies Co. has been
shunted away to North-west Bay, is alleged
to be part of the plot. It is hinted that, in
order to keep the big fellows from squeezing
him out and ensure getting some ore to
work on, Gillies has had to acquire a mine
on the West Coast (Tas.). But if he has
got it, why all the yell? Anyway, the Post
and some other people in the Speck are out
to upset the Amalgamated contract, if they
can. They say it isn’t legal, though the
Attorney-General says it’s all right. Any-
way, the contract doesn’t have to go to
Parliament, so it will be a job to disturb it.
* * # *
Six months after the close of the period
with which they deal, reports of the Briseis
‘•Tas.) stroll in under order from the
London branch of Tom Husband’s office.
Lindesay Clark recounts that just enough
water was available to keep the nozzles
dribbling on to the last remnant of the old
Briseis claim and to sluice some of the top
drift on Ringarooma Hill. But good rain
fell then, so this year he has already cleaned
up 223 tons black tin against 382 - tons for
the whole of 1915. His working profit was
£20,007 for the year, and £13,000 for the
last six months. Taxation was ravenous in
London, so that, after the usual little depre-
ciation allowance had been made and the
London Board had got its whack, onlv £3904
was left to carry forward into 1916. The
grip on the reserve of £50,000 isn’t relaxed,
and the co. had £7700 in cash on December
31. This plus the profit of £13,000 to June
30 may mean that London will feel inspired
to shell out another div. before the winter
campaign is over. Clark has sluiced 1564
tons black tin out of the Krushka flat, and
he counts on another 800 tons before he
starts to think about shunting the Ringa-
rooma River into a new channel. Mean-
while he may hunt round for any dabs of
the rich stuff which kept the old North Home
mine a performer, and will also try to get on
to the drift below the lower layer of basalt
in the first section the Ringarooma Hill.
Already he has trucked 2,000,000 cub. yds. of
burden away at a cost of 7fd. per cub. yard,
thus clearing a good bit of his track to the
4000 tons of oxide that he counts on getting
out of the drift below No. 1 pile of bluestone.
It won't be to-day or to-morrow; but if
people hang on to Briseis and tin doesn’t
slump too far, they should have a fair run
of divs. Clark’s side show is dredging for
gold. He does this on the Wallace claims
(Vic.), where his four machines shjfted over
1,300,000 cub. yards last year for gold worth
£21,781. Running exes, were £16,871, so
he must have more than met all Australian
staff costs out of this show alone, thus free-
ing the Briseis mine of outgo in that direc-
tion.
As Upper Langi Logan got £l5OO to help
it keep going after having done very little,
it would have been too glaring if an old
battler like Duke Extended, Maryborough
(Vic.), had been turned down; so it is to
get something. But the something is a pal-
try £5OO, though it has as good a chance of
proving a run of dirt as the other show. A. W.
Moore now runs the Extended and reports
16dwt. dirt to the south-west, with the pay
wash widening out to 100 ft. His programme
is to push out and get under the surface bore
which gave good prospects in the
misty past, thus learning, incidentally,
if the gutter really has split into
eastern and western runs of wash. He
also will get ahead of No. 7 rise to
find out what the gutter is like towards the
Duke Consols. Of course, it would be a nice
plum for Consols if the Duke Extended fell
into its lap—and shareholders ought to re-
member that when they talk of slinging up
their scrip.

The usual tall talk over Catherine Reef,
Bendigo, doesn’t hide the fact that the
monkey on the fence is patchy ground. The
reef at 2200 ft. is big enough to run out divs.
like shots from a quick-firer, but from its
baby days it lias been a tricky lot. Tributors
working on the spurs got the show on the
move, but their reign won’t last long. When
the co. enters on its own it will have over
500 ft. of reef to pick and choose from. The
tip is for a run of divs., with in-and-out
yields to keep the manager on tenterhooks.
If Central Blue had as much to show it
would be up to 205.; but the mugs have
been nipped by Catherine Reefs before, so
they are handling that stock very cautiously.
Porepunkah, Bright (Vic.), isn’t dead
yet; but it is some time since the dredge
emptied anything into shareholders’ pockets.
On a capital of £5OOO it has recovered
£34,457 worth of gold ; but last year there
was a net profit of only £251 on the £2325
raked up.

restive, and there has been talk of realising

* * * *
For the half-year ended April 30 Corella,
Cloncurry, smelted 4272 tons ore for 257
tons copper and 14Soz. gold. Profit on
working account was £B7OO, or more than
£33 per ton of copper; the metal averaged
£l3l per ton, and Corella should be de-
voutly thankful. At the end of the term
there were liquid assets valued at £4700 in
excess of liabilities; £3155 of this was in
cash. It takes £5OOO to pay Is. per share,
so although the matte produced in June
gave 117 tons of copper, dividend prospects
are obscure. A co. producing matte has
usually a large quantity of products in pro-
cess of realisation, and the Corella had no
less than £30,000 thus hung up on April 30.
Obviously a rapid decline in the price of
copper would hit the concern hard.
* * * *
Little St. Mungo, owned mostly at Char-
ters Towers, paid its 23rd div. the other
day, and that makes £14:000 on a capi-
tal of £5OOO, of which £4500 went to pay
for the mine. There is on hand another
£2500 or so (net) in broken ore and cash.
Last half year wasn’t brilliant, in spite of
the Ilia'll price of copper: after allowing for
depreciation the co. had £491 to jingle. There
have been troubles with machinery and labor.
The ground between No. 4 and No. 5 level
(2Soft.)_ is giving most of the ore, and the
small rich vein there isn’t likely to be the
last: a lateral make was lately cut at 340 ft.
The stuff sent away last half averaged 22
p.c., and there were just over 300 tons of it.
* * * *
Carpathia (N.S.W.) had a sale this week
at 45., so there is still at least one man who
hasn’t been quite fed up on Ardlethan. He
will certainly have a better chance of mak-
ing money than the crowd which came in
at 30s. The co. has a handy cash balance,
and, besides a big heap of seconds, owns a
fair heap of concentrates which will be put
through again to bring them up to the
required 60 p.c. The mine will also go on
yielding patches of ore—perhaps more than
enough to keep the pot boiling for some time.
* * * *
The Adelaide Stock Exchange got a shock
last week, when a local gold-mine declared a
dividend —of a shilling on its 20.000 shares.
It is the first time such a thing has hap-
pened in South Aus. for a quarter of a
cc-ntury. The State has gold nearly every-
where in small quantities, but not" enough
of it in one place to make mining pay, even
where fossickers can knock out tucker. The
co. that has broken the spell is the Delo-
raine, which works in the hills near
Adelaide.
* * * *
The S’Afric-an House of Assembly has
agreed to a special levy of £1,000.000 on gold
mines. Payment is spread over three years:
£300.000 in 1916, £500,000 in 1917, and
£200,000 in 1918.

One result of the Mt. Lyell Co.’s interest
in the Hercules-Rosebery field has been a
rattle among the dry bones. The Rosebery
Lodes Co. is having its little out in, and the
other day the Government assayer reported:
Gold, 2dwt. 21 grains; silver, Boz. Tdwt.;
lead, 7.(54 p.c.; zinc, 14.09 p.c. The co.
reckons it is on a big body of good sul-
phide ore, and says it has a buyer waiting
for the stuff.
“Six-and-Eight”: Whoever told “W.A.M.”
(B. 13/7/T6) that he would have to patron-
ise Costs to the extent of £lB gave him
rotten information—so far, as N. S. Wales
is concerned, at any rate. The Registrar of
Probates or any of his agents (C’s.P.S.
generally) will get letters of administration
or probates in estates under £3OO. The fees
charged are 10s. for the first £5O of value,
2s. 6d. for every additional £5O, and the cost
of a probate advt. in a daily paper—about
five bob. The total cost therefore in aN. 8.
Wales estate of £lOO would be under a
pound. Or alternatively, if the estate be
worth more than £3OO, “W.A.M.” may go to
the public trustee, who will do such jobs for
a commission of 2| p.c., or less in the cases
of big estates.

The General Electric Co. manufacture Mine
Electric Locomotives of both storage battery and
trolley types, which will withstand the most severe
operating conditions of mine service. Careful study
of locomotives in actual service and a thorough
investigation of the varied problems of mine-haulage
has resulted in the introduction of new improvements
and refinements of design. These locomotives cover
a range of sizes and forms wide enough to include
any haulage requirements within the demands of
electric locomotive work, and incorporate all those
features which have been found most desirable bv
practical mine operators. Anyone requiring- either
information or quotations in this connection mav
obtain same by communicating with the Australian
General Electric Co., corner Wentworth Avenue and
Goulburn Street, Sydney ; or corner Queen and Little
Collins Street, Melbourne.— Advt.
ALUMINOFERRIC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.
