A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, July 24, 1916.
My Dear Arini. —
Friday’s meeting at Government House
was a social advt. for tlie Flag rally. IF \.
Braddon addressed the company from a forti-
fied area behind the grand piano. W ith
samples of the Union Jack he goaded
those present to enrol their names—for
a shilling a head —in tomes that are hence-
forth to rest, in a suitable sarcophagus in
the Mitchell Library. Mrs. Mary Gilmore,
asked to speak on behalf of "Women War
Workers, flung a literary posy at the. thou-
sands of nameless toilers who work silently
in the background for every patriotic fund.
War-Chest Walker gave a brief and lucid
story of the British flag, and then tried to
flee, but was buttonholed by a good lady
who told him, at considerable length, that
every State-school child already knew the
tale. Mrs. Mackinnon, in honeyed tones,
described her own emotions —a favorite
theme —when she suddenly beheld a Union
Jack. Lady Edeline was in the front chair;
and Mrs. Danger Owen, worn with overwork
for the Red Cross, sat, as usual, in the back-
ground.
The new young gentleman help, Lieut.
Boulton, came back with his Ex. from
Melbourne. He's the slightly-damaged hero
I mentioned a few weeks ago. on leave from
a Highland regiment. The Stricklands met
the poor boy in Victoria and led him over.
He’s only 19.
Having only a clouded notion of what the
Women’s Movement is going to do for Aus-
tralia, one treads warily. There’s going, for
a start, to be a big stir at the Women’s Club
next month. That slender bundle of nervous
energy, Mrs. David, is president of these
Girls o’ Freedom. “The old shackles” —this
is how the Movement talks—will be broken.
Emancipated woman is to sit in both Houses
and knock the nonsense out of hon. members
of the Opposition sex. If the Girls get shaky
on their planks Annie Golding, who is on the
committee, will stiffen the supports. Mean-
while, Man wriggles uneasily. Already he
feels the Movement’s hatpins in his bones.
Writing in his tent, “Somewhere on the
Indian Frontier,” Lieut. Cecil Rae drops in
with a pithy remark. “You often speak of
coincidences. Well, here’s one. I was sit-
ting in our mess tent reading the Woman’s
Letter in The Bulletin of May 18. Just as
I read a par. about Miss Janet Fell, of Syd-
ney, a strange officer rode up. He intro-
duced himself as Fell, of Sydney, and said
he w T as a nephew of John of that ilk. I
picked up the paper and showed him the
item about Miss Janet, who is his cousin.
It was news to him, as he’d just been trans-
ferred from Mesopotamia, and had had no
home letters for months.”
Rae, who Avas a well-known mining engi-
neer in Queensland a while back, is now
bossing a company of sappers and miners.
With other units they’re watching, like
terriers, one of the roads by which the
enemy rat might try to reach India. Talk-
ing of heat he says: “The old Australian
‘Hay, Hell and Booligal’ needs revising. We
can give all three a start of half-way round
the course and win easily.”
Passengers by the recently-departed Malwa
included the Countess of Glasgow, wife of a
former M.L. Governor.
Winifred Docker, eldest daughter of the
Judge, has been busy for months past help-
ing to amuse Tommy Atkins in the Cold
Country. Uncle Arthur and his wife lend
a hand occasionally. Mrs. Arthur is a sister
of Carr Smith, the big, bearded parson who
introduced chasubles and other millinery at
St. James’s. Carr looks after a collection
plate nowadays at Grantham, in Lincoln-
shire, and has just been made a canon of
Lincoln Cathedral. Let’s hope the Zeppelin
commanders who raid those parts occasion-
ally will know -enough about ecclesiastical
matters not to think he is some sort of
howitzer which ought to have a bomb
dropped on it. Arthur Docker’s eldest son
(by a previous marriage), a major in a
British regiment, was killed in action in
Belgium just after the war started.
A note from an Artillery boy at Wands-
worth Hospital,, where many sick Kan-
garoos are sent:—-
Queen-mother Alexandra looked in the other day
and took great interest in a ward in which Queen
Amelie, of Portugal, assists several afternoons a
week. The visitor, who is very deaf, you know,
noticed one Billjim with a badly-swollen face. He
was one of the hospital orderlies, but had been so
badly bitten by mosquitoes that they had sent him
into the ward for a couple of days. Sister Barrett
tried to explain this. You can imagine how the lads
heads shot under the bedclothes to stifle noises when
royalty exclaimed: “You poor boy! And did it
knock your teeth out?’’
Our enterprising ally has scored again.
There are Japanese pianos now on the Syd-
ney market! They are cased in a strangely-
perfumed wood.
Ruth Bannister, the Cremorne girl, now
in London, who sculps in wood, has been
given an order by the Russian Grand Duke
Michael for a couple of chairs for his front
hall. Michael is domiciled in England,
where his morganatic wife is known as the
Countess Torby. Their countess daughter
has but lately accepted a sparkling half-hoop
from the Battenburg princeling who was
out this way not so long ago.
The other day Dorothy Boulton took _ a
plain gold ring from Engineer-Lieut. Dennis,
of H.M.A.S.’ Brisbane. He had the luck,
by the way, to be on the Sydney when it
pounded the Emden —a thrill he has put
away as an heirloom. Dorothy is the only
daughter of Mrs. Boulton, of Gladesville.
Father —now passed over the border—used
to manage the Union Bank at Clunes (Vic.).
Two of the bride’s brothers are at the Front,
so a quiet wedding preceded the honeymoon
to Cairns. Bride Dorothy, who calls artist
Margaret Flockton her auntie, wore cream
outfit with the prevailing seagull millinery.
Miss Theo Cowan’s is the face that meets
yours in this little
portrait. The
sculptor is a Syd-
ney-born girl, but
has lived nearly all
her life in foreign
parts, and was edu-
cated in Italy,
where she also
studied her art
under Riv a 11 a.
Busts of Dr. Flin-
ders Petrie (the
Egyptologist grand-
son of our famous
Matthew Flinders),
the Bishop of
London and Mrs.
Brown Potter
stand to her credit.
In our own
National Gallery Tlieo Cowan is represented
by a bust of its late president Du Faur.
At present she is engaged modelling a sol-
dier with kit and rifle—a regular Tommy
Anzao, which will serve as a model for the
disabled soldiers who propose to work at
toy making.
In 1909 Mrs. Ivettlewell asked her friend
Mrs. Clunies Ross what she thought of
Chief Gardener Maiden’s idea of wattle as
the flower of Australian patriotism. The
reply was favorable. So the two conspira-
tors formed themselves into a deputation,
and saw the Botanic Gardens boss. He
gave a glad “Yes” .to the notion of creating
a Wattle Day League. The babe drew its
first breath at a meeting in the Royal
Society’s rooms, and the founders were soon
submerged in wattle literature. This was
poured in from every hamlet in the State.
After seeing yellow for some time, the
League settled down to business. Just now,
it is preparing to give our soldiers a wattled
feast on August 1.
Things are different. Two years ago, or a.
little less, the war was a novelty ; and don’t
you remember the fervor with which our ex-
G.-G. Lady Dudley went into the nursing-
business at Boulogne and generous old Lucas
Tooth poured out beer-money on the Aus-
tralian Voluntary Hospital? But Kitchener
had little use for either of them, and the
hospital, magnificently equipped, was
to only when there W3.S no room unjwhere
else. Meanwhile an expensive staff, etc., sim-
ply ate up money; and a plaintive letter by
last mail told that they’d all got a month’s
notice and couldn’t sleep at nights for the
howling of the wolf at the door.
Parson Robinson, shepherd of souls at
Coff’s Harbor, annexed Ruby Stevens at St.
Philip’s the other evening. The tall, willowy
bride is a daughter "f George Stevens, of
Berry and Co. A mist of white tulle fell
over' the usual bridal plumage. _ Brides-
maidens Johnston and Bateman, in flufty
white garments, carried baskets of roses —a
charming floral climax. And Parson Cook
was second-best man.
The George Sly family seems to have
settled down for a while on the other side.
Now father goes with daughter Hazel by
way of the Cape, to see how they’re getting
on.
A recent snapshot of “Gerry” Cooper
showed that young man in khaki _ and a
British training camp. Father William, by
the way, after waiting for the English bliz-
zards to blow themselves out, had the bad
luck to fall ill on the way ’Ome. Carried off
the Vancouver boat at Honolulu, he came
back to Sydney to recuperate. Now he
makes another start by the Niagara, to join
the rest of the Cooper baronetage in Eng-
land.
A subdued chorus of feminine uismay
bailed the Frampton bust of Edith Cavell -
the nurse who was martyred by the Huns.
Marble is cold; still it is suitable for the
presentment of the dignity of death. But
Frampton. It.A., judging by the photo, of his
work, has mistaken dullness. for a.gnity -
dullness relieved by large stiff. bows under
the chin, which give an odd Christy Minstrel
effect.
Dr. and Mrs. Larkins are home in Mar-
riclcville after three months’ honeymooning
in Japan. One of them—l won’t tell you
which—brings word that at some of the
springs up there mixed bathing in the garb
of Adam and Eve prevails. Tourists have the
fact indicated by guides beforehand, so they
go on —or bolt.
Furs, according to a recently-returned
Sydney woman, are so cheap in China that
some Australian women were buying pelts
to match every frock. The Australian Cus-
toms officials have been rather hard to con-
vince ; but as it’s quite possible to wear a
new fur coat every day most of the stuff
squeezed through duty free. But you can
get more than you pay for when you buy
fur in China. I won’t tell you what.
The Apollo Club (nee Liedertafel) gave a
sing-song in the Conservatorium the other
night. Arundel Orchard shook the baton
with special vim over the “Barcarolle.” The
swinging melody from “Tales of Hoffman”
has been arranged for the Apollos by their
conductor, and it goes gaily. More solid food
from “Rig Veda” consisted of incantations to
ancient gods. But it was hard to imagine
the sacrificial flames addressed by W. H.
Parkinson in his solo on the Conservatorium
platform. The soprano of the evening was
Katie Stevenson. Goossens, pulling himself
together after last week’s shock, was in
better form.
A voice from Fremantle breathes o’er
Eden, per penny post. Lieut. Jack Kerry,
of the R.A.G.A., says he has married the
nicest girl in the West. She’s Marie
.Haynes, a daughter of R. S., K.C., and was
a visitor to these parts a couple of years
ago. The pink-and-khaki wedding occurred
in Perth R.C. Cathedral, where R.A.A. men
supplied the usual arch of steel.
This week Lieut. Griffiths claims Merle
Campbell for his own. In St. John’s, Dar-
linghurst, I fancy.
The Women’s Horticultural Society is now
actually on the land. It’s a suitable patch
for experimental gardening, at Chatswood,
furnished by Mrs. Harding. For the
present a gardener of the other sex is
demonstrating to the spade ladies, and his
Saturday afternoon-at-homes have been well
attended. The lessons cost members only
sixpence a time. The next move is to
have an Eve trained by Adam. Then she’ll
take hold of the demonstrations. The
Ilorti.’s new depot opens this week. You’ll
find it snuggling up alongside the Cafe
Franqais in George-street.
William Holman, senior, of the
N.S.W. Premier,
belongs to the old
English school of
dramatic art, which
insisted on distinct
el-o-cution as first
aid to winning an
audience. The mod-
ern actor, notably
the U.S.A. brand
which has been so
much in evidence of
late years, gener-
ally treats language
like chewing-gum
(Fred Niblo was a
brilliant exception)
and merely ejects
the mangled re-
mains. As for the
U.S. actress —well,
she scorns to take any but the front stalls
into her confidence. On his standing as an
elocutionist, Holman pere is well placed as
teacher of his art in the State Conserva-
tcrium. His photo, is included in the Letter.
The Vagabonds played “Dr. Wake’s
Patient” in the Repertory on Saturday
night. Results go to their cot in the Ren-
wiek Hospital. There was a floor-to-ceiling
audience, so there’ll be enough and a bit
over for the sick babes. The only brick—
really a pebble—one throws at the Vags. is
over the long waits, a complaint common
to all amateurs. In consequence suburban
society had to bolt for home before the
trembling hero screwed up his courage and
audibly kissed the heroine. The old farmer,
Andrew Wake, cleverly played by Jack
Mackey, was up to professional form. Ruby
Coxhead pleased as the mild old farmeress.
Frieda Bennett as a haughty Countess was
well-frocked for the occasion. But her stage
daughter, Lady Gerania, was obsessed by a
waggling feather and a bank-holiday get-up.
One of the 4th Battalion. Lieut. Travers
Brett, took possession of his legal property
in St. Philip’s the other eve. The bride was
the tall and comely Essie, a younger daugh-
ter of the Roseville Mutton household, lhe
’groom is over six feet in liis socks, so the
pair made a stately progress down the aisle,
with four pink rosebuds —-Gracie Brett, Lily
Perry* and Marcia and Vivienne Mutton m
attendance.
Sydney has surged with wintering Maori-
landers this season. Amongst them came
and lately went Mrs. Bob Gillies, of \V ai-
mate, S. Canterbury. She used to be one
of the Woods, of Bega. Here she saw off a
soldier brother who went w T ith a camel corps
commission. Bob Gillies is one of an Auck-
land clan and related to Dr. Sinclair Gillies.
Dame Hay, of Crow’s Nest, and various
other Scotch people. Other M.L. gadders
are the Elworthys and Mrs. (Major) Willie
Bond, from Christchurch, who are camped
at the Australia while Mrs. Bond (who
used to be an Elworthy) looks for her mis-
bud health. Doctors and a private hospital
gave her a fair start. The Elworthy widow
owns sheep and acres at Pareora and three
squatting sons, the youngest of whom is in
khaki and France.
Mrs. Walter Johnston, from windy Wel-
lington, also seeks our sun. It was her
nephew, Fetherston Johnston, who was killed
in France the other week, making the third
child of Charlie Johnston, M.L.C., to pass
out this year—two of them Hun victims.
His daughter Zoe lost her husband, “Booty ’
Levin, at the war, and she herself died
lately. Lieut. O. K. Fetherston Johnston
was a pupil of St. Ignatius College, at
Riverview. The five Johnston sons all
joined the colors.
William J. Little, M.L.C., writes from
Samarai to say, apropos a recent mention
of Mrs. Lewis (formerly Nurse Tait), that,
great as that lady’s services were, the awful
dysentery outbreak at Lakekamu had been
mastered before her arrival by the late
Nurse Ellen Combley, of the Anglican Mis-
sion, and Miss Nowland. A movement is
still afoot at Samarai to establish some
memorial to Nurse Combley.
Those Red Cross Club rooms which hum
with spinning machines have a new fore-
woman. She is Mrs. Southern, a Westralian,
who is aiding while she’s visiting.
One of the Potts Point Nettlieim family,
Leopold, a son of the late Oosmann, passed
out in New York in July 22.
' . Signaller Tom Skeyhill, who will see no
more the beauty of his native Australia, lec-
tures this week in the Little Theatre. He
has a tale of Anzacs to unfold.
Any old linen? Send it to the Town Hall,
where Ella Richards will organise its pas-
sage through the sterilising department.
At the Y.M.C.A. appeal the other night a
concert auction interlarded the music. A
basket of flowers brought a golden harvest
Every bidder’s name was taken down and he
(or she) had to pay up a bob to swell the
tctal. Honorary-manager John Brownlow
had Philip Wilson, Lawrence Campbell, H.
de Robeck, Ella Goodman, and the aesthetic
Archie Ranclaud to help.
Crinolines, long threatened, are hanging
over the community like small meat-cages.
As yet the war-haze has kept the average
woman too chastened in spirit to encourage
the frenzy of hoops. But who can look un-
moved ait the early-Victorian fashions in
t’otherside journals? It only needs an over-
whelming verdict in France to turn Woman
into a sartorial menace to the traffic. And
but yesteryear the hobble-skirt seemed as
though it might have stood against the world
for many ankle-breaking seasons.
The Red Cross pictures at the Piccadilly
drew big audiences. Convalescents at Rand-
wick Hospital and the artificial-limb trying-
on department elsewhere were amongst the
best in a good show. Comforts workers
chortled with glee at the “million garments
sent to the Front” packing depot. Some-
how the jumps had attacked the cinema,
just here, and the frigid girls looked like
marionettes on electric wires dashing
through mountains of softgoods.
Saturday’s orchestral concert in the Con-
servatorium induced a few hundred citizens
to sit indoors on a gorgeous sunlit, after-
noon. Verbrugghen makes his fiddlers
attack the old masters on their feet, so they
had the traffic-policeman’s leg-weary expres-
sion when they’d . finished Beethoven’s long
Symphony No. 2. J. Messeas, on the ’cello,
with the orchestra to supply the high lights,
had to thread his return journey through the
knees of his friends, in order to bob his
head as an answer to clamorous recalls.
Sergeant-Major Will Thompson, who in
peace helps the Solicitor for Railways here-
abouts, is now attached to the Provost-
Marshal’s staff in London. Thompson, who
was wounded in the Gallipoli landing, is
engaged to Dorothy Richards. She’s the
only daughter of Dudley Richards, a well-
known member of the Barnes Operatic Co.,
London.
Yours affectionately.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, July 24, 1910.
My Dear Yarrie, —
To the deplorable “queen” method of rais-
ing hospital funds there is no end. One
reads that “Sol” Green, after a profitable
day at Echuca races, donated £5O to a queen
competition in aid of the local hospital. It’s
to be hoped that Echuca will not drop a
portion of the money won by “queen” votes
in making a costly fiasco of the “crowning”
ceremony, as was done by the St. Vincent’s
amateur show-folk in Melbourne the other
Saturday. The grand climax of the St. Vin-
cent’s excitement was a financial failure
which is said to have eaten up quite a big
slice of the profits. The management, after
spending £3OO in erecting a huge platform
in the wrong place, expected 30,000 people
to come and pay 2s. admission to the Exhibi-
tion Building. But the attendance was not
more than 2000 or so, composed of friends
and adherents of the mostly-disappointed
“queens.” Old experts, who knew what to
expect of the public, had warned Mother
Bectress and her advisers that anything more
than a shilling charge at the turnstiles would
keep the crowd away. But they wouldn’t be
advised. The publicity given to the voting
competition had turned their heads, x'ney
thought the world was dying to see the
crowned queens and courtiers in all their
glory, whereas the world was merely smiling
at the notion of charging two shillings for a
silly sort of entertainment.
The new extension of the Soldiers’ Lounge
on the Lower Esplanade at St. Kilda was
opened on Saturday after. a by Lady
Helen. There was a tremendous crowd, and
L saw her Ex.’s lips movi er tinder a wine-
colored hat. A bouquet of yellow jonquils
clasped devotionally in white-gloved hands
was visible over the shoulders of the multi-
tude. A pair of genthelps supported an
elbow apiece and stamped, I suppose, on the
toes of the foremost gazers. Mrs. Pearce,
very daintily frocked in fawn color, had
her tall Senator to defend her. Mrs.
Hughes, who is fairy godmother to the St.
Kilda warriors, had her General with her,
and he faced the raffle- girls without flinching.
The William Sargoods, who grow mutton
at Narada, back of the You Yangs, are off to
London by the Pacific route (the All Right
route, some call it). Mrs. William is going
to work among wounded soldiers; William,
who is a son of a former Defence Minister,
will offer his services to the British Govern-
ment. The Sargoods usually dwell in Gee-
long, so it will be a change to go and live
in a quiet, unlighted town like London after
being used to a city with electric lights all
down the main street.
Captain Firth, who came to Australia last
January as A.D.C. to your Governor Strick-
land, has gone over to the G.-G. Captain
Haskett-Smith, the G.-G.’s A.D.C., belongs
to the same regiment as Firth—the. Worces-
tershire —and it was a natural thing that,
when opportunity offered, his old pal should
join him in the job of helping Lady Helen
with her bazaar-bargains.
Captain G. G. Anderson, a young Mel-
bourne doctor who joined the R.A.M.C. at
the beginning of the war, has been inva-
lided to Nice after a bout with double
pneumonia. He is shortly to be returned
fo Melbourne and a home-service job.
Captain Greenwood, the spectacular
young man who used to secretary the St.
John Civil Ambulance Association, and who
was one of our amateur theatrical idols, is
off to the fighting ditches. Since the begin-
ning of the war he has had charge of in-
valided soldiers at the central military Rest
Home in St. Ivilda-road. Just after the
G.-G. returned from Westralia he called on
the Captain to say good-bye and congratu-
late him on the Rest Home organisation.
Charlie Strong, a son of the reverend
Peace Society apostle, is off on the Orsova
with three other military vets.
Colonel Vance, the medical commandant
of the Caulfield Military Hospital, has just
had an accident that will lay him aside
for two or three months. The Colonel was
riding a motor-cycle when he became in-
volved with a lamp-post, a motor and a
tram. Result, a fracture above the ankle
and many bruises. Vance is a heavy man,
and from the first had a shy feeling toward
the useful military motor-cycle which has
laid out a number of the military heavy-
weights who have tried it. Dr. Cuscaden,
who commands all the Victorian military
brass plates, says this is. the twelfth leg-
fracture case to the debit of the noisy
machine.
The clan of the McKay, of the Sunshine
Harvester works, wants a lot of beating for
patriotic endeavor. One McKay brother
and 20 nephews and cousins have enlisted.
Whernside, the fine old Albert Miller
home at Toorak, is to be auctioned. It has
acres of park and garden, and these will be
cut up remorselessly into handy suburban
blocks in the same way as the Armytages’
Como estate. The place belonged originally
to knight James Lorimer, who had it built
and planted in his early political days. The
Albert Miller widow has taken to flat life
in the city.
Beatrice Day, in Melbourne for a fort-
night on her way to France, boxed at
“Romance” in the Theatre Royal the other
night. She declares that she has not bought
a new rag since the war, so I presume the
gorgeous black chiffon velvet and silver she
was wearing dated back to 1914. As you’ve
been told already, her Robin has just left
college in Maoriland and departed in khaki
for France.
“Rosna,” writing from London, tells me
that there has just died in Switzerland the
Countess de Salis, who was a La Trobe,
daughter of Vic.’s first Lieut-Governor.
Capt. La Trobe came to Vic. in 1839 as
superintendent of the infant colony, and
brought along his wife and this daughter,
then aged two, so she was one of the State’s
very first assisted immigrants. Perhaps the
very first.
Women boundary-riders are the latest in
Australia, and. Otway Falkiner —one of the
younger Falkiner sons in the Riverina —-
has to be credited with introducing them
at Widgiewa station, which Falkiner. sen.,
bought from the late squatter Horsfall. Mr.
Falkiner coveted Widgiewa for years, but
it was long before the Horsfalls could be
persuaded to part with it. Eventually
Falkiner managed to purchase the place, but
he died before having the joy of personally
taking possession.
Last week a son of the Edmund Jowetts,
whilst flying for his country, was winged by
the Huns and dropped into the enemy’s lines,
llis brother (another flying man) cables
hopefully that the broken machine was seen
to descend fairly slowly, arpl it is thought
that the occupant was not fatally injured.
Charles Dalton, vicar of St. George’s.
Malvern, has heard the call and decided to
go back to England, where he has been
offered a satisfactory stipend at Camden
Town. The departing vicar is a tail, lean,
clean-shaven young fellow of uncertain age,
and has much in common, as to his air and
appearance, with that other well-razored
young Englishman who sometimes comes to
sit under him at St. George’s—Arthur
Stanley, of Government House. Dalton is
extremely High Church, which suits the
influential minority at St. George’s very
well; and lie is an earnest agitator for a
full offertory-bag, which is rather a bore to
the poor rich in these times when one has
to pay one’s gardener as much as 305.-a-
-weelc-and-found or be called a sweater.
Henry Cornish and his wife lately started
out with the idea of motoring into Riverina.
Mrs. Cornish, poor thing, has exhausted her-
self doing Braille work. She has thumped
out so many volumes with the little stilo
that she has developed neuritis in one arm ;
hence the motor jaunt. Mrs. Fred Army-
tage has now presented her with a machine
for doing the work, and she will tackle it
that way on her return. Henry Cornish
himself toils like a warehouse packer for
the Red Cross. He and Billy Williams
have a steady job o’ nights at the Govern-
ment House depot, where they work a big
press between them.
Aunt Adelaide gives the family news: —
Adelaide's severest storm for 20 years fell on it
one evening- last work, arriving from the east —a
quite unexpected quarter. Yet the place is so solidly
built that the damage, done was surprisingly small.
A woman was killed by the falling in of an old
cottage, a horse was electrocuted by a live wire, and
the Botanic Park lost about 80 trees. Apart from
these thing's the worst loss was in the orangeries,
which suffered horribly.
Heinicke’s place as conductor of the Elder Con-
servatorium orchestra is to be taken over by Winsloe
Hall, another member of the staff. He is better
known as a teacher of singing, but had long orches-
tral experience before leaving England, and has
already had charge of the Conservatorium band
during Heinicke’s absence some years ago.
Our recent hurricane was no gentleman. A nice
girl was crossing Rundle-street with her escort when
the whistling scoundrel raced up, ripped off the lady’s
dress and petticoats, and left her looking like a frank
hoarding advertisement. The dazed and blushing
escort promptly enfolded her in his overcoat, and she
ran before the gale into a nearby shop.
Some inter-collegiate football at the Adelaide Oval
and “David Ballard” by the Repertory Theatre at
Walkerville were Saturday’s chief dissipations.
Lady Galway was one of the audience at the Rep.
show. It was Wilfred Neill s last appearance for
an indefinite time in the amateur ranks. He has
enlisted.
Died at North Adelaide on Saturday, 11. D.
O’Halloran, youngest son of the late Major O’Hal-
loran, who was this State’s first Commissioner of
Police.
The reception arranged in the Stricklands’ honor
at Government House on Monday night started the
storm I've told you about. Sheets of galvanised iron,
cocked hats, advertisement hoarding and butlers were
blown around like so much rubbish. Otherwise the
reception proceeded without casualties. On Tuesday
there was a lunch with the Commonwealth Club, and
in the afternoon a visit to the School of Mines,
with President Langdon Bonvthon as pilot.
The sons of the late Captain Hubbe, who was
killed in the Boer War, have both received promo-
tion. One, who left here as a lieutenant, is a cap-
tain, and his elder brother, who went from W.A. as
a private, is now a lieutenant. He was one of t le
few to come seathless through the Gallipoli venture.
Explorer Mawson has been appointed embarkation
staff-officer at Liverpool, with the rank of captain.
Lieut. A. L. Kennedy, who was one of the Mawson
Expedition, and was making a magnetic survey of
Central Australia when war broke out, has been
wounded.
A picture of Theberton Hall, Suffolk, where Colonel
Light spent his boyhood, has been presented to the
Public Library by Mr. J. \V. Bakewell. Incidentally,
our Thebarton was christened after Colonel Light s
home, and the difference in spelling is attributed
by tradition to somebody’s bad writing. Theberton
Hall has so strong a resemblance to Government
House that the chances arc Colonel Light had a hand
in designing the latter.
The Women’s Non-Party Association is appealing
for women to stand for election to the municipal
councils at the end of the year. If I owned a really
impressive hat 1 would try to be Councilloi
Adelaide.
Singer Clytie Hihe, now in England, was the
soprano in “King Olaf’’ at the 105th concert of the
Sheffield Amateur Musical Society, and had a part
in the production of “The Messiah’’ at Hull.
The Gaffney-Cotter engagement will in-
terest Melbourne and Auckland folk. Am-
brose Gaffney, a well-tailored young clubman,
who “represents an English firm” in Mel-
bourne, has induced Winnie, the youngest
Cotter girl, to allow him to “endow" her
“with all his worldly goods." Mind, I’m
only quoting the marriage service, my dear.
As the law stands, a man may give his wife
next to none of those worldly goods and
bequeath her Nothing at All at his death.
No Gentleman One drew up the marriage
service, and it’s quite time the sec. of the
Law Institute looked into that clause which
should deal with the matter. However. I
merely skidded —the paper is so smooth. The
fair Cotter girl is herself well endowed. She is
an Aucklander, one of three sisters, and is
at present staying with mamma in Macleay-
street, Sydney. Papa Tommy Cotter, re-
cently deceased, was formerly City Solicitor
of Auckland and a barristerial Iv.C. (they
are all amalgams over there, you know —
these legal folk). He had the biggest practice
as an advocate in Auckland. The Cotter son
has just got back to Sydney after working
with the Red Cross on an Austrian frontier.
He intends to enlist from his native land.
Ida Valentine, only daughter of Alwyn
Valentine, of the Victorian Railways, is
busy on the trousseau hunt. ~ Her engage-
ment has just been announced to one
Prescott, who came here about two years
ago to advise on electric signalling. Ida
met him on shipboard when she was return-
ing from a schoolgirl “finishing" expedition
to Europe.
On Wednesday this week, at Melbourne
Grammar School Chapel, Dr. Lillie’s girl
Vera was due to marry Billy Blackwell.
Twin-sister Madge was cast for the role of
bridesmaid.
Professor Baldwin Spencer is now grand-
father. daughter Mrs. Young having pre-
sented the major-husband with a baby girl
in Scotland. The soldier-dad is busy
in France. Aunt Aline Spencer is going in
for flying, and is airing the cutest of avia-
tion clothes at Hendon.
At the Beatrice Day farewell benefit oil
Saturday at The Playhouse Lady Helen sat
in one of the latticed boxes behind* a bank
of beribboned almond blossom. Miss
Mandeville, the Tasmanian visitor with the
brilliant complexion, was with her. The
Sandersons filled the next pen with exciting
clothes and diamonds. (The society sheep
wired and ’phoned furiously for these smart
little boxes when they heard that dear Lady
Helen was going.) Lesley Madden arrived
furred to the eyes. Dr. Davenport was in
the circle with his daughter, and the
Dalrymples brought a big party. Statisti-
cian Knibbs held a good strategic position
in the front circle. The MeCubbins were
escorted by son Louis, who was on final
leave.
The programme was laid with three slices
of drama. Beatrice Day, Roy Redgrave
and Eardley Turner played the convincing
one-acter, “Naturalised.” Beatrice herself
appeared as Lady Schultz in golden hair
and mist-blue clothes, and got a tremendous
welcome. Gregan McMahon’s Repertory people
put on another Gertrude Robins playlet called
“Makeshifts,” and the third course was one
act from the dear old “Walls of Jericho.”
The playgoers sat tight after that course
and clamored for more until the Anthem
stood them up and wafted them out of the
theatre. The cast was fine and the work
brilliant. Beatrice Day had her old Brough
part. Beatrice Holloway was an adorable
little sister Lucy in a blue-grey frock. Big
sister had amber-tinted clothes with shoes
and ankles to match. If the same cast liked
to put up the whole of “The Walls,” they
might do a record run at the Playhouse.
It was a quaint idea to run in a sort of
suburban concert between the dramas. The
Magpie Orchestra (average age about 10)
played “selections” in short frocks with long
stockinged legs stowed away under the
chairs. One clever child played the trumpet
part in a “William Tell” outburst. She
looked like a wobbly Futurist sketch when
she got her plump rosy cheeks well inflated
inside its frame of long black hair decorated
on one side with a big bow of black-and-
white ribbon. Mrs. Summerliayes at the
piano wore a comfortable draught-proof
shawl, and daughter Gertrude fiddled furi-
ously with a muscular arm that was quite
bare up to the blue shoulder-straps of her
dress.
Sara Allgood (“Peg o’ My Heart”)
arrived in Melbourne on Friday and revelled
in one off-night in front of the curtain. She
chose “To-night’s the Night” for her holiday
in the manager’s box at Her Majesty’s and
wore a black frock with a black beauty spot
on her chin and a wreath of black velvet
leaves on her hair. Mrs. Frank Tait,
accompanying, wore pale rose pink, and the
attendant actor-men the usual black and
white. By the way, black and white is now
the popular scheme for a background. "To-
night’s the Night” works it into its chief
color schemes.
The Girls Who Stayed at Home are a
patriotic band of clever young amateurs
who give song-and-dance shows round the
subbubs. The money they earn has bene-
fited various patriotic funds, but their chief
objective is comforts for the fighting Aus-
tralian in the trenches. So far they have
made £lOOO out of 26 performances in town
and suburbs, and the country centres have
still to be worked. There are 10 prospective
performances on the engagement book. One is
to be at the St. Kilda-road 5 .M.C.A. “hut. ’
Another, timed for next week, at Brighton
Town Hall, is the final of four in aid of
the Bed Cross. Bumper houses are a habit
with the co. Last Friday they .filled Mal-
vern Town Hall with the suburban peerage,
headed by the Ileunessy dame. Mrs.
McKinley was organiser-in-cliief of the even-
ing. Mrs. Bert Cooke is travelling chap-
erone to the Girls, with whom are two
Geddes maidens, two Hills, Gwyn Watt
(clever enough for two), Lily. Morton
(accompanist), Vera Jackson, Daisy Gove
and Dorothy Maxwell.
Beatrice Day got news whilst in Mel-
bourne of the promotion and special men-
tion of her nephew, young Mapelson. He
ia the son of Dr. Mapelson, a well-known
London man. Beatrice is much related to
the medical profession and was a nurse
before taking to the boards.
Composer Arthur Chanter, bachelor of
music, is one of the few Melbourhe profes-
sionals who believe in getting back to nature.
Chanter does it in the Sandringham. rear
blocks, where he lives in an old-fashioned,
rustic dwelling on a quiet road—where vio-
lets grow under one’s bedroom window and
the magpie chortles in the black-wattle. Lest
he be mistaken for a poultry-farmer, how-
ever, the melody-founder has had a bold
sign planted among the rosemary and lo've-
l’es-bleeding. He and Randolph Bedford, by
the way, once collaborated in an opera and
had a beautiful time preparing it for the
stage; but the cash they didn't get out of it
would fill seven long-felt wants.
Some anonymous railway man donated
a line collection of walking-sticks to
the French Red Cross Fund. A Collins-
street window showed nearly a hundred of
them. They were cut from different Aus-
tralian hardwood and given a high polish by
the men at the Newport workshops. The
same patriots were busy for weeks making
metal and enamel souvenirs, which were
sold on Friday at the Railway stands. The
emblems were made in spare time, and there
were thousands out for sale at a shilling
each —or more if the pretty vendors could
get it. The cutest trifle was a tiny French
.75 gun. But there were also Joan-of-Arc
and Joffre medals, little metal sprigs of
wattle and the sort of flags fairy armies
might fight for.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

the oyster joint.
.[For Thh Bulletin.]
I dreamed last night that I was horn
The Mighty King of the Peppercorn;
I dreamed I was the Cayenne Boss
The Mixed Spice Prince and Savory Joss.
My throne, I thought, on old fish rose,
But never a courtier held his nose ;
None hut I could prolong old steak,
Nor the taint of faded oyster fake.
No pepper grew beyond my State;
The crayfish stank outside my gate;
m But once he passed within my door
I fixed him well and he stank no more.
I held the code of Irish stew,
I mended meat the blow-fly blew ;
The oldest fish I turned to young,
I rescued tripe that time had stung.
And whilst I mused on State affairs,
A herald bellowed up the stairs:
“My liege, my liege, outside your walls
The Dago loud for pepper bawls.
“He offers you a quid a pound!”
“Ask thrice a quid, you snivelling hound,
For pepper is, O crazy loon,
The savior of the Fish Saloon.
“But stay, methinks our magic spice
Should bring to us a higher price;
Begone, and hurry up the job—
Our royal wish is eighty bob.”
And then I heard a Dago cry,
I felt his fork stick in my fry.
He yelled at me, “You want da bed?
Go to the hospital instead.”
He waked me up and broke my dream—
The mighty king and prince supreme
Of pepper goods was counted out
And Dagos saved from a pepper drought.
MAOllILAND. II. B.
* * * *
“Aislynn”: Jimmy Regan leant far back
in his chair and reflected. “There’s a lot in the
superstitions of these Malekula boys,” he said; and
the other traders nodded. “I was managing a place
for Captain Hansen near where Paddy died; and
after the burial I went over to see the tombstone—
a sort of cairn, with Paddy’s skull and shins inside
and a leaf dome with all sorts of offerings under it.
The skull stared uncannily at me through a chink,
and the natives, when they saw me touch the shrine,
fled in terror, leaving me alone in the forest gloom.
I was amazed at the beauty and liberality of the
offerings—ear-rings, nose-rings, armlets of clam-
shell, pearlshell carved and inlaid with tortoiseshell.
JACK’S AS DRY AS HIS MASTER.
BARMAN (to Jack, outside): “What’s your drink, Jack?”
JACK ( hearing loss order “two ales”): “Mine’s two rums, Boss”
A regular treasure-house it was. Then I gave one
shriek and rushed through the bush.” “W-w-vvhy,
what did you see?” gasped our newchuni. “I’ll
leave that for you to think over when I’ve finished
my yarn,” said Jimmy. “When I reached the boys
(and I did the distance in double-quick) I was
shaking and scared looking, but I gave them no
explanation, and to this day none go near the place.”
“But what ivos the” _ “Wait a bit until I’ve
finished; the really uncanny part of the yarn isn’t
reached yet. When I got home I feverishly tore
off every article of clothing and thrust the lot in the
bottom-most compartment of my trunk. Two days
later I left for Sydney on furlough; and whenever
I was in my cabin I felt that old King Paddy, too,
was on his travels. Ugh! It was true, for at
Sydney I found in the box the arm-rings, charms
and nose-rings just as I had seen, them at Point
Harsh, while gazing up at me, with its shin bones
crossed beneath it, was the skull of Paddy.” “But
how do you account” Paddy's eyes twinkled.
“I got seven guineas for the lot at the curio
dealer’s,” he said.
* * * *
“Channel” : There’s a lot of misapprehen-
sion about Corriedales. They have never been fixed
as a distinct type, for the simple reason that they
have been established variously from practically all
the leading British breeds, crossed with merino
ewes and not with Lincolns alone. Nevertheless,
sheepmen who had bred and culled for a few years
claimed them as pure-breds and applied for admis-
sion to the “Flock Book of British Breeds.” After
a painstaking search it was discovered that Maori-
land, where they originated, was not in Great
Britain, so the request was politely refused. Any-
way, as good a sheep in every respect can be pro-
duced by a first cross. It would be interesting to
mix a pen of Corriedales and best-quality first
crosses and get a breeder to pick them out —interest-
ing guess-work. To boil it down, the Corriedales
are only a much-boosted crossbred. (P.S.— I This is
where I apply for a license to carry a revolver.)
* * * *
“Campbell D.” : According to “L.E.” (B.
29/6/’l6), Professor Stirling, of Adelaide Museum,
has discovered boomerangs in Egypt and India, as
well as in Orstralia, and is wondering whether this
can be taken as an indication of some common
ancestry, or, at all events, inter-communication.
Dunno much about ethnology, but I do know that
boomerangs are even at the present time in common
use among the Pueblo Indians of New Mexico
(U.S.A.). These weapons were first referred to in
1539 by one Alvar Nunez Cabeza de Vaca, Esq., a
Spaniard who footed it through the New World for
nine years or so. He says the Pueblos “were armed
with weapons which they threw with astonishing
precision, and killed with them more hares than
they could consume When a hare is seen the
Indians surround and attack him with their clubs,
driving him from one to another till he is killed.”
Although repeating-rifles and six-shooters have re-
placed the boomerang for most purposes, the wooden
sleep-producer is still used whenever a rabbit drive
is held. Does this affect Professor Stirling’s wonder-
ing’s? New Mexico is a good step from India, Egypt
or Australia, and—speaking as one who doesn’t know
much about the subject—it doesn’t look likely to me
that the inhabitants of all four places were ever
nearly related —-at all events, not since Adam gath-
ered his family round him.
“Ela”: The brown brethren who inflict
themselves upon the pleasant land of New Guinea
are a primitive, evil-disposed, treacherous and cruel
type. From birth the young savage is systemati-
cally taught to have no compassion or mercy for the
unfortunate birds and animals that may fall into his
hands. Parrots and cockatoos are kept in nearly
every village for their feathers, and the job of care-
fully plucking the poor birds alive is always handed
over to the young people, who extract much simple
THE SET-OFF.
PIANDFORTE TUNER: “What’s this five bob for? I told you. my charge for tuning was
half a sov.”
C OCKY: “Ay, but yer forgetting mon, ye had yer denner.”
amusement with each feather they tear from their
screeching 1 victims. The docking of dogs’ tails and
the cropping of the dingoes’ ears are other forms
of innocent pleasure to the children. The pig, the
only thing in the whole universe for which the murky
wretches have any respect or affection, also comes in
for his share of torture. Any hog who blunders into
a garden more than once is blinded by having burn-
ing firestieks jambed into his eyes, and his tusks
knocked out as a hint that rooting in the village
compound is not allowed. When hunting wallaby the
men never kill the game outright if jt is possible to
catch it alive. The miserable ’roo when taken has
its legs broken neatly, and is left in agony as long
as Brown Brother pleases. An awful scoundrel
and ex-cannibal friend of mine says that the same
method of “preserving” man is followed in the in-
terior ; and one whitefellow, at least, has had this
awful experience. Werner, the American, who sought
for radium in the mountains behind Goodenough Bay
(Papua), was captured and his legs were fractured so
that his meat would remain fresh for several days
until the guests could arrive for the banquet. In
tribal fights among the sago-eating peoples of the
Gulf of Papua it is considered the essence of bravery
and good manners to bite your captive’s nose off
and cook and eat it before his eyes. This is sup-
posed to give the eater all the virtues of the ex-
owner of the property.
“Flinderino” :, The Fitzroy River crocodile
“Big Ben” measured 23ft. 6in., and, being an out-
size in amphibians, found its way to London. “Hinch-
inbrook” (B. 22/6/’l6) can rest assured that a
25-footer is a record breaker. The Johnstone River
has produced many fine specimens, but only in rare
instances were they over 18-footers. Crocodiles are
slow growers, and are probably a hundred years old
before attaining maturity. One authority allows 10
years for every foot of its length. About the coolest
crocodile-slayer I’ve come across is Jim South, a
young Queensland telegraph operator, once well
known in Normanton and Cairns as “Alligator
Jimmy.” Whilst stationed in the Fai;, North he
made a hobby of collecting crocodiles, using both
rifle and wire-rope traps, and he was out to beat Sid
Robinson’s tally, but an unsympathetic department
intervened by transferring him to Rockhampton,
where the big game is now mostly mosquitoes and
scrub-ticks. I helped Jimmy settle a few crocs,
around Cairns. We used dogs to bait the traps, and
the local papers made a good thing out of Lost,
Stolen or Strayed advts. After a time the supply of
hound gave out, and we used goats instead. There
were so many goats about that the few we took were
never missed.
“Pick”: I learnt the trick by watching
McClung, the packer champion at Connolly’s flour-
mill in Goulburn (N.S.W.). A very long and well-
curved packing-needle is used, and it is cork-
screwed full length, through and over alternately,
along the lips of the bag, the twine being drawn
with one pull at the finish. When the mill was at
high-pressure spilling- flour at the rate of up to 102
bags per hour, McQlung could keep the packer clear,
sew the bags and stack them, all on his lonesome.
Even then he had time now and then to mop his
brow and curse the luck that made him handy with
his fingers instead of with his tongue.
“Nobby”: On the Jilliby Creek, in the
Wyong district (N.S.W.), there is a blue-gum stump
that measures 25ft. 4in. around the top. After
three cuts of sleepers were dug off the tree it mea-
sured 20ft. 3in. Then the party gave up. It was
next tackled by another crowd, who made one cut.
Eighty-seven sleepers were got from this tree, and
enough was left for three more cuts. Timber-getters
find that very large trees don’t pay. There is

doned because it fell across the creek. It measured
90ft. in the barrel, and the average girth was
16ft. 6in. The longest tree that I know of was.
cut for mill logs. It measured 134 ft. to the first
branch, and was a blackbutt.

“\ igilans”: The most intrepid wheel-
barrow shover in the bush was Joe Bagshaw
(“Wheelbarrow Joe”). He had pushed his barrow
over most of Australia before he essayed to navigate
the vehicle from Boulia (Q.) to Victoria Downs
(N.T.). After shoving across the Barkly tableland,
where there are 30-, 40- and 50-mile dry stages, he
reached Newcastle Waters. It was in August, when
there is a 90-mile dry stage on what is known as
the Murangie track. Notwithstanding this Joseph
decided t.o propel his barrow across the semi-desert.
He took water, flour and tea, and started away
merrily with two kerosene tins of liquid and a quart
bag. His remains were found by Walter Rose,
the well-known drover, 10 miles from the water,
which was then only a mud pond, where a howling
mob of dingoes camped beside the carcases of several
bullocks. The spot where Bagshaw died is marked
by the kerosene tins fastened on posts. The Murangie
track from the downs to the overland telegraph line
i 3 looked upon as the “dead finish.” The country
is mostly sand, and water lasts only a few weeks
after heavy rain.
* * * *
“E.S.S.” : A case in which a trapper was
trapped by the rabbits occurred the other day at
Balliang, near Bacchus Marsh (Vic.). J. P. Ryan, one
time professional boxer, put ferrets into a burrow
leading from a dugout he had made, and was
collecting the scared inhabitants when the stampede
loosened a heavy stone at the entrance to the
warren. Then other stones were released, and sud-
denly there was a fall that buried the trapper up
to the neck. He had a greyhound with him, but
the dog didn’t understand anything about going for
help, as dogs do in picture-shows. So there was
nothing to do but study the scenery and wait for
a chance comer. That was on Thursday, and just
when things were becoming monotonous on Sunday
the luck turned, but it took the stranger half an
hour to extricate the prisoner. Geelong Hospital
was the next item on the programme.
“Barcoo”: To-day the up-to-date cattle
ranch in Queensland and the Territory works with
the lasso, and the old and expensive stockyard is
used as firewood. The advantage of the lariat is
unquestionable. All kinds of stock, more especially
cattle, when allowed to roam unfettered by fences,
take to a certain run of country and will stick to it
for years. The head-stockman knows all the resorts,
and when the time comes for branding-up and mark-
ing the young calves he chooses a clear patch of
ground near the watering place of the particular run
of cattle he intends to attack, sticks in a hauling-up
post, collects the animals as they come in to drink,
and moves camp to the next favorable place. The
advantage is obvious. The cattle are not knocked
about as they would be if they were driven to a yard
perhaps 30 miles away and then starved for a couple
of days. Also, the building of cattle-yards on a
large station is a most expensive item. The man
with the new ideas carries his stockyard hung - to the
horn of his Mexican-model saddle. Davenport Downs,
Diamantina (W.Q.), with a herd of 45,000, will
have no yards. The boss learnt his business pro-
perly at Carlton Hills (S. Aus.), where the manager
has, or had, nothing but a horse-yard on the place,
lie had been in the Argentine and Mexico, where a
stockyard could not be found on the outside ranches
in. a radius of 500 miles.

“D.F.”: About eagles’ wings and
“A.A.B.’s” doubts (B. 6/7/T6). He says that Dr.
Leach, an eminent authority, asserts that there is no
authentic record of an eagle having shown a larger
spread than 7ft., and asks for undoubted evidence of
one having been found with a wing-spread of Bft.
or over. All I can say is that when the tape was
put over an eagle my brother snared in the Sorell
(Tas.) district it went Bft. 6in. from tip to tip.
But if “undoubted evidence” means the bird, I’m
sorry, but it’s past producing now.
**■ * *
“Bex”: Spider-web for wounds isn’t the
only silly superstition that dies a hard death. Axle-
grease is also recommended by the bush faculty, and
not long since I met a trusting brother making for a
doctor 100 miles away with a poisoned arm due to
using the alleged specific. Two years ago another
goat tried the same cure. Nan is now a widow.
* * * *
“Te Pana”: The black and white snake
found hereabouts (Tonga), althpugh harmless, has
the poison fangs well developed and furnished with
hollow tubes, which, instead of leading into sacs, ter-
minate in the jawbone. In the hazy past when the
saurians disported in the mud of Atlantis this chap
might have been equipped with a potent defence.
Either that or he is not done with evolution. The
young are reared in rock crevices on the beach. I
have met more in the sea than ashore. Their move-
ments in water are slow, although they drag their
six oi- seven feet of length through the bush at the
regulation gait.
* * * *
“Nomad”: “X.Y.” was a bit wide of the
mark in his recent explanation of the poisonous
character of Johnson grass when eaten green by
stock. His theory, if correct, would apply to most
green plants, as they all contain starch cells in
greater or less number. The fact is that Johnson
grass is a species of sorghum, and like all members
of this genus contains a cyanoge'netie glucoside; in
other words, a substance which is capable of produc-
ing prussic acid under the action of the digestive
juices. When the plant is young the glucoside is
present in large quantities, but it gradually dis-
appears, until, after the vegetable flowers, it has
practically all gone. You can feed at this stage with
safety. Nitrous ether in small doses, either intro-
duced per mouth (internally) or per skin (hypo-
dermic injection), is a sure cure if given not more
than an hour after the sorghum in its poisonous
stage is taken. There is no excuse for growing John-
son grass now that its near relation Sudan grass
can be obtained. This latter is also one of the
sorghum tribe, being a cross between Johnson grass
and amber-cane sorghum, with all the advantages of
the Johnson, but without the creeping underground
stems which render it so difficult to eradicate. For
the benefit of those who have the Johnson and wish
to get rid of it by some other means than the back-
breaking method of grubbing it out, it is said that
if one has the patience to allow the stems to flower
and then cut them the plant will weaken and die.
“Beeritus”: As one of the bloques referred
to by “Yancy” (B. 22/6/16) who throw colts for
castration purposes, I would, with all humility, beg
to disagree with him. A colt .standing on its feet is
in absolutely the wrong position. I will go further
than that, and challenge “Yancy” or any other vet.
or quack to perform a successful operation on a colt
in that position. As for the mask, I’ll admit that
it has its uses for saddling or handling a bad
animal, but it won’t prevent it from kicking your
head off if you take undue familiarities with the
hinder parts. Peter Rankine got his brains strewn
over the rails of the stockyard through trusting a
mule in that position, and Peter knew horses better
than most men know their wives. Also, let me say
that the emasculation cannot be done in a jiffy, as
stated by “Yancy,” even with the latest and most
up-to-date instrument—that is, if you don’t want a
dead colt on your hands. On further consideration, I
think “Yancy” must have been referring to dead
colts.
The brown brethren at Petane settlement,
Hawkes Bay (Petane is Maorified Bethany), unveiled
a fine monument the other day and wired into roast
porker in honor of one of the rude forefathers of the
hamlet, Henare Pohio. When the proper incan-
tations had been made the tribe selected a man whom
they christened Kohatu, meaning “stone," to
represent the tomb of deceased and to go on tour
from kainga to kainga “carrying the tears,” as John
Maori puts it. Wherever Kohatu goes he will be the
object upon which the tribespeople will expend their
keenings. Incidentally, he will gather in, as per
custom, gifts of sorrow galore, greenstones and
mats, five-pound notes and fat pigs, ponies and pipit
and maybe a bouncing new wahine or two. The job
has some drawbacks, for a bush tangihanga in full
blast is something to avoid, but it has its solid com-
pensations, and it means a good 12 months’ fat living
at the very least. Mr. “Stone” of Bethany, taking
it all round, is on a good foundation.
•*■ ***
“Barcoo”: “Quandong” (B. 15/6/T6) is
barking up the wrong tree when he condemns electric
shearing plants. Similar objections were loud in the
land when Wolseley machines were installed for the
first time on Dunlop (N.S.AV.), and although the
invention was going to make the barber’s cheque 50
per cent, easier to get, and increase his daily average
by from 40 to 50 sheep, his bleating was loudest. The
proof that electric gear is the thing and has come to
stay is that in nearly every instance in Queensland
where new gear is installed it is electric. The sav-
ing in lubricant alone for a 50-stand shed for the
headgear is not to be sneezed at, to say nothing of
the wearing apparel destroyed by the dripping from
the headgear when the temperature is high and the
oil thin. Electrics require no headgear and no oil,
nor a man with a can busy an hour before starting.
At Clarke and Whiting’s Isis Downs (C.Q.), 50
electrics have been running for three years, and if
“Quandong” wishes to see the wool peeled off a
quarter of a million monkeys in less than eight
weeks, just wire me and I’ll guarantee him a stand.
He will also see the best shearers in Australia.
There are nine million sheep in the two adjoining
districts, and English, French, Japanese and American
buyers plump for it as the world’s best-treated wool.
* * * *
“Bex”: The Jensen (hot water) method
of treating grain for smut as advocated by “X.Y.”
may be all right in theory, but it is not practicable
for the man on the land. The efficacy of the treat-
ment depends on the water being maintained at a
temperature between 130 and 134 deg. F. (below
130 deg. will not kill the burnt spores, and above 134
will kill the wheat germ), and there are very few
farms tllat have the necessary conveniences to keep
the water within this very narrow range of tem-
perature. Smut, or more correctly speaking bunt,
can be readily controlled by either bluestone or
formalin, but, as the latter is more subject to adul-
teration (it is on record that formalin sold in
Victoria one year contained only 2 per cent, for-
maldehyde instead of 40 per cent, required in com-
mercial formalin), first place must be given to blue-
USED TO WILD LIFE.
LONDON C ITIZEN: “I suppose, note, you
would be accustomed to dealing with
wild characters at home?"
ANZAG: “My word! The boss in our office
used to be something awful if you
turned up a bit late in the morning."
stone (copper sulphate). In using this a 1 per
cent, solution of copper sulphate (lib. bluestone to
10 gals, water) is effective, provided care be taken
to see that every grain is wetted. There are pick-
ling machines on the market, but the price is high
for the use they get. The method generally
employed is to immerse about a bushel of wheat
for a few minutes in the solution, which should be
in a wooden or copper vessel, and well shaken to
ensure wetting every grain. Grain will give a
better germination if pickled with bluestone some
time before sowing. Should the seed be badly
affected with bunt the strength of the solution may
be increased to 1J per cent. (111 b. bluestone to 10
gals, water).
THE CELESTIAL CAB.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The streets were full of dancing stars;
The buildings skipped by in a row;
I hailed, at times, some passing bars
And drank a hundred drinks or so.
I had no need to be conveyed ;
No weariness was in my feet;
The world changed for me, while I strayed,
From Martin Place to Oxford-street.
For cabs I, therefore, had no need;
But in a cab I chanced to see
I climbed, with crays on which to feed,
Because I thought it wanted me.
The horse sprang upward with a bound;
The cab soared high as if on wings ;
From far above the common ground
I sprayed the world with claws and things.
Our flight the sleepy stars awoke;
The wheels in silver circles twirled ;
And, while the scarlet shells I broke,
We travelled twelve times round the world.
But ere the thirteenth lap began
I heard a husky word or so,
And, looking upward, saw a man
Who asked me gruffly where to go.
I said he might, perhaps, try Spain,
Although I did not care, at all;
But speed I urged him to maintain
Lest, losing leeway, we should fall.
Yet he would argue from aloft
On matters that had little worth,
Till, with a jolting far from soft,
We landed, somewhere, on the earth.
Then demons in my vision leapt
Who talked of cash. As mine was gone
I turned my face away and slept,
And while I snored they argued on.
This morn, upon my bed I woke
With cray-claws braided in my hair,
And vague ideas that someone spoke
Hot words about a paltry farel
N.s.W.

to supply another £5OOO. On the old
£15.000. the profit is now at the rate
of 30 p.c. p.a.; and practically there is no
risk of principal —all but a very few pounds
of capital and reserves are in public securi-
ties. Certainly the co. might be compelled
to pay up if it did something very wrong in
connection with a trust estate ; but when in
doubt it can almost always protect itself
by asking either the Court or the interested
parties for directions. And, though it is
quite possible that trustee cos. may have to
do more for their living by-an'd-bye, it seems
almost certain that their business will grow.
There is every reason, too, why it should
grow, while the business of the inexpei .
unprofessional, unsecured and unguaranteed
private executor and trustee dwindles.
**. * *
“D.F.” on the Tasmanian hydro-power
schemes
“Buckley’s” remarks on the water-power of the
Tasmanian Great Lake deserve attention. He is
right in saying that some wild nonsense has been
talked about the rainfall of the Great Lake region.
There has never been more than one rain-gauge on
the drainage area of the lake or close handy to it;
that one is kept where the Shannon runs out of the
lake. Its record averages about 30in. a year, or a
good way below the 45in. “Buckley” allows. But
there is good reason to suppose it rains more on the
western side of the lake and at the northern end than
it does at the southern end, though nobody knows
how much more, and all the statements made about it
are mere guesses. A good deal of the precipitation, by
the way, is in the form of snow, and maybe the lain-
fall records miss a bit of it. They are now doing
what should have been done long ago, putting rain-
gauges around the lake to try to find out what the
fall really is. But while it has yet to be proved
whether the lake is good for 75,000 h.p., enough has
been done to show that it is equal to a lot more
than the 16,400 e.h.p. which “Buckley’s” formula
would give it. A dam very soon raised the level of
the lake by lift., which means pounding up a big
body of water, seeing that the original area of the
lake was 42 square miles, now increased to nearly
50. And the water it takes to run the present
10,000-h.p. plant is only a fraction of what is avail-
able. Personally I think that to get the 75,000 h.p.
the lake’s catchment area would have to be increased
by the diversion ot' the Upper Ouse, but that would
be comparatively easy. The Lake Margaret scheme
gives a line on the possibilities ot the Great Lake.
There they have been delivering 5000-6000 h.p. for
about two years with a catchment area of only eight
square miles and a lake of 370 acres. The fall and
the head of water at the power station are very much
the same as at the Great Lake, but the rainfall at
Lake Margaret is about 150 in. per year. It leads the
Great Lake there ; but its catchment area is only about
l-25th of the other’s, and its reservoir is not l-50tli
the size of the Great Lake, though it’s a lot deeper.
On the face of it, the big scheme ought to be
good for 35,000 or 40,000 h.p. without the Ouse, and
it is reckoned the Ouse would double it, though
that’s mostly guesswork so far.
* * * *
“E.D.”: Senator Pearce has been prevailed
upon to announce that the Commonwealth is
short of metallurgists. As a matter of fact,
for years past the profession has been so
overcrowded that most Schools of Mines
graduates fail to obtain a job in the line
for which they were trained. On Kal-
goorlie’s Golden Mile alone, over 50 qualified
chemists are doing pick and shovel work,
while in Perth and other Westralian centres
dozens are serving behind counters or keep-
ing books in commercial establishments. The
same story could be told of the eastern
States. The trouble with the profession is
that ever since Broken Hill and Kalgoorlie
became great metal-producing centres, Aus-
tralian schools have turned out too many
assayers, etc., while a good many have
flocked hither from other parts of the world.
An announcement siich as that just made is
a fine thing for slackers, and I hope the
Minister won’t make any more of the same
kind about other professions, trades, or call-
ings without thorough inquiry.
Nowadays the doings of the Sand Queen
(W.A.) is wrapped in mystery. For-
merly the management used to publish pro-
gress reports, announce monthly returns
and advertise dividends. Even its half-
yearly meetings, attended by two or three
of the controllers and the same number of
dummies, used to be mentioned in the
papers. Now’ nothing concerning' it is
allowed to leak out. One explanation given
is that the reef lias gone w’rong at depth, but
probably the silence is due to the fact that
in the West it pays best for a mining co.
doing well to keep its mouth shut.
Leonora (W.A.) has regained some of its
old-time activity. Several new cos. have
recently settled in the district, and all reckon
that they are going to do well. African Ex-
ploration Syndicate is at work on the Auck-
land lode, from which the previous owners
are reputed to have got about £2500 worth
of gold. The Melbourne-financed Gold Blocks
Co. is developing a body which is declared to
average well over 80s. per ton; a Perth
crowd is toying with the Harbor Lights;
and the London-controlled Gwalia Central
Corporation never misses a week without
finding something. Yet, in spite of all this,
the Sons of Gwalia went, elsewhere to look
for a new property; and all that co. doesn’t
know about the field isn’t worth much. The
once live, but now almost dead, centres of
Kookynie and Niagara are also getting a
little new capital. The flotation of the
McTavisli is being engineered in Melbourne,
the disabled Butterfly is tipped to be on the
wing again shortly, and some of the leases
that the old Cosmopolitan Co. abandoned are
being equipped. Another show which settled
the London Orion Co. is also being spoken of
.as a likely thing, but it has already adminis-
tered one lesson to speculators.
The winter is no friend of old-age pen-
sioners, and in June 920 of them went to a
place where pensions are not paid. But 813
others got old enough to qualify for the
dole.’ so there wasn’t much difference in the
total. The Commonwealth’s babies are get-
ting less numerous, or else people don’t want
the bonus (which latter isn’t likely), for
there were only 133,430 allowances granted
for the financial year ended on June 30,
while for the year before there were 138,188.

Times (N.S.W.)
MUNICIPALITY OF NORTH ILLAWARRA.
Caretaker and Librarian.
APPLICATIONS are invited from persons, married,
for the position of Caretaker and Librarian at the
Council Chambers, Fairy Meadow. Salary £5 per
annum and residence. Preference will be given
returned soldiers.
But I think they ought to limit it to V.C.’s.
A Melbourne inspector asked a suspected
milk-vendor for a sample of his goods, and
he curtly refused to supply one. For this
pleasantry the law provides a penalty of
“not more than £20,” but it says nothing
about “not less than,” so when the hono-
rariums were asked to fix the amount they
said “One shilling, without costs.” The
local Council failed to appreciate this jest
and asked the Attorney-General to treat the
matter seriously. But Lawson regretted
that he couldn’t. If the penalty had been too
severe he could have lowered it or remitted
it altogether, but he couldn’t raise it one
bean. All he could do was to promise the
honorariums something if they acted in that
fashion again. Which is quite satisfactory
to the ‘ Milky-ohi
“Bram”: The first recorded lajbor organ-
isation in Australia was foaled in 1837, when
some seamen and rouseaboufs in Sydney
demanded an increase of Is.'per day 6n the
3s. they were being paid. The shipowners
at once issued a manifesto in which it was
proclaimed that “the demand for increase of
wages does not arise from scarcity of seamen
or laborers or from inadequacy of wages,”
but had been made by “a systematic, organ-
ised body, whose intentions are not yet fully
developed, but whose object, if accomplished,
would materially retard the progressive ad-
vancement of our colonial marine.” A strike
followed and vessels were detained for some
days. This was Australia’s first strike
between organised parties. Last year there
were 358, of which N.S.W. enjoyed 272. It
is the March of Progress—or something
equally riotous.
The yell against “Germans” being allowed
to do this or that in Australia is damaged,
for practical purposes, by the absence o
any definition of how long a person o
German descent is to have it counted agams
him. The rough-and-ready general rule is to
go by the name —which means that the
descendants in the male line remain Germain
for ever, while the children of the daughters
are in one generation above suspicion. >' 11-
son, Labor M.L.C. of S. Aus. has at least
given the Port Adelaide AH British
League something definite to bark over. 1 e
would have “all persons of German birth and
parentage, to the third generation of Ai -
tralian born, disallowed ownership of land in
Australia.” Wilson’s rule, besides making it
extreme]v uncomfortable for ‘the Grown,
would disqualify many who have actually
gone to the Front on our side, particularly ni
his own State. The German influx to S.
Aus. was strongest between 1840 and loou.
The youngster who came then had a family
in the ’oo’s. The first generation of Aus-
tralian-born may have kept up the old tradi-
tion, but tlie second generation is generally
sound Australian, and the case for the third
is stronger still.

“Haka” : The fact that the Maori and the
pakelia are both natives of Maoriland is
causing the Frenchman more worry than the
war. On a recent occasion Henare cleared
the atmosphere a little with the following
explanation: “Py korry, tat te easy—te
pakelia porn in te day time ; te night I porn
it te plurry well pitch dark.”
“Alpha” : I can take “TAXI.” to one more
of the eight church bells that were presented
to St. Philip’s, Sydney, by George 111. in
1807. It is hung in St. Matthias’s Church
of England, Bombala (N.S.W.), and is call-
ing the good girls and boys to choir practice
as I write this. The oldest _ inhabitant
doesn’t know when St. Matthias’s was first
opened, but it must have been close on 60
years ago. When I first struck Bombala,
36 years since, the bell was in the place it
occupies to-day, and old records state that
when St. Philip’s got its new peal one of
the original eight was sent here. It still
retains its clear, mellow tone, and on a calm
evening one has difficulty in not hearing it
at a distance of. over three miles.
The good old conservative Hobart Mer-
cury is burning all its old josses. The other
morning it lighted on to the evils of land
monopoly at the following high rate of
speed:—
In point of fuel, English constitutional usage is
against absolute property in land itself. Land and
its minerals are possessed subject to the prior rights
of the community, which must not be imperilled by
any pedanticism in this or any other matter.
And now what has the Mercury got to say
why the sauce for the goose in Britain isn’t
sauce for the gosling in Australia?
