POLITICAL
POINTS
Applications for the War Loan must be
in by August 1, so there is no time to
waste. Forms may be got at any bank,
savings bank or post office. Any amount
may be subscribed, from £5 to £50,000,000
Treasurer Higgs doesn’t want more than
that right away. But he wants and every
other patriotic Australian wants a response
so large that it will not only pay the bills
but do two other things: cheer up our lads
at the Front and give the enemy a still
more uneasy feeling in his confidence. In
this connection The Bulletin recalls what
a young officer wrote to it from Egypt the
week after the news of the very fine result
of the last loan had reached the troops.
“Isn’t it magnificent!” he said. “That
bucked up the men more than anything else
that has happened for months past. It
would have done the subscribers good if they
HIS LITTLE BIT.
Business houses are encouraging employees to sub-
scribe to the War Loan by small weekly payments.
BILLO: “ What’s Stinker putting on dorg
for, Peeler ?”
PEELER: “Oh, he's applying for ten quids'
worth of War Loan."
could have heard the cheers there were in
camp as the news was passed along.” There
is, thus, more than the money value at
stake. There is the moral effect on both
sides. And there is per cent, interest.
Ten per cent, of the amount Subscribed for
is payable with the application, and the
balance by instalments running to the
yiiddle of next year.
Employees of the N.S.W. railway service
having invented and manufactured substi-
tutes for 20 different German patents used
in locomotive construction, the Freetrade
Sydney Telegraph cheerfully remarks that
“our mechanics can beat the Germans with
their own industrial weapons,” and that “if
the conditions have not been equal in recent
times we have only ourselves to blame.” Of
course we have. What has happened lately
is that Australian mechanics, working
under a forced Protection, have done what
the Telegraph has for years yelled that it
was an economic offence not to encourage
the foreigner to do. After the Telegraph
has finished blaming itself for having been
a wooden-headed obstacle in the way of
Australian industry, the next bgst thing it
can do is to start being an agitator for a
tariff that will compel Australians to make
a lot more ironmongery, exclusive of cranes
for lifting boxes out of steamers. The war
is not going to protect us from Germany
for ever, and, anyhow, other foreigners are
getting busy.
The monster petition of women organised
by Lady Leith and Viscountess Temple-
town, imploring Hughes to “come back
without a moment’s delay,” is touching evi-
dence of the impression made by William on
the great heart of the British—er —worker.
Not, of course, that the Lady works in a
jam factory; nor does the Viscountess toil
like Doreen pastin’ labels in a pickle joint.-
Lady Forbes-Leith inhabits Castle Fyvie,
in Scotland; also Clashnadarroch Lodge,
near Aberdeen; likewise 1 Grosvenor
Crescent, London. She is a Yank and
married her Baron when he was running
a steel business the U.S.A. In his youth
the Baron, son of a British admiral, was
in the navy, and served in Maoriland against
Henare in the ’GO’s. Viscountess Temple-
town, the other worker to whom William
lias so powerfully appealed, is a sister
of the tenth Earl of Winchelsea and Not-
tingham. The lady also has a castle —in the
County of Antrim —and her husband owns
about 23.000 acres of the Distressful Isle.
Certain workers (in moderation) are
demanding (1) high wages, (2) cheap living
and (3) low production. It can’t be done.
They can have high wages and low produc-
tion; but that means dear living. Or they
can have cheap living and low production;
but that involves low wages. Or they can
have high wages and cheap living; but that
demands high production. What they can’t
have is high wages, cheap living and low
production. If they want high wages and
cheap living there is only one possible way
of getting it —by increasing production and
seeing that no parasite has a bite of the
product.
N. S. Wales Railway Commissioner
Fraser has just presented some more of the
fruits of pouring out borrowed money on
public works when money is dearest and
wuges highest because the cost of living has
climbed to the top of Kosciusko. The rail-
ways don’t pay, he told a deputation the
other afternoon, and he didn’t _ think they
would ever pay again. It doesn’t matter so
much if ordinary working exes, run up.
because they will adjust themselves to any
future change of conditions; but to spend
dear money and expensive labor on per-
manent works means that the costs go up
for all time.
Jack Scaddan (W.A.) is to be shorn of
his Premiership as soon as the House meets
—apparently on a “sudden death” motion,
such as time-saving Charley Kingston intro-
duced to S. Australian politics. But the
wind has been tempered to the shorn goat.
.Tack has won the Labor nomination for his
old seat.
The All-British League in Adelaide has
had a wave of sanity. It begins to realise
that, while everybody agrees with its fren-
zied claim that “Germans” shall not hold
positions of trust in Australia, nobody is
able to say which of the many Australians
who have inherited German names ought to
come under the axe. So it has asked the
Military to publish name, occupation and
address of every enemy subject interned
in S. Aus. up to date. It says it wants
to know who have been proved disloyal; and
that sudden waft of business-like behavior
comes as a blessed change after much wild
flag and chin wagging.
Triumphant shout of a Holmanite during
the opening debate in Ma’s Assembly: —
During the six years Labor has been in power it
has been responsible for more developmental work
and consequent material prosperity than its opponents
in twice that period.
And this boastful claim that it has been twice
as often to the pawnshop as anybody else is
advanced as Labor’s chiefest glory !
Maoriland is leaving nothing to chance in
her hour of magnificent cadge from John
Bull. In order that the hearts of all the
native Imperialists may beat as one and
entertain but a single thought—the achieve-
ment of the next loan —the Dry Hash Gov-
ernment proposes to adjourn local elections
for a year. This is part of a War Precau-
tions Bill. But why this anxiety for the
local Bumble and the County Councillor at
Kapaiwaipirowiro? It looks as if someone
heard an axe on the way to the grindstone.
Buzacott, seconding the Address-in-Reply
nonsense in the N. S. Wales Leg. Chamber:
He did not think the Government would be so
reckless as to send any women to the Council unless
the people had a chance of showing their views
on the matter by electing women to the Lower House.
But if the people will elect women to the
Assembly, the Council is no place for them.
The rule is to send up there only people
whom the people don’t want. And, so long
as there is a Second Chamber, it’s a very
sensible rule, too. For what wouffi be the
use of sending the same sort of peop
both Houses, one to be merely the echo ot
the other?
POLITICAL ECONOMY.
“What’s this stinking fund they fork erbout
in Parliament, ’Erbit?”
“I dunno. S’pose it’s the money the mem-
bers make out of land deals.”
Cold Tea and Bung—especially Cold Tea,
for Bung feels a sinking pain in the stomach
already—are preparing for a mighty fight
over six o’clock in Vic. as soon as the Refer-
endum Bill is passed. The Anglican Bishops
and the Scotchbyterian Moderator have
issued circulars to the faithful telling them
to go bald-headed for six, and most of the
other churches except the R.C.’s are getting
into line. As to the R.C.’s, the Sacred
Heart temperance crusade is going strong,
but the church as a whole is standing pat.
Premier Peacock is in boiling water with
the National Council of Women. It wanted
to send him a deputation, but he said he
was only receiving one from each side on
the liquor question and he had already had
’em both. A few days later he received one
from the Clubs, and now the N.C.W. says
this shows a scandalous leaning to the side
of Beer.
The Vic. Housing Commission has re-
ported unfavorably on “pocket-handkerchief
allotments.” If such reports usually led to
legislative business, which they don’t, re-
formers would have reason to rejoice, for
there is a mild land boom now proceeding
in Melbourne’s newer suburbs, and the
cramped allotment is a common article of
sale, generally without any serious restric-
tion on the part of the municipal authorities.
Naturally there is a reason for that. So
many municipal councillors are land and
estate agents and dabblers in property that
interference would be equivalent to hari-
kari. And the evil will last so long as ever
municipal councils represent property in-
stead of people.
“Carfax” on the great shirk: —
The Commonwealth Government will appoint a
journalist to accompany the Uncle Sam Inspection
Commission, “making the selection from three nomi-
nations to be made by the A.J.A.” Does the Govern-
ment know what it is doing? The council of the
A.J.A. consists of half a dozen Melbourne reporters.
They may know something, of the capacity of Aus-
tralian journalists whom they have never met and
whose work they never see. But it is not likely.
In any case, these men are not on the council of the
A.J.A. because they have any critical faculty. Sup-
posing The Bulletin, or the Age or the Telegraph
or the Worker wanted to send a man to wring copy
out of Yankeeland or Darwin or Canberra or Hughes,
would any of them consider the view of the A.J.A.
worth 2d. ? The Government should make the appoint-
ment and take the responsibility.
In the Speck the row between Hobart and
Launceston is obscuring the small disturb-
ance reported to be in progress in Europe.
The combatants are having a preliminary
trial of strength over the Northern demand
that the head centre of railway administra-
ticfn should be moved to Launceston.
Hobart is kicking fiercely, and ex-Minister
Fitzgerald has mildly
informed Ministers
that the Launceston
people are actuated
by motives of callous
selfishness and in-
satiable greed, but-
tressed by unparal-
leled audacity. He
asked Ministers to
squelch the whole
agitation by a
straight-out answer;
but Premier Lee,
who happens to re-
present a Northern
c o n s t i tuency, de-
clined to commit sui-
cide in such an ob-
vious way. The
general feeling, both
North and South, is
that if the railway
administration goes
north, quite as good
a case can be made
out for shifting
Mines, Education
and a few more.
Peacock has put
forward a plaintive
excuse for offering
to take a referendum
on the early-closing
question, declaring
that “Mr. Elmslie
would have delighted
in pulling feathers
out of Peacock’s tail
if the Ministry had
not decided to fol-
low the example of
S.A. and Tasmania.”
But if Peacock does
things because other
States do them, why
doesn’t he join m
the procession be-
hind the same two
States and offer Vie-
tori a an honest Totalisator Bill?
On the futile Address-in-Reply Hannah,
M.L.A., spent several hours urging on the
disinterested Peacock Government the crying
need for immediate Protection. If he really
intended more than an explosion of hot air.
why didn’t Hannah address his remarks to
the P.L.C.? That body could put consider-
able weight behind a Government which,
judging by recent movements, will still need
a lot of pushing before it provides the pro-
mised tariff.
Australian timber got a fine advertisement
in London the other week, and the Vic-
torian Agent-General’s office got another. A
presentation table made of choice Australian
woods was forwarded to a former G.-G..
Denman, by the committee of the V.R.C.
That was long ago, but only nbw has his
Latex, acknowledged receipt. He explains
that he would have done it before, _ but the
table had only just arrived, having lain
unnoticed in the Agent-General’s office for
more than a year.

THE DIFFERENCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Our fleet had to be built with one eye on English
polities. —Von Hit low.
We watched the movements of their stalwart jaws
Where Cobden’s banner fluttered on the breeze —
The men who toiled at making British laws
To keep their fcieign trade upon the seas.
We watched for chances, and our navy grew;
Between the talk and deed we still got through.
Laughter was ours. The game was good to play.
Our hammers thundered on the glowing steel.
At night we raised our glasses to “The Day,”
And dreamt of larger gun and stouter keel.
The future must be sure. The British style
Was loud and bland and wholly free from guile.
The day arrived. Our watching all was done.
Familiar British antics still were there;
But with the red flare flaming from the gun
We found our eyes apd thoughts were otherwhere.
We learned, alas, the difference at sight
Between a gunner and a Cobdenite.
JT.S.W

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The poor old Public was saved consider-
able cash when the owner of Uxbridge took that
popular hurdler out of the Australian Steeplechase,
leaving him in the companion event. Last year, after
winning the Australian Hurdle Race for the second
time, Uxbridge looked very likely to win the Steeple
until he made a blunder. * This year he was thought
to be going to have another try at the bigger
jumps. The theory that a clever hurdle-racer can
manage the moderately stiff fences at Caulfield still
holds good, and Uxbridge was the confidential tip of
all the hard-faced men in the street. Moreover, the
handicapper had been blamed for “chucking” the old
chap into the Steeple. By scratching Uxbridge three
weeks before the four-miles race came due, owner
Moffatt did Ikey Mo a bad turn —thank the Lord.
The scratching of baronet Cooper’s im-
ported horse Redfern drew salt tears from any num-
ber of early speculators on the Epsom and Metro-
politan double. One big punter, usually well-armed
with information, coupled Redfern with William the
Silent to win fully £IO,OOO. The owner has stated
that Redfern will run only in weight-for-age events.
Without that explanation it might have seemed
strange to* find the beast entered for any of the big
handicaps. During the past fortnight some person
made a desperate effort to back Redfern straight-out
for the Epsom, but found that the books had the
bar up. The incident made some people wonder
whether, if a good parcel had been secured, Redfern
woyld have considered a rich handicap beneath his
dig. Anyway, Redfern has so far shown no glimpse
of his English form.
It has come to pass right enough, and
deep is the woe in Israel. The punters fell on Ikey
once more at Rosehill (Sydney) on Saturday, and,
getting him down, tore cash out of the bag in big,
thick wads. Three favorites and three second
favorites captured the six races. At a place like
Rosehill this spells disaster to Ike, for on these
suburban courses betting is always concentratecf. It
is now odds-on that after these three calamitous
Saturdays there will be a tightening up of the alleged
odds and a yearning amongst the tribe of Bar Von
for a holiday until there is something doing at Rand-
wick. Should the public be in good form at that
burial-ground of blasted hopes, the wail of Abraham
will be as that of a thousand souls outside a Sydney
pub on a hot day when the clock strikes six and an
anguished Bung puts up the shutters.
Rosehill horse-trainer W. Booth seldom
makes a mistake when he places the “out-to-win”
ticket on one of his nags. His gelding Boureong was
always favorite for the Flying on Saturday, and
landed the goods from the consistent Hopfield. Horse-
flesh which cannot pay for its feed seldom stays in
Booth’s stable, which this season has, perhaps, turned
out more winners than any other, thanks mainly to
the early deeds of Eulacre and Dame Acre. But
Boureong had a close shave. Black had the mount
on Hopfield, who has usually been decorated by the
apnrentice Crockett. Seven pounds less meat on the
saddle would have made a lot of difference to the
Malster horse, who was meeting Boureong on 61b.
worse terms than when he outed Booth’s gelding at
Randwiek last month.
* *. * *
During the 1915-16 racing season £203.000
has been distributed in the way of stake-money by
the various race clubs in the Svdnev metropolitan
area. The A.J.C. handed out £93,000. Of course,
we will pay Cohen sometime or other, but it doesn’t
look as if we are trying very hard to do without
him. AVe might at least have a Boomanbust Handi-
cap to remind us of what’s coming.
* * * *
This continued success of imported nags
in Sydney ought to be inquired into. Corrie,
a mare from Maoriland, is the latest offender,
having appropriated the Hurdles at Rosehill on
Saturday. The mare showed great improvement on
her form at Canterbury Park three weeks earlier. _ A
consoling point is that on Saturday she was favorite.
Horses from M.L. are beginning to wander over this
way in larger numbers than usual, and by the time
the Spring Meeting arrives there should be a big
team on the premises. Some of the prads already
on the continent are well forward, and it is more
than likely that other events may fall to them
shortly. Strange to say. no imported horse managed
to win at Rosehill, although Medley very nearly
succeeded in keening up the average. Gadabout, for
which 1500 guineas was paid in the Cold Country,
was a starter in the mile and a quarter handicap, and
was fourth.
* * » *
“Ambler”: The bowl about the injury
that will be done to the breeding of utility horses if
Victorian Chief Sec. McLeod persists in his intention
to reduce the number of trotting meetings in the
year from 20 to 10 may have something in it so far
as the square-gaited trotter is concerned, though even
that much is problematical. But it is a certainty
that as applied to the pacer the howl is just a howl.
The pacer is no use outside a racing track and no
ornament anywhere. Probably 75 per cent, of the
nags started in the Victorian “red-hots” are pacers—-
artificially-,gaited brutes which have to be tied up
with complicated harness and ironmongery in order
to keep them on their feet. Not onlv are they of no
utility but they are a positive danger to people
unaccustomed to their charming habits, for unless
going at top they are always likely to come down.
How much utilitv is there about a beast which can
only be driven like Hades in a lot of hardware for
two or three miles and then has to be spelled for the
rest of the day?
* * * i *
' It is stated that Carbine’s bones have been
set up by Rowland AVard, the famous London taxi-
dermist, and are to be presented to this continent
by the Duke of Portland. Another nag whose bones
went back to the country that had witnessed his
triumphs on the track was the mighty Ormonde, per-
haps the greatest of all racehorses. He died in
’Frisco, and his Yankee owner was “sport” enough
to have the skeleton set up and returned to Fogland.
The first speed animal to be preserved in this way
was the unbeaten Eclipse. His framework is at the
Roval Veterinary College, Camden Town. Edward
ATI. had a habit of preserving all he could of his
most famous nags when they died. Persimmon’s head
decorates a hall at Sandringham, his tail is in the
stable-room there, his bones are in the Natural His-
tory Museum at South Kensington, and his hide was
cut into small pieces to form the special feature of
numerous gold and silver caskets' that Edward dis-
tributed among his many horsey friends.
* * * * *
The imported Tosson, a winner at the
S.A.J.C. meeting, got' away with the Valley Handicap
at Moonee (Vic.) last week, carrying 9st. 21b. over
the mile in “43.” It was a very good gallop and
made several people look up what the horse is asked
to carry in the Caulfield Cup. The list says 7st. 91b.
As Tosson cost less than a couple of hundred he
doesn’t owe anyone much, and is suspected of being'
the kind of animal that may win again.
* * * *
Ecuador, winner of the Jumpers’ Flat Race
at Moonee Valley (Vic.) on Saturday, cost his owner
CO guineas; and at that figure was fairly cheap. He
has been an occasional winner at country meetings
since the deal. But Ecuador wasn’t always cheat),
llis previous owner acquired him at three years old
for 380 guineas, and was glad to get rid of him.
The previous proprietor had given 1500 guineas for
him as a yearling. Ecuador is by Grafton —Silver
Coin, and was bred by T. Payten. He is full brother
to the Australian Cup winner Peru.
* * * *
. Just before the recent Hawkes Bay
(M.L.) Winter Meeting a local sport, G. L. Stead,
who specialises in ’chasers, took a fancy to an aged
gelding named Sam Pan, by San Fran —Waikaha, an
acceptor in the June Steeplechase. The owner of
the animal wasn’t much interested in Sam, and
didn’t reckon the beast had a ghost of a chance,
so he said he would part with the prad for “ £5 a
leg.” Stead promptly handed over the 20 sovs., and
a couple of days later got his capital back plus a
big war profit, the animal romping home in the race'
and demanding 245 sovs. Whereupon the original
owner of the moke leaped on his hat and made libel-
lous remarks about himself.
* * * *
The liowl to have all racing stopped in
Maoriland is still hitting the stars, but no one
outside the Little Bethel seems to be taking notice.
There might be something in the yelp if everything
else were stooped. Meanwhile the certain thing
is that since the big scrap started the racing clubs
over yonder have contributed more than £50,000. to
patriotic funds and handed the Government £300,000
in taxes on receipts and tote investments. Even
piety is impressed by a stream of revenue like that.
* * * *
Jimmy Clabby is not the first pugilist to
own a racehorse in Australia. Tommy Burns had
Brilliant Boy running in six-furlong dashes back in
1909, and won some cash with the nag. Burns also
acquired Julia B. (Soolt —Roxiana). At the time
it was thought that Julia B. —then a yearling filly—
would never stand a preparation, and a buyer, Fox,
of Westralia, was found prepared to take a chance.
The same man purchased Brilliant Boy. Both
animals won numerous races in the West, particu-
larly Julia, who afterwards went to Victoria and is
now on the Speck. Frank Thorn, present feather-
weight champion of Queensland, had a. string of
ponies, which he trained himself, in Melbourne a
year or two back, but has found stoush more profit-
able than moke. George Johns, who was weltei - -
weiaht champion of Australia for years, lias made
laying the odds better business than talcin’ ’em, and
does business on Sydney pony courses. Busra, the
lepper Clabbv is stated to have an interest in, ran
second in Moonee Valley (Melbourne) Steeplechase
last Saturday.
* * * *
Owner E. Oatley writes to Sydney Tele-
graph complaining about crook riding and demand-
ing that it be “put clown.” “No owner.” says
Oatley, “has of late suffered more than mvself. In-
variably mv horses are severely dealt with, and my
jockeys bitterly complain, but unfortunately for
reasons best known to themselves they dislike report-
ing offenders to the stewards. Hence malpractice
continues without abatement.” Oatley will have the
sympathy of a lot of people. This business has been
referred to several times recently by The Bulletin,
and it hones to see him stick in the game and root
some of the crooks out. But it will onlv be done
with an axe. It is no use waiting- for complaints to
come from the boys themselves. They are terrified to
say a word ; and there is more than one prominent
horseman in Australia to-day whose failure is due to
fear more often than to anything else. Racing
stewards seem to he blind to what goes on, and this is
well known to all racing men. This paoer despairs
of seeing the evil cut out by the people who now
have the matter in hand, and is coming to the con-
clusion that what is wanted is the appointment of
one individual, at a good salary, in every racing
centre, armed with authority to issue jockeys’ licenses
and suspend or cancel them on his own initiative and
without having to hear evidence which is mostly
perjury. If such an appointment were made and the
official had a chin, several courses would be much
more tidy places than they are now.
*' * * *
A Sydney paner the other day gave the
record turf winnings of a horse as Donovan’s
£55,000. But Isinglass (’92-95) raked in over
£57,000. Even that figure would almost certainly
have been far exceeded by Flying Fox, who, after
winning £40,000 in two seasons, and being looked
upon as a dead cert, for another £40,000 in the
following year, was disqualified by the death of his
owner, the Duke of Westminster. But the biggest
money often tumbles in after the turf is done with.
St. Simon, at a 600-guinea fee, totalled over £BO,OOO.
La Fleclie cost as a yearling £6OOO. Then she won
over £30.000 in stakes, and was sold into the stud
for £13,000.
The very latest English war levy is the
“spectator” tax. It was collected for the first time
at the Newmarket Second Soring Meeting and was
as follows: “Looking” from the Grand Stand, 2s.
per clay, ladies Is.; from the five-bob stand, 3d.;
from the half-crown stand, 2d. So far no one
has had to pay a tax for using a field-glass, but that
is probably on the way. A harassed Minister of
Finance anxiously looking round for cash to plough
up the road to Berlin is liable to leap at tuppence
wherever he sights it.
* * * *
While horse-naming worries some people
there are others who approach the job with light and
airy casualness. E. E. D. Clarke (Vic.) is one of
the casuals. A couple of years ago he christened a
trio of his prads One, Two and Three. This season
he labels another batch Thrice, Billion and Trillion.
He seems to have forgotten Hop, Skip and Jump,
likewise Rags, Tags and Bags. And what about
Dearly, Beloved, Brethren?
Fred Kay, the lanky Westralian who
generally contrives to be absent when someone tries
to hit him, had an argument with Tommy Uren at
Sydney Stadium on Satuiday, and Thomas mostly
landed where the other chap was before he shifted.
It was a rather dreary conflict and raged with furious
monotony over the full journey—with the exception
of the 17th round, when both men cut loose and
handed out frightfulness. Mainly the thing consisted
of Uren struggling to get there and arriving when
Kay had moved to anew address. If the Stadium
people could manage to import someone fitted with
arms designed on telescopic principles, a battle
between the stranger and Kay ought to draw like a
revival picnic. Then Kay would find himself nailed
with a left hook to tiie jaw, and when heleaped
away the glove would leap after him !7 f t. jthd hook
him again, and the other one would spring forward
and biff him on the reverse. On Saturday it was
reckoned by Arthur Scott that Kay s absence scored
more points than Uren’s arrival at where he dicta t
want to be. Perhaps he was right. It didn t mattei,
anyhow. _ *

PHIL
SHAPPERE,
of Vic. footracing.
If Wave Gieke thought that Frank Thorn
was not still carrying a punch he made a mistake,
because at Brisbane on Saturday it passed him out in
the 17th collision. It used to look at one time as if
young Gieke would go a long way. But it often
looks that way until it looks the other way.
Eddie Moy had another belt at Herb
McCoy at Melbourne on Saturday night, and inci-
dentally cleared the atmosphere of doubt. This
time McCoy wasn’t in the hunt, Moy’s left knocking
with the persistency of a time-payment collector and
taking all the sting out of Herbert’s most strenuous
efforts. The harder the Victorian slogged in, the
cooler the Yank took it, planting straight lefts and
right crosses with due deliberation and much fre-
quency. As in their previous disturbance, Moy
finished up with his opponent on the totter, but this
time took home the dispensation of the referee.
Either McCoy is very stale or he has hit the slide.
More imported articles —Arlos Fanning, who will
meet Spargo, and the swarthy Mexican, Benny Palmer,
McCoy’s next opponent—were put on show before the
performance.
* * * *
The tax on amusement is collected occa-
sionally. Les Darcy was ordered by a beak the other
day to" pav £4 6s. for driving his motor-car at ex-
cessive speed along a Sydney street. As Darcy has
acquired a car and a fortune in a couple of years by
the amusing process of driving his fist against the
midriff of various persons, there seems to be a lot of
solid joy in being a pug. who hears the referee do
the counting for someone else. But not always.
There are some fairly successful pugs who haven’t
cars.
* * * *
N. S. Wales’ motor registrations to June
30, 1916, total 21,055 —a rise of 3149 on the figures
for 1915; also 30,624 drivers’ licenses show a jump
of 4352. So the poor horse is pushed off the highway
into the paddock. But he still possesses the final use
of being turned into razor-strops.
*■ * * *
A youngster not yet 18 is making a blaze
in N.S.W. road-cycling. In successive week-ends this
E. Sells has won the Windsor (the League open
event of the season) and two 10-mile club events.
The efforts have dragged him astern among the
baekmarkers; but the manner of his wheeling sug-
gests that he may have an appetite for still bigger
prizes.
Australians continue chasing motor-cycle
records and the old man with a scythe. The ink
chronicling the world’s record whiz of H. A. Parsons
was not dry when Jack Booth (Vic.) leaped aboard
a 7-h.p. Indian and streaked over a mile in 43 2-ssee.
(3sec. off the “best”) and five miles in 3min. 57sec.
(14 2-ssec. improvement). His arrival at the end of
10 miles in Bmin. 28 4-ssec. was 2-ssec. late.
Drizzling rain and mud were no assistance. Now for
the next.
* * * *
Word comes that Charlie Ivelleway, the
tall Glebe (Sydney) cricketer, has got in the way of
a Hun missile, and is on one side for repairs.
Young Fred Weiss’s win in N.S.W.
Markers’ Tournament at Sydney kept the family
record going in the billiard world. “Dad” AVeiss was
a great cueist in these parts; and young Fred looks
like being a useful performer in the same line.
Charlie Memmott, one-time Australian champion, is
helping the youngster over the stones.
“D.F.”: Fanny Durack has a complete
monopoly in world’s swimming records as far as
women are concerned. She holds the time for 100
yards, 100 metres, 220 yards, 300 yards, 300 metres,
440 yards, 880 yards, and the mile. Some of her
speeds compare not too badly with the men’s records.
She splashed the 880 yards in 12min. 52sec., and the
world’s best is llmin. 11 3-ssec.
* * * *
Tlie 100yds. professional race for girls,
decided at Mudgee (N.S.W.) on Saturday, re-
solvvd into a championship match between
Bessie Crandemange, of Botany, and Hazel
Meers, of Mudgee. Bessie won by 3in. in 11 2-ssec.
—a world’s record. The best previous sprints
registered by girls were done in 12sec. A man who
can run a hundred in 11 2-ssec. is no slouch. The
Grandemange family is noted for its sprinters and
distance runners. Freely translated, the name means
“big eater,” which suggests that the appetite of the
ancestral Grandemange was not in vain.
“W.Ii.H.” (from the other end) : Last
week the papers briefly recorded that “George Gray,
the champion billiardist, has suddenly departed for
America, without any previous intention, and without
any arrangement for engagements.” Far be it from
me to suggest that the fact of eligible men being
called up on June 17 under the new compulsion
scheme had anything to do with this precipitous
departure.
Lieut. Cecil McVilly, who left the Speck
with the A.I.F. recently, was for the past five or six
years Australia’s foremost amateur sculler. He first
came to light in 1910, when as a raw recruit he won
the championship of Tasmania from P. C. Ivens (Vie.j
and A. Priddle (N.S.W.). Next year lie wiped out
Jack Towns at Sydney, and thereby annexed the
ribbon for Australia. But his crowning achievement
came in 1913, when he followed up a victory in the
International Grand Challenge Cup on the Thames by
pulling off the event beyond which no amateur can
hope to g'O —the Diamond Sculls. In this race he
earned a good deal of criticism from the English
papers by allegedly giving his backwash to the
English champion. It was afterwards pretty generally
admitted that the accusations were unjustified, for,
apart from the fact that giving an opponent one s
backwash is unavoidable in a narrow stream, anyone
who knew McVilly was aware that he was one of the
finest representatives of the great Dinkum family, and
a man who under all circumstances endeavored, to
play the game. McVilly was often pressed to join
the profesh ranks, and had he done so there s not the
slightest doubt he would have gone a long way
towards winning the world’s championship.
“Sound trumper-like cricket” is tlie way
the Egyptian Mail (9/6/’l6) refers to a score of 25
made by a Sergeant Thompson in a regimental match
played the previous day by Australians. The wicket
was finally captured by Sergeant De Boos. How
Sergeant MacDougall topped the score with 42 is
told by the Mail in a graphic sentence: He did it
with a succession of hits and misses, ’ That s the
beauty of cricket. Hit a man for six byes or miss
him to leg for five, it is all the same.
* * * *
Although two rounds have yet to be
played, Sydney Soccer premiership is all over. Annan-
dale's efforts in winning 11 out of 12 matches
pushes them so far ahead that not even an earthquake
can rob them of the glory. During the season
Annandale put up a splendid record by scoring 53
goals to eight. W. Carroll- gets goals as easily as
a curate swallows tea.
* * * *
On the Adelaide Oval Prince Alfred
College added to its many sporting wins of the last
two years by giving St. Peter’s a bad beating at
football. ' The game was not one-sided, but the
winners had more system when attacking. It is the
only football the Oval is likely to see, this season, the
game being dead for seniors, except as to an alleged
“Patriotic” League, which js sternly thrust out into
suburban ovals and park lands. Almost the same
day P.A.O. learned that two of its teaching staff had
been killed in France —sons of parson historian
Blanket and of doctor-of-music Harold Davies. Both
soldiers were “old Princes,” and brilliant athletes in
their day.

To The Motorists
of Australia.
'*r
The importation of Foreign-made Motor Tyres
at any time is against the advancement of
Australian Industry, but at the present juncture—
whilst the Empire is fighting for freedom and
civilization—the buying of Foreign-made goods is
economically unsound and contrary to Australian
interests. Last year Motor Tyres to the value of
£427,000 (including a small proportion for General
Rubber Goods) were imported into this Country.
This Australian money went to enrich oversea
capital and oversea workmen, when it could have
been more loyally employed in furthering
Australian industry and prosperity. There is a
supply of “ DUNLOPS ” and other Australian-
made tyres to meet all demands without the
importation of a single foreign-made tyre.
Why not support the Country that support
you? Australian-made
DUNLOP
TYRES
offer vou equal—if not better —mileage and tyre
satisfaction than you can secure from foreign
tyres, plus lower initial cost, and the additional
advantage that "Dunlops” are made specially
heavy, with a view to withstanding hard work on
Australian roads, and that we as manufacturers
arfe on the spot to liberally meet all adjustment
claims. The Motorist who always uses “ Dunlops
helps his country and helps himself by ensuring
economy in tyre upkeep, and maximum service.

“Yarriambiak”: I hope that Speaker
Madden of the Victorian Assembly will stick
to his guns in the matter of lobbying, and
no harm would be done if the Federal
Speaker followed suit. Within a week or
two the Victorian Parliament will consider
the proposed referendum on the six o’clock
closing question, and as both Bung and the
Wowser are mighty 100 Dyers somebody will
be smothered if Madden doesn’t stand firm.
But lie was unfortunate in picking on Hyett,
secretary of the Railways Union, as the
first object of his wrath. The railway men
have been agitating for wages boards for
years, but. have always been loftily told that
they had no need for either wages boards or
arbitration courts, as they had the right of
appeal to the highest court in the land —
Parliament itself. Now they are thrown
neck and crop from that tribunal, too.

the skin the better. I do not
believe much in massage, but a
little cream to the face is neces-

wind or sun. What cream
would I recommend? Well, I
advise you to use a little mer-
colised wax every night and
again in the morning, after
washing the face. Rub it
gently into the skin, then wipe
off any superfluous wax and
dust a little barri-agar over
the face. You will find that
this will be the only -“treat-
ment” necessary and will keep
your face fresh and youthful-
looking for all your life. The mercolised
wax removes ail the dead outer skin, so
that you have always a fair, fresh com-
plexion like a girl’s.

Blackheads Fly Away.
Instantaneous Remedy for Blackheads, Greasy Skin and Enlarged Pores.
A practically instantaneous remedy for
blackheads, greasy skins and enlarged pores,
recently discovered, is now coming into
general use in the boudoir. It is very
simple, harmless and pleasant. Drop a
stymol tablet, obtained at the chemist’s, in
a tumblerful of hot water. After the effer-
vescence has subsided bathe the face in the
liquid, using a small sponge or soft cloth.
In a few minutes dry the face and the offen-
sive blackheads will come right off on the
towel. Also the large oily pores immedi-
ately close up and efface themselves natu-
rally. The greasiness disappears and the
skin is left smooth, soft and cool. This
simple treatment is then repeated a few
times at intervals of four or five days, to
ensure the permanence of the result.
Grey Hair—Home Remedy.
An old-fashioned Recipe restores Youthful Appearance.
There ai'e plenty of reasons why grey
hair is not desirable, and plenty of reasons
why hair dyes should not be used. But, on
the other hand, there is no reason why you
should have grey hair if you do not want
it. To turn the hair back to a natural color
is really a very simple matter. One has
only to get from the chemist two ounces of
concentrate of tammalite and mix it with
three ounces of bay rum. Apply to the
hair with a small sponge for a few nights
and the greyness will gradually disappear.
This liquid is not sticky or greasy and does
not injure the hair in any way. It has been
used for generations with most satisfactory
results by those who have known the
formula.
To Kill Roots of Superfluous Hair.
The most Effective Formula ever Discovered.
Women annoyed with disfiguring growths
of superfluous hair wish to know not merely
how to temporarily remove the hair, but how
to kill the hair roots permanently. For
this purpose pure powdered pheminol may be
applied directly to the objectionable hair
growth. The recommended treatment is
designed not only to instantly remove the
hair but also to actually kill the roots so that
the growth will not return. About an ounce
of pheminol, obtainable from the chemist,
should be sufficient.
Good News for Fat People.
Something New in Obesity Cures.
A London chemist says: “The latest
method of reducing obesity certainly is far
more pleasant and convenient than all pre-
vious methods. It consists merely in eating
clynol berries. The fat person who wants
to reduce without the usual rigid diet, exer-
cise, sweating baths, etc., now puts a few
of these little brown berries in his or her
pocket and eats three or four each day.”
Clynol berries not only eliminate fat from
the body, but also correct the tendency,
which is usually constitutional, to create
fatty matter. No discomfort whatever is
caused by their action; in fact, except for
the loss of superfluous fat and the feeling
of . “fitness” so created, you would not be
aware that these little berries were doing
their work.
Local inquiry shows that clynol berries are
not very well known in this country, but
the demand is increasing daily, and any
chemist can quickly procure them if specially
requested to do so.

“Norbell” : In Uncle Wilson’s country they
have a simple device for emptying the “con
tinuous” vaudeville dens which send up the
lunacy 'statistics there. The real 18-carat
turns are shown only in the evening, and
the afternoon audiences often exhibit symp
toms of wanting to sit tight and take a
second helping. To foil this the managers
put on diabolical “supper turns” about
0 pan. The yarn goes that once the audi-
ence was obstinate and wouldn’t shift. The
manager sent on the “Refined Sketch Team”
first, and then the “Performing Pigs;” and
so on over and over again in that order.
Rut the house endured it. Then the Refined
Sketchers were sent on twice in succession.
That was too much. The audience rose
as one man and yelled: “For Gorsake give
us the pigs!”
“Up Stage”: After a satisfactory argu-
ment with the doctor, two members of the
Bert Bailey Co. have signed up for the
big fight. Jack Fowler (Billie Bearup in
“On Our Selection”) goes into camp next
week. The previous exponent of “Bill”
(Fred White) found his way to a commis-
sion, and is now “somewhere abroad.” And
George Kensington joins his partners next
month.
“First Old Man” : The reason why “To-
night’s the Night,” just staged at Her
Majesty’s (Melb.), is a somewhat crude
reminder of “The Girl in the Taxi” (B.
13/7/’l6) is that both pieces are free-and-
easy versions of the ancient farce “Pink
Dominoes,” one version being a little less
slipshod than the other. “Pink Dominoes”
itself was an adaptation from the French
and belongs to a numerous family of so-
called “naughty” plays very popular in the
’7o’s in London, any one of which with just
a little tinkering and a few song-and-dance
interpolations would be thought good
enough for a new musical comedy nowadays.
“Bunker”: When the Japanese cruisers
were lately visiting Wellington (M.L.) the
theatre band, after playing a variety of
National Anthems, struck up something that
puzzled everybody. Presently the whisper
was passed that it was Japan’s tune. All
present stood up except a number of Japan-
ese officers. One of our soldier-men, puzzled,
said: “They’re playing your Nat’onal
Anthem.” The astonished vis'tors remarked :
“Our National Anthem! So sorry. Not
know. We are stupid fellows.” A word was
said in Japanese. And they all rose to their
feet, and there wasn’t even a ripple of a smile
among them. You can’t beat that for polite-
ness.

from the Brook when they return from the

Haddon Chambers has written a new
play,, this time for Cyril Maude, who, like
Chambers, is seen oftener in the U.S.A.
than anywhere else these times. The piece
is a comedy, “Uncle Blinn” —to be sampled

“T.” : Playwrights worry their brains to
find what they consider suitable titles for
their pieces, but do play-titles matter, any-
how ? Here are a few of recent London
attractions: “Samples” (Vaudeville), “More”
(Ambassador’s), “Hot and Cold” (Opera
House), “Half-past Eight” (Comedy), “Fol-
low the Crowd” (Empire), “The Bing Boys
are Here” (Alhambra), “Bric-a-Brac”
(Palace), “Honi Soit” (Metropolitan).
“Pick-a-Dilly” (Pavilion). True, these all
belong to irresponsible revues, but I believe
that some of the plays now running would be
every bit as successful if titles were swapped
-—that “Romance” would draw just as well
if called “Hot and Cold” and “The Boomer-
ang” still knock ’em as “Honi Soit.”
“Up stage”: Glancing down the glorified
programme of a vaudeville house last week,
I was confronted by the awful spectacle of
the names of 14 performers due to arrive
shortly from the U.S., and prominently
spaced was the enormous expense necessary
to entice these “stars” from their native
land. Local talent wasn’t mentioned. If
we must have mediocrity—and nine-tenths
of these U.S. impoiTs are no better than
that —what about giving the Australian
accent a chance? Australian audiences
could help in that matter by showing their
appreciation of the few Australian turns
they do get, instead of being bluffed into
giving their loudest roars of applause to the
foreign goods.
THE CHORUS MEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’m glad I’m not a chorus man
Upon the stage parading,
To amble forward with my clan,
And caper like a frisky Nan
The fair soprano aiding.
I’m glad I’m not a chorus man—
There’s times when it's degrading.
1 would not do my sex the wrong.
I would not swank and flutter
While someone sang a rotten song.
I'd hate to gibber with the throng
And hoot the hoots they utter.
I would not do my sex the wrong—
I’d rather sweep the gutter!
I would not dog the tenor round
In wig distinctly brummy,
Nor wag my arms, and buck and bound,
Nor rag with gravity profound,
And grin and gape, Lor’ lumnie!
Before I’d dog the tenor round
I’d be a barber’s dummy.
They’re always “coming to the front.”
According to their showing;
They talk about their little stunt,
Worked with an imitation punt,
And weird pretence of rowing.
They're always coming to the front —
Gorstruth, why ain’t they going?
VICTORIA.

Iu matters of State economy it is often
difficult to know where the line should be
drawn, but the Vic. Education Department
has a pretty fair idea of the correct thing.
In the winter there are Government tires
in the administrative offices, but no Govern-
ment fires in the State schools. Any warmth
there is contributed by the parents of the
school-children. One infuriated mamma
complained the other day in the daily press
that her little Willie, in order to help with
the firing, had to carry a billet of wood
and a lump of coal every day to school!
This sort of thing isn’t national economy—
it’s simply one of several mean habits from
which the Education Department doesn’t
seem able to free itself.
Where on God’s Earth shall we cast them,
The Bones of the Kaiser Cursed ?
In which fair Country of Europe
Shall the “Dust of the Hun” be nursed?
Defile not proud Belgium who fought so
For FREEDOM with might and main:
For France the Fair, —O Rulers,
We crave for her the same.

How YOU Can Help Win the War.
Every man. every woman, with money to in vest, however much or little, can
help to win the War by lending it to the Country now.
It is not a favor you are asked, "but a privilege, the privilege of doing your share
in helping our men —your men —at the Front.
This reason alone must appeal more strongly than the rate of interest,
unquestionable security, and other advantages.
Employees of Australia.
Your mates are fighting your battle. Are you prepared to help finance them t
£1 down and £1 a month will pay for a £lO War Bond. Surely you can spare
a little for your Country from your wages.
Buy War Bonds for yourself, for your Wife, for your Children, but above all
buy them.

Government Notices.
QOMMON W E A L T H OF A U STR ALI A.
DEPARTMENT OF DEFENCE.
NOTICE.
War Precautions Act ' 191-1-.1916.
Requisitioning of Rabbit Skins.
(1) It is notified foi* public information that, the
Commonwealth Government has decided to requisition
rabbit-skins suitable for military requirements, and
has appointed authorised agents for such purpose.
(2) The authorised agents are Wm. Haughton &
Co., of—
-255a George Street, Sydney ;
515 Collins Street, Melbourne;
Currie Street, Adelaide;
C/o A. G. Webster & Sons Ltd., Hobart; .
C/o Lee & Co. Ltd., Launceston.
(3) The maximum prices which the authorised
agents will pay for rabbit-skins requisitioned for
military purposes will be, until further notice:
First Winter Skins, “Sleeved” ... . • 1/8 per lb.
First Winter Skins, “Butchered” or
“Slippered” •• •• ••
Second Winter and First incoming
“Sleeved” • • • - - - • • 1/® »
Second Winter and First incoming
“Butchered” or “Slippered” .. .. 1/1 ~
The sum of 2/6 per cwt. will be allowed in addi-
tion to above prices for packing, etc.
The above prices are for skins delivered at such
places in Sydney, Melbourne, Adelaide, Hobart and
Launceston as ‘the authorised agents order, in
thoroughly dry condition, free from ears, meat, leg
bones and tail bones. For skins not complying, with
the foregoing- specification an allowance in price must
be made to render them equal thereto. Typical
samples of the skins required will be available for
inspection at the offices of the authorised agents.
(1) Terms of payment—Cash on delivery.
(5) Every owner or person in possession of rabbit-
skins is required to deliver them up when called
upon so to do at such time and place as the author-
ised agents appoint, and any owner or person in
possession of rabbit-skins who refuses or fails to
comply with any requirement *of the . authorised
agents in connection with the requisitioning of
rabbit-skins shall be guilty of an offence.
(6) Owners or persons in possession of rabbit:skms
are called upon to classify them and notify the
authorised agents that they are ready for inspection.
It is not proposed to requisition all rabbit-skins, but
only such skins as are selected by the authorised
agents or their duly-accredited representative as
being required for military purposes. The classes of
skins required are those mentioned in paragraph 3 of
this notice. All skins not requisitioned by the
authorised agents after inspection will be immedi-
ately released to the owners and will be entirely at
their disposal. . .
(7) 1. Every authorised agent or Ins duly-accred-
ited representative is empowered to: -
(a) ask questions and require the production of
documents in relation to the purchase, sale, dis-
posal or origin of any rabbit-skins, or the actual
or probable supply of rabbit-skins and;
( b ) enter at any reasonable hour in the day-time
upon any premises or place in which there are,
or are believed by him to be any rabbit-skins,
and inspect any rabbit-skins therein.
2. Any person who refuses or fails to answer
questions or produce documents asked for or required
in connection with the requisitioning of rabbit-skins,
or who resists, obstructs or in any way interferes
with an authorised agent or his duly accredited
representative in the exercise of his duty in the
requisitioning of rabbit-skins, shall be guilty of an
offence.
PENALTIES.
Section 6 of the War Precautions Act 1914-1910
provides that any person who contravenes or fails to
complv with an Order made in pursuance of the Act
is guilty of an offence, which may be punishable
either summarily or upon indictment.
The maximum penalties are:- —
Upon summary conviction, a fine of one hundred
pounds or imprisonment for six months, or both:
Upon conviction or indictment, a fine of any amount
or imprisonment for any term, or both.
G. F. PEARCE,
Minister of State for Defence.
Bth July, 1916.

BANANA LAND
Premier Ryan probably earned the bis-
cuits and beef-tea he ate on the voyage.
Anyhow, the Privy Council upset the High
Court decision in the Eastern case, and
thus definitely settled, it is hoped, the ques-
tion as to the liability of Governments for
acts of negligence by licensed pilots. It has
generally been laid down in Australia that
when pilots are guilty of carelessness the
State is not liable for damages. Last year,
however, the High Court decided otherwise
when a Queensland pilot landed the E. and
A. liner on a shoal; and it was to put an
appeal before the Privy Council that Tommy
hopped off to London.
“Politicus” : The recent death of Foxton,
one of the longest-lived (politically) of
Bananaland’s public men, reminds me that
of the Pliilp Government which went out in
the debacle of 1904 Philp himself is the only
elected _ Minister who is now alive.
Foxton’s best feature was the concern he
always showed for the aboriginal popula-
tion. Prior to his day Warrigal had no
advocate in Parliament; but the loud-voiced,
domineering Home Secretary was a genuine
friend to the black remnant.
Two elderly gentlemen, Tolmie and
Rankjn. are the total contribution to the
war .ranks from the 120 legislators who
make up the Queensland Parliament. Yet
fully 50 per cent, of the 120 are men appar-
ently eligible from age and physique, and
numbers are unmarried. Further, Queens-
land is- supposed to be more accustomed
than any other part of Australia to the idea
of universal service, for Sam Griffith carried
a measure over 30 years ago compelling
every citizen from 18 to 60 to serve in the
Defence Force.
P.F.” : A few years ago there was much
complaint in Bananaland that Japan had
stripped the country of its best horses for
remount purposes. Don’t you believe it.
Only a few days ago one of the surviving
(alleged) weeds got on to the Babinda rail-
way and knocked over a locomotive and
four trucks.
Oliver Holmes Woodward, who can wear
a Military Cross now, was born at Tenter-
field (N.S.W.) and is an old boy of New-
ington College, Sydney. He was one of the
most brilliant students of Charters Towers
School of Mines, collecting honors in 11
subjects in his three years’ course, as well
as the Browne memorial medal for mining
and metallurgy. After getting his mine
manager’s ticket in 11)10 he had a job at
Mount Morgan, and was looking after the
development of the co.’s prospecting shows
in Papua when Bill broke loose. Woodward,
who is now a lieutenant of Australian
Engineers in France, is a nephew of J. 11.
lieid, partner of John Moffat, of Irvinebank,
old-time mining king of North Queensland.
Rockhampton District Court lately saw
the three card trick illustrated without
having to pay a copper for the fun. Two
keen blades were charged with taking down
a Russian with the wearisome old swindle,
and, the defence being mistaken identity, one
ot the accused—a flash colored ge’man—-
asked the Court’s permission to play a brief
game and see if prosecutor could recognise
NOT THAT SORT AT ALL.
“Politician , isn't he?"
"Oh dear, n o—very decent chap.”
i f as the one he’d dropped his money on.
The Court agreed and “Nipper” got busy.
"Now, here y’ are." lie said to the Russian.
"Two red cards and a black. Keep your
eye on the black, and see if you can pick it.
There —you see the black right., under your
eyes. I turn tlie cards down, so. Now,
which is the black?” And of course Bruin
picked the wrong one, and couldn’t pick the
right one even when there were, only two
on the table. It was a fine exhibition of
flashness —so fine that the Judge thought
Brudder Johnson was best inside for six
months.
The Shire of Taroom contains 5,000,000
acres, and it is stated on good authority
that to-day not one holding is free of
prickly pear. Where are Dr. Jean Bell and
Oliver Cromwell Roberts? They s&ray and
talk and talk and spray, and meantime the"
spiked helmet of the vegetable Hun peeps
daily over more advanced entrenchments.
There is a powerful and growing feeling
in the country districts of Queensland in
favor of conscription. So many men have
gone from the smaller places that in many of
them there is hardly a family that has not
a representative at the Front. All these
households naturally favor compulsion. The
country—the true Australia—is almost
solid: it is only in the big towns, which
are too cosmopolitan to be anything, that you
find the apologists for the shirker.
The King’s Arm.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Kings have long arms, my dear, my dear....”
“But your horse is swift and I ride so near
And the will o’ the wisp that rules the fen
Will play a trick on these monarch’s men,
Will lead them floundering round and round
While we go safe on the steady ground.
“Oh, I saw his eyes on your face last night,
His strange dark, eves in their mask of white,
And I saw the Queen’s glance follow him—
Poor patient Queen —and her eyes grew dim.
She may remember (God fend her heart!),
As she sits soul-chilled on her throne apart,
.In days when she was as young and fair
How his eyes followed her everywhere.
“She has seen so many come and go—
The flsher lass with her breast of snow,
The gypsy girl with her jetty brows,
'They all have reigned in the dower house.
But you....put spurs to your grey jennet
We will win the hills; we shall foil him jet!’’
The witches ride on their brooms to-night,
The moon peers out with a ghostly light
Over the black pits and the moss
And the shallow ford where the lovers cross;
But a warm inn-liglit on the dark night spills—
They have left the bogs, they have won the hills 1
The hills that cover the lowest stars,
The loyal hills with their long brown scars ;
And the straggling pines and the mountain flowers,
The friendly hills with their guarding towers!
“O little pigeon that fled the lure,
O mistress mine, ye shall sleep secure,
Where the scented heather the whole world fills
With a lullaby of the wind i’ the hills!
“For better be this than flick of gem,
And a scarlet shame for a diadem,
And a crushed white rose to ease the drouth
Of a great king’s ever-thirsting mouth.” v
She laid her down on her small white bed,
A cross was hung at the curtain’s head ;
And the window over the black ravine
Was tightly closed with a bar between.
He slept, worn out by his long night ride,
Outside the door of his hard-won bride—
Slept and dreamed of the happy chance
That would land him safe with his love in France;
Slept and dreamed of a filling sail
That would bear them out on a splendid gale.
When morn tripped over the world’s wide floor
He rose and tapped at the chamber door.
The light through an open window shone
As a vacant bed it glowed upon,
Picked a silken slipper from out the gloom;
A knot of ribbon. . . .an empty room. . . .
What dastard reiver has entered here?
“Kings have long arms, my dear, my dear!”
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, July 22, 1916.
My Dear Tiiargomindah,—
We are all working with our coral-like
ears set back for the lied Cross hunt next
Friday. Huntresses will pad all parts of
the city, and the committee has been feeding
them on raw steak for weeks past to make
them tierce.
Button Day is also assuming a threaten-
ing aspect. Two-tliiids of tne proceeds will
go towards tilling the dug-outs of 20,000
men with comforts. The spare third will
be divided between other regimental funds,
except those of the 41st and 42nd Bat-
talions, whose promoters prefer to work
independently.
A bal masque in aid of the French lied
Cross was herd in the South Brisbane Tecii-
nical College to-night. French Consul A. J.
Carter and Mr. Giraud, sec. of our Ally’s
lied Cross, received the party, while Major
and Mrs. Pendlebury, Major and Mrs.
Weavers and representatives of the rabbit-
skin profession adorned the stage.
At the Women’s Club on Thursday night
two papers, one giving some information
about the late Mr. P. B. Shelley, and the
other contrasting Jane Austen and Char-
lotte Bronte, whose Irish father, as you
remember, my dear, was born Patrick
Prunty, were served up. The business of
the gathering was knitting, and before
settling down to the job, each woman un-
loaded a pair of socks, or something equally
good for the men on active service.
Some' time ago Rosemount, a beautiful
home on the Bowen Bridge tramline, was
lent to the Red- Gross Society until the end
of the war, for ‘use as a convalescent hospi-
tal. Now I hear from an Anzac who
is at present being repaired there that the
sick and wounded are not permitted to
enjoy the gardefi, the same being reserved
for the use qf the staff. Neither must the
battered inmates enter or leave the premises
by the front gate, the side gate being con-
sidered good enough for them. Now, who’s
been getting some wrinkles at Shepheard’s?
Miss A. Doyle, a Cairns maiden, is on
her way home from Sopth Africa, laden with
spoils of the chase. ■ .Rude man, who insists
that lovely woman cannot hit a haystack
unless she aims at some other object, is
invited to inspect the peelings of lions,
elands and monkeys which were bowled over
by the lady. This is a case where the
beasts were not drugged, as is the practice
when distinguished visitors (such, for
instance, as the Kaiser’s hopeful, wffien he
contributed to the gaiety some years ago)
are on the warpath.
That energetic Bananaland soldier, Col.
Brand, D. 5.0., continues to distinguish him-
self. His mother, who lives at Bundaberg,
was the proudest woman in the North the
other day when a cable told her that her
son had been promoted to Brigadier-General.
According to sister Room, who can't
leave London, the latest matrimonial threats
for the near future were those of Capt.

AUSTRALIA JANE.
Maryborough buried its oldest inhabitant,
Henry Palmer, the other day. He lived to
be 95, notwithstanding the debilitating
climate, etc. He was the first mayor of the
town, and still found time to amass much
wealth. In the old days he acted as agent
for immigrants, looking after them whilst
they awaited bullock drays to cart them
Outback to the stations. One particularly
old and almost inseparable friend attended
the funeral. Someone left the gate open,
and the old man’s aged horse, without saddle
or bridle, followed his dead boss to the
resting place.
The other day Home Secretary Huxham
visited Westbrook Boys’ Reformatory, and
these are some of his subsequent remarks: —
The place is dirty and the boys are dirty. I don’t
think some of them wash once a week. Their hair
sticks out through the crowns of their hats, their
clothes are ragged, and their toes show through their
boots. Yet we have received no requisition for
clothes. There are no night-officers to watch the
boys. I found no attempt made to educate them.
The staff is totally inadequate, and the sanitation is ~
bad. No inducement has been given them to reform.
Of course the Government is going to make
a change. But so that the boys shall not
find it too sudden, control is to be given to
manager Jones, of the Warren State Farm.
He has been quite successful so far with
pigs and poultry.

“WE RADIATE HEALTH.”

direction of Prof. Adams, M.A., LL.D., D.Sc., grad-

Institute.
The imfccted Static generator employed by Prof.
Adams and used solely at the Radio Health Institute
is at once the most costly, modern and the most
effective instrument now in use in any part of the

most sanguine expectations of the most far-visioned

g3oa m. to 5 p.m. Address all communications

If any illustration appearing in 'xiie
Bulletin takes your fancy, write to tlio
Manager, who will quote you price for it.
Business, Robbery, etc.
“Dyer" : Talk about tlio shortage of dyes
to the proprietor of a dye business and lie
weeps; talk to the dyer who has to person-
ally use the rubbish which the dye specu-
lator palms off on his boss, and lie curses
like a bullocky. The boss groans and weeps
because he says lie can’t get any true-blue
German dyes now at any price, and his fool
of a foreman can’t make shift with the fake
stuff. I know a dyer who experimented
with local material in cotton blacks and
blues, and got a fair result—encouraging
enough, anyway, to warrant further experi-
ment. lie showed the work to liis boss and
asked assistance to continue. But the hide-
bound boss thought otherwise. There was
the expense, the uncertainty of successful
results—all the deadening arguments that
have tripped up Progress since man learnt
to talk. But Private Enterprise shouldn’t
be allowed to hold up the procession that
way. The search should be carried on by
the nation at the nation’s expense.
“X.”: The X. 8. Wales Department of
Agriculture thinks very little of the com-
mercial possibilities of poultry-raising if the
salaries offered for positions on the experi-
mental farms are anything to^ judge by. A
position of instructor at Wagga in poultry
raising is advertised at £lOO a year, with
board and residence. What sort of success
can students be expected to make after leav-
ing equipped with the knowledge of an
“expert” worth only two quid a week and
tucker? It recalls G. B. Shaw’s wisdom:
“He who can, does; he who cannot, teaches
others.”
“Try Fluke” : It’s about time the hopeful
Maorilanders who finance the periodical
treasure-hunts to the Auckland Islands real-
ised that whatever was dropped there by
the gold-ship General Grant, out of Mel-
bourne, is beyond reach. A stop certainly
should be put to these hopeless endeavors
by the report of Captain Cotting, the
skipper of the cutter Enterprise, which
turned up at Stewart Island the other day
from the penguin-roosts. The Enterprise’s
shellbacks made a determined but unprofit-
able try for the alleged treasure, and
Cotting’s fixed opinion now is that it was
recovered in 1869 by a party which went
to the Aucklands in a hooker named the
Daphne. But whether or no. there’s
Buckley’s hope of getting it after 50 years
on an appalling coast where there is an
almost continual raging surf—and especi-
ally when nobody knows exactly where the
ship was battered to driftwood.
“Fru”: Prior to the war there was a bit
of an industry known as "the imperishable
fern trade.” Maiden hair and other ferns
were cut here, dried, and shipped in bulk
to Hamburg. There the industrious Huns
got to work and by a process known only
to themselves revivified the Australian
vegetation and dyed it so as to look like
the fresh fern. In this form some at least
was shipped back here and sold at a good
price as everlasting fern. I have a speci-
men over two years old which looks as if it
had been gathered a day or two ago. The
war stopped the industry so far as the Hun
was concerned, but it needn’t stop us.
“Copabella”: There’s a man down in
Footscray trying to do his bit for Australian
industry. For many years he had manufac-
tured a common pattern of turned-leg kit-
chen chair. When the war caused the
Austrian bentwood species to be passed,
except by city councils and railway depart-
ments. lie decided to be a pioneer. First lie
tried to secure the man who knew how. but
without success. Russian knowledge
stopped at a three-ply bottom; Austrian
experience wasn’t available. So the Aus-
tralian had “to work it out himself, experi-
menting with home-grown timbers and home-
grown processes. He is making good, slowly.
Working in blackwood he has succeeded in
getting the right bend on the legs, the seat
rings and the struts, while the curve of the
back rail will pass muster. But he hasn’t
yet succeeded in making the bentwood back
and legs in one piece. The bottom he still
has to import: the other problem he works
at nights and Sundays. The chair is a sub-
stantial article, heavier than the Austrian,
the blackwood being very weighty. In
appearance the advantage is all with the
Footscray sea£. But the actual producing
cost —listen, you tarilf-shelvers ! —is about
75.; the cheaper-grado Vienna article used
to be landed for about Is. 3d.
*.* * *
“Yarrum”: I admire thrift, but it can
be overdone. A well-known vet. in N. S.
Wales had ordered a cocky to destroy a
diseased cow. and on his return to the selec-
tion some time later asked what had been
done with the carcase. “Gev it to the
pigs,” said the cocky. Everything about a
farm that the hens, dogs and other animals
will not touch goes to the sty. Cocky will
tell you that the pig is 0.K.. so long as
he is “topped up” with corn. No. thanks;
no pork to-day.
LEAD AND SHOUTING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Alilson said that lie shouted for another man
until they both got drunk, and then the man led
him into the trouble. The magistrate said that
many cases had come before him of lead being
stolen from houses. He imposed a sentence of three
months.- — Police-court report.
The skies are full of flying lead ;
The fields are full of shouting men.
All shouting in the bar is dead
When six o'clock comes round again ;
Hut in the fields lead rains at night
And shouters carry on the fight,.
The battle knows no closing hour,
And shouters drunk with rage and fear
Press onward through the leaden show’r.
The S.M., pen behind his ear,
Says in a voice quite cold and dead,
‘‘Another case of taking lead!”
One shouts and braves the leaden gale
And thereby wins the bronze V.C. ;
Another tells a moving tale
Of shout and lead. Ilis months are three.
The cannon’s lead makes heroes’ sport ;
ltoof-lead means gaol in any court.

An early start soon
gets the best of a
hard day’s work. Set
the time for any hour
you like, Big Ben will
ring you up.
Two o’clock or five
o’clock, dark as a
pocket or broad day
light, it’s all the same
to Big Ben.
Just set the indicator
and think no more
about it.
Big Ben ticks off the
seconds till the rising
hour arrives, then wakes
you with his tuneful call.
You can depend upon
Big Ben.
Big Ben is seven inches tall,
well built, handsome, punctual.
He has a big, easily read dial—a
cheerful, deep toned voice—large, com-
fortable winding keys—a light running
movement with watch-like escapement
—an alarm that calls steadily for five
minutes or intermittently for ten.
Big Ben undergoes a hundred inspec-
tions in the making and six rigid timing
tests before leaving the factory of the
Western Clock Co., La Salle, 111.,

The Inky Way.
“Kettledrum”: There comes a time to
pressmen all when news is not worth 30
cents; so I, when even cables pall, find rest
in the advertisements. I weary me of read -
ing how the Austrians are on the jump; how
Ilollweg smites his fevered brow, and how
the Kaiser’s heart must thump. I take no
note of submarines that sneak across to
U.S.A.; I care not if there are no means of
getting there some other way. It matters
not a whit to me if Wilson stands upon his
head, or writes a Note or lets it be ; I do
not mind what Itoosevelt said. The things
that move my sluggish mind, replace the
twinkle in my eye, I look hot in the news
to find, but rather seek them on the sly.
Page 1, of course, is bare of ads., but
oftentimes upon Page 3 I find Dame
Fashion’s latest fads, and much therewith
that pleases me. There is the Ostrich Neck-
let Girl, who from her column shyly smiles;
and lower down, amid a whirl of hair that
seems to stretch for miles, a damsel stoops
to wash her head, as I am told maids often
do ; most frequently my eyes ai-e led to that
dpcollete shampoo. And then the Photo-
graphic Peach, the beauty of the studio, ap-
pears to have so much to teach—and there’s
so much Ido not know! There’s nothing-
much upon Page 4, except a movie ad. or
tw r o ; the Cough Cure Grandam I abhor, for
she is old, and I am, new. Some odds and
ends of hats and vests are bordering the
cable page; they thrill me not—my interests
are elsewhere at the present stage. I turn
the leaf, and straightway flush a covey clad
in filmy things whose buxom beauties make
me -blush —this ad. the praise of corsets
sings. Right here my wife asserts her sway,
and drags the paper fiom my eyes—but I
have seen enough to say: “I’m glad that
people advertise.”

From the cypress column of the Argus': —
In memory of my dear kind son, Willie, who fell
asleep in the Murray River.
The casual Australian again.
“Black Rock” : I surrender to “Pejay” on
his claim (B. 29/6’lG) regarding the an-
tiquity of Portland Guardian. But he is
wrong in saying that it and the Geelong
Advertiser are more ancient than Mel-
bourne Herald. The latter is the oldest
Yictorian paper, born on Jan. 3, 1840.
George Cavanagh, who came from Sydney,
was its founder, and it was he who hobbled
it with the dreadful “Impartial, not neu-
tral” gag, which hung round its heels until
a year or so ago. It was a morning paper
at the time, and remained so until 1869,
when it began its career as an evening yell.
Geelong Advertiser, which sent its first
forme to press in November, 1840, has the
second-best claim to an old-age pension. It
beats the Portland Guardian by nearly
two years.
* * * *
“Lou”: The lady’s visit to Australia to
help in the six o’clock campaign reminds
me. Years ago I was a shandygaff hand in
a Melbourne printery where the big monthly
job was a wowser journal. Next door was
the ungodly Weekly Shocker office, and
one night, when both sheets were being-
mangled out, the Shocker double-royal
broke down as they were printing the inside
page. After an interview with me (head
serang for one night only) they brought in
their forme and paper. We lifted the
Wowser, put on the Shocker, and were
running it off nicely when our fly-boy
dropped a spanner in on the type and settled
a photo-group of variety artists. The
Shocker man was safely asleep in a Hack-
ing case, so we lifted the damaged block,
got one of Mrs. Harrison Lee, temperance
lecturer, out of the Wowser, and dropped
the poor lady into the gap in thp Shocker.
I’ve often wondered since whether the good
woman ever knew that in one desperate
week she went out to the Shocker’s little
world as something extra in high-kicker
circles.
“Chasuble”: They take a long while to
understand. Chaplain Goldsmith (styled
“Chaplain Admiral” by the mariners who
haunt Melbourne Wharf Sailors’ Institute)
has just issued a number of his little Jot-
tings From Our Log. He also habitually
distributes a monthly magazine received
from London entitled Church and Sailor.
In his last Jottings he apologised because
the Melbourne subscribers have not received
any copies of the English mag. for months.
Why? Because an order went forth from a
Government office in England that it must
not go to neutral countries, and Australia,
home of the Anzacs, was classed as neutral!
I wonder whether it was wise, after all, to
coin that new word Anzacs? It might have
given us better and much-needed advertise-
ment had we stuck to “Australians.”
•** * *
“Dalriada” : An excited quadruped, “Dun
Brown,” canters into print in B. 13/7/T6
with the statement that Melbourne Herald
published a photograph of a young man
with the remark that he “has the distinction
of having been the first to receive one of
the medals issued by the Defence Depart-
ment to those medically unfit for service.”
Would the Herald, he asks, supply a photo-
graph of a youth who had acquired the
“distinction” of failing to pass the matric?
Possibly not. It didn’t even publish this
young man’s photograph. What it printed
was a replica of the medal itself, mention-
ing incidentally the name of the first man
to receive it.
* * * *
“Full Point”: Most of the superior Maori-
lnnd dailies expurgate such words as
“drunk,” “Phryne,” etc. I “took” the
‘Magistrate’s” and the “Supreme” one day
and had just sent up half a dozen sides of
the first sort when a hurried note came
down from the sub. to say that “drunks”
must be .“inebriates,” and “Phryne” “a
female of doubtful character.” Also that
first-offending drunks must be “persons
charged with over-indulgence.” From the
Supreme Court I sent a note asking the blue
penciller how the I was to decorate the
co-re.; and I’m still waiting for an answer

A Few Bars of Australia’s Greatest
National Song', Written and Signed
by tbe Composer.
Mr. Marsh Little’s famous song, “Boys of the Dardanelles,” is known to everyone. On Anzac
Day it was played by' every band in the historic procession, and the soldiers marched into the
Lord Mayor’s Luncheon at the Town Hall, Sydney, to its stirring tune. Nearly 100,000 copies of
“Boys of the Dardanelles” have been sold, easily an Australian Song record.
In addition to Mr. Little’s wonderful gift as a Composer, he is regarded as one of Australia’s
most popular singers. He we s born at M.djee, N.S.W., and, wishing to benefit the many' Australians
who appreciate his work, he writes to us the following valued letter.—-
“It may interest you to hear that I regard your New Discovery as being a great
asset to singers and elocutionists. When appearing at the Opera House, Auckland,
N.Z., on the Fuller Vaudeville Circuit, I was so indisposed through a severe relaxed
sore throat that I thought I should have to step out of the bill. I could only deliver
my songs (in a song scena) in whispers. The stage-manager advised me try New
Discovery. I did, and the result was magical—my voice was quickly restored, and
I’ve been singing the praises of Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery ever since.
“I am, cordially yours, “(Sgd.) MARSH DITTIfE.”
Stops Coughs——Banishes Colds.
When the cold winds blow and the Winter rain falls, then look out for Coughs and Colds.
Then is the time to put Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery on the shelf where ail the family can
quickly find it when they wish. Dose a cold with Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery and the battle’s
won. Don’t let Influenza get you. Dr. Sheldon's New Discovery will keep it away. Stop that
Cough. Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery will do it, and give you a chance to sleep at night.
Winter weather is best for our health if we only take care of ourselves and keep coughs and
colds and kindred ailments at bay with Dll. SHELDON'S NEW DISCOVERY. Price 1/6 and 3/-
