S~\ THE wind, 0 the wind in the rushes l
g 1 0 the slant of tlie pale long leaves
i M That the grey light sears and crushes!
0 the shameful garland of rushes
That the naked dawn-light weaves!
It was but yesterday we rode,
Four friends, our lances glinting gay;
No evil hap did we forbode —
I think it was but yesterday.
We rode to joust before the king
in yon spired town the march encloses;
Mv Alvs was to queen the ring,
Her "shy head crowned with royal roses.
At dusk there danced across the feu
A marsh light, beautiful and red;
Had I but fallen lifeless then!
My comrades crossed themselves, and fled.
They bade me follow, in God’s name;
Their voices died on the still water.
I waded out to the weird flame,
And saw at last the Marsh King’s daugh-
ter.
O the wind in the moon-pale rushes!
0 the yielding of supple leaves
When a slim shape cleaves and brushes
A path through the Minding rushes!
0 the wind that mocks and grieves!
She stood aloof among the reeds.
“To-morrow, young Sir Knight,” she
sighed,
“I'll lead you where your own wish leads,”
By her slow smile I knew she lied.
Clear as the crescent moon she shone,
I could not stir, to follow after.
“Fair youth, await me !”—she was gone—-
“To-morrow night!” trailed back in
laughter.
A day ago—a little space....
Once, stabbed with sudden doubt and grief,
I looked down at my mirrored face;
My heart dried, like a stricken leaf.
A day ago? A life ago!
Those old sad eyes within the water
Peered out, hope-starved, through locks like
snow,
And still I wait the Marsh King's daugh-
ter.
0 the wind in the desolate rushes!
O the plaint of the pining leaves !
But the wind still treads and crushes
The fragrant swaths of the rushes,
Waste wealth of ungarnered sheaves.
Ha oh year an hour, each hour a year,
No brave thought frees me from my
dreams.
I watch the reeds, the wind I hear
Alone, save when at times it seems
Thin echoes of a far-off May
vaguely through my bleak December.
Aou loved a sweet maid once,” they say.
Her name was Alys, I remember.
VICTORIA.
ELSIE COLE.
“Marther.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MIIS. TVilliam Wiegald Nicklepitt
tiptoed into the dining-room, closed
the door with meticulous care and
turned a tragic pale face upon Mr. William
Wiegald Nicklepitt.
Mr. Nicklepitt reclined in a copious saddle-
bag armchair, with his feet stretched to an
ordinary chair on the other side of the
hearth.
William looked up from his book, and
glanced at Mrs. Nicklepitt. Apprehension
grew in his large, ox-like eyes.
“Well?” he said almost pitifully, with the
air of a man primed for the last vicissitude.
Mrs. _ Wiegald Nicklepitt stepped closer.
She pointed towards the kitchen, punctur-
ing the air many times with a rigid index
finger.
. “She’s got a man there,” said Mrs. Nickle-
pitt. Mrs. Nicklepitt spoke in a whisper,
with fearful precision.
“A man!” spluttered Mr. Nicklepitt
sitting . up. He restrained his natural
disposition to squeal just in time. His
v °ice, too, fell to a whisper: “In the kit-
chen?”
Mrs. Nicklepitt nodded sharply three
times. “She’s giving him beer!”
Mr Nicklepitt fell back into the depths
of the chair that almost engulfed his slim
figure. He seemed on the point of hurling
his book into the decanters on the side-
board, but restrained himself, partly out of
consideration for the value of the articles
on the sideboard, but also in some measure
from a craven fear of disturbing the party
in the kitchen.
“And only two bottles in the house!” said
Nicklepitt. “And those infernal Phillipson
people coming this evening ! The—the—
hound !”
Willie Nicklepitt screamed in a whisper
(it can be done) ; he tossed himself right
and left in his chair; he uttered little fierce
noises. Mr. Nieklepitt was not yet recon-
ciled to living in houses in which you could
not throw large articles about without in-
curring heavy expenses for breakages. The
most serious.disadvantage of moderate afflu-
ence was this compulsory respect for one’s
surroundings. Nicklepitt had not been
married long enough to be able to
swear unctuously and whole-heartedly in the
presence of his wife. In selecting a wife
one may indulge the refined and artistic side
of one’s nature far too much. When one
places oneself in the position of being unable
to give adequate, open expression to one’s
emotions, one violates a primal need of the
purelv animal man.
“The —the freckled griffin!” spluttered
young Mr. Nicklepitt. He blew little bubbles
in his anger. This trick of blowing small
bubbles in the corners of his mouth under
the stress of emotion was a peculiar habit
of Mr. Nieklepitt’s. “I’ll have to fortcli more.
I hate going into pubs for beer. I hate
coming out of pubs all over bottles. I won’t
do it. I won’t; so you can just drop the
idea.”
“Couldn’t you go and stop her, Willie?”
suggested Mrs. Nicklepitt in the smallest
imaginable voice.
William Wiegald Nicklepitt jumped out
of his seat. fie dashed G. B. Shaw’s
“Dramatic Opinions” down on the chair;
the springs shot it into the grate. Willie
caught up his trousers deftly, and fished
for the last button. It was gone. Securing
the all-important garment with a twist of
his left hand, Willie made for the door,
spluttering and babbling, a do-or-die look in
liis wild eyes, which at such times had a
curious affinity with soap bubbles, his small,
thin, ascetic face rigid with a splendid pur-
pose. His fingers were on the knob, he
paused, he vacillated. lie returned to his
seat, and flopped in it. There are thiugs
even a brave man may not be expected to
do.
Willie caught his wife's eye. Something
in it he resented. “Well, do it yourself!” he
said, still in a whisper. “Go on, go on, do
it yourself!”
Mrs. Nicklepitt shook her head. “I
couldn’t,” she faltered. “I—l try to, but I
can’t. I sort of fade away. My bones
melt.”
Willie clawed at his mop of hair. “You’re
weak,” he said. “You haven’t the moral
courage- of a cuttlefish. All you’ve got to
do is to rip out. Rip out, why don't you?
You’ve got to rip out at them or they
simply _ trample on you.” In his excitement
Mr. Nicklepitt had almost raised his voice,
lie dropped back to a whisper. “Rip out!”
he said.
Little Mrs. Nicklepitt was in great dis-
tress of mind. “But you can rip out ever
so much better than I can, dear. If you
would speak to her —severely, you know!”
Mr. Nicklepitt made little gurgling noises
in his throat. Fierce anger warred with
the necessity of repression. “A man can’t
go raising little hell in the kitchen,” he in-
sisted. “It isn’t his department. Fancy
me flopping about in the slops out there,
raving about duty arid conduct to a rigid
female with no more head than a hen, who
would slap the potato peelings in my- hair,
for all I know. See here, Nettie, fair’s fair
—that ain’t my half of the' contract.”
Mrs. Nicklepitt shook her head. “I sup-
pose you can’t,” she faltered miserably.
“She was telling me yesterday that the
master at one of her places attempted to
bullyrag her. She said she ‘swiped him
one’!”
“Swiped? Assaulted the man?”
“Yes —with a mop.”
“Jezebel !” hissed Willie. “Beelzebub’s
dam ! Splay-footed Amazon !”
A pop sounded from the kitchen. Mr.
Nicklepitt relapsed to the softness of his
chair. “The second cork!” he said. “All is
lost.” He fished the remains of his book
out of the fireplace, and prepared to resume
reading.
“Suppose we both face her?” Nettie sug
gested in desperation.
“Ilang her!” William retorted. “Hang
her!” he repeated, becoming shrill. “I’m
done with the she-fox.”
“Hush! She will hear you.”
William paled slightly. Both listened for
a moment. “The door’s shut?” said William
very softly. Mrs. Nicklepitt nodded. Wil-
liam breathed more freely. “Blast the whis-
kered she-cat, anyhow!” he said.
“We could have her in here, and—and
tell her,” Mrs. Nicklepitt persisted.
“You ring, then.”
“No, you ring.”
Mrs. Nicklepitt rang. She approached
the bell timidly, faltered, touched it sud-
denly, and then retired behind Willie’s chair
with marked precipitation.
“Come out!” hissed _ Mr. Nicklepitt.
“Come out.” He seized his wife’s hand and
dragged her to his side. “No ducking!” he
said. They were both pale. Willie’s lank
hair fell in wires over his haunted eyes.
They listened. A minute passed—two. •
“She's not coming,” whispered the master
of the house. A great sigh escaped him. He
had done all that was humanly possible;
nothing more could be expected of him.
Willie turned the singed leaves.
With sudden resolution Nettie went to
the bell and rang it sharply. William’s
gasp of anguish came too late to prevent
the calamity.
“Oh, I say,” he murmured, “now you've
done it!”
A few minutes of dire strain ensued, and
then the door opened and “Marther” entered.
“Marther” was about 25, a sound, well-
nourished, large-boned Australian virgin,
with 10 years’ experience of domestic ser-
vice to give her confidence in any emergency,
and a wholesome and imperishable contempt
for the heads of families and all those
miserable incompetents upon whom Provi-
dence has visited the necessity of keeping
“girls.”
There was a trying silence. Mr. Nickle-
pitt was deep in his book. Mrs. Nicklepitt’s
hand found her husband’s shoulder, and
clung to it.
“Ju ring?” ejaculated Martha.
“Dear me. yes, so I did,” said Nettie.
You —you might bring a log for the fire,
please.”
Martha looked at the flaming grate,
glanced scornfully at her mistress, and
bounced out. William turned his eyes on
his wife. They were full of reproach
touched with disdain. But when Martha
returned he was absorbed in his book again.
William had to make way for the girl. She
dashed a log on the fire. Her glance, which
William was careful not to meet, meant—
“ There, frizzle!”
“Nothin’ more?” said Martha. She made
for the door.
“Martha!” said Mrs. Nicklepitt timidlv.
“You have a visitor.”
“I have,” Martha replied. “It’s a con-
nection of me aunt’s.”
There was another silence, quite a try-
ing silence. William Wiegald Nicklepitt
was equal to the occasion. He looked up
brightly; he gave Martha quite a nice smile.
“Perhaps your friend would like a glass
of beer!” he said.
“Thank you, I’m sure,” Martha answered
disappearing. She might as well have put
the implied “for nothing” into words; every-
body understood.
“Oh, Willie!”
Mr. Nicklepitt avoided his wife’s eye
“Had to get rid of her somehow,” he barked
(but he barked in an undertone). “Fat lot
of good keeping the dashed animal standin’

This distressing condition is made worse
by drying the hands or face carelessly after

to complain, you ought. You did wonders, I
don’t think. That’s the way—everything’s
left to me. For two pins I’d go and throw
that fellow out.”
“No, no, you mustn’t do that.” Mrs.
Nieklepitt showed genuine alarm.
“Why not?” ejaculated the master. He
sprang up. The erect attitude necessitated
an expert rescue of the falling trousers.
Holding them so, a mere splinter of a man
of not more than eight stone, Mr. Nickle-
pitt did not impress the observer as an
effective instrument for hoisting unwieldy
objects. “Why not?” he insisted. “Dirty
scamp, skulking round a man’s kitchen, eat-
ing a man’s food, drinking a man’s beer!
Darned good hiding might knock some
decency into the swine. Suppose he won’t
go ?”
The sound of a sudden scuffle in the small
servery between the dining-room and the
kitchen put an end to Mr. Nicklepitt’s
heroics. He collapsed into his seat. Martha
was heard in vehement protest. A man’s
voice thick and articulate replied. The
dining-room door opened, and a large hand
appeared, holding a full glass of beer. The
hand was red, coarse and hairy, it jerked
about in the stress of the contest, and beer
was spilled on the carpet. Then a
face appeared. The face, too, was
red, coarse and hairy; the eyes closed
as an effect of advanced inebriety; the large
mouth wreathed in an amiable but uncer-
tain smile.
The intruder was still resisting force from
behind. He found it difficult to get the glass
to his lips.
“Dring yore ’ealth,” he said, and snapped
at the glass. “On’y prop’r dring yore ’ealth.”
He heaved his way into the dining-room,
towing Martha after him. Martha was
hanging on his clothing. “You come outer
this,” she commanded, breathless from her
valiant effort. “Come out of it, Jim.”
Jim leaned against the table, and broke
her hold by applying his left foot to her
waist line and exerting force, but not un-
kindly. Martha shot back into the servery.
.Tim had still some beer left in his glass.
He waved Martha an adieu, and looked
carefully round the room. At length he
located Mr. Nieklepitt. He met Mr. Nickle-
pitt’s eye and smiled fondly.
“Oh, there yar!” he said. “Looked in
dring yealth. Goo’ ole Marther sez you
stan’ beer. Well, I dring yealth, ’s one
man to ’nother.”
Mrs. Nieklepitt had retired into the cor-
ner behind her husband’s chair. Mr. Niekle-
pitt was sagged down in the saddleback,
helpless in this desperate emergency.
“Ony ri’ dring yealth,” said Jim. He
tottered forward, clutched at the mantel-
piece, and sank into the chair facing Wil-
liam. “Goo’ luck!” he said, and swung
the glass round his head, swirling the beer
all about the room. A deal of it went into
Mr. Nicklepitt’s hair. After all, Jim did
not drink. He looked darkly at his host.
“No ’fence,” said he sternly. “No ’fence.”
“Oh no, not at all,” replied Mr. Nickle-
pitt. He affected to find the situation
rather humorous. He laughed. “Not at all,
old chap,” he said.
“B’eause.” said Mr. Jim Willis cheerily,
“if there was any ’fence, I’d punch your
nose.” lie smiled most genially, and chipped
Mr. Nieklepitt gaily on the knee. “B’lieve
me I’d punch your jolly old snoot, you ole”
. Jim pulled himself up, his eye went
to Mrs. Nieklepitt. “Nearly said it!” said
he. He took careful survey of Mrs. Nickle-
pitt. and then nudged William and whisp-
ered thickly behind his hand: “Prirry girl.
Yer’ prirry girl. Why donyeh ask her shoin
us?” He had a moment of deep thought,
looking searchingly into the face of William
Nieklepitt. “Don’ know you,” said Jim.
“Don’ want know you. You sen’ for me
dring you’ realth. Tha’s all ri’. Dring you’
realth you insis’.” He examined bis glass.
It was empty. The face of Mr. Willis
clouded. “Wha’s this?” he said. “Where’s
beer? Don’ le’ no man dring my beer.
Frien’ or no frien’, mus’n’ dring feller’s
beer.” He broke into a happy smile again.
“Goin’ punch your jolly ole snoot,” he said.
“Knew I would. Le’s be ’appy.” He swung
a wild blow at Willie. Willie ducked it,
and backed away. Willie laughed. He still
pretended the whole thing was highly
humorous. “You arsk me ’aver dring.” said
Jim. “No dring. Goo’ joke. Funny man
m.'self —smash yer jore.” Jim laughed quite
heartily. “Smash yer jore,” said Jim —
“mos’ amusin’.”
But William Wiegald Nieklepitt was a
master of strategy. He opened the side-
board, and arose armed. “I suppose you
take whisky, old man?” he said.
Mr. Willis took it. He helped himself
to a full glass, and then in the nick of
time Martha arrived with reinforcements.
The newcomer was the dustman’s assistant.
The dustman’s assistant looked in, and sur-
veyed the situation with the eye of an ex-
pert.
“Remove him for a bob,” he said in a
business-like tone. “Take it or leave it.”
“I’ll give you half a crown,” gasped Wil-
iam Nieklepitt.
The dustman’s assistant got right down
to his job. Jim was hostile. There was great
doings in the dining-room for five minutes,
and while the clatter was at its highest
there came a sharp ring at the door.
“The Phillipsons!” cried Mrs. Nieklepitt.
It was the last straw. The Phillipsons
were buyers, they were Eminently Respect-
ables, people to be propitiated. Phillipson
was suspected of an intention of securing
AVillie’s big pastel, “Evening’s Ruddy
Glow.”
“You go,” hissed AA T illie. “Shut the in-
tervening door, smuggle them into the smok-
ing-room. Say the plumber’s in a fit.”
Mrs. Nieklepitt fled. Jim Willis went
under compulsion, but he dragged the dining-
table after him to the door. Then he over-
turned the ice-chest. In the kitchen a chair
was broken. He trailed the sewing machine
with him on to the verandah, off the veran-
dah, and half-way down the yard. Then
he passed.
After burrowing into some clothes, Willie
spent a pleasant evening with the Phillip-
sous, an evening devoted mainly to the
eccentricities of plumbers. It was at bed-
time Air. Nieklepitt said to Mrs. Nickle-
pitt:
“Why the devil don’t you sew some but-
tons on my trousers, Nettie? How’s a fellow
to stand up for hearth and home without a
button on his pants?”
In the morning Nettie spoke to Martha
almost reproachfully. Curiously enough
Martha was not resentful. “Acourse yore
pore ’usban’ didn’ know,” she said, “ ’n’ I'm
not denyin’ Jim’s too easy encouraged.”
EDWARD DYSON.
Three Little Inches.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
fl packer from the store jogged easily
up to the magazine for fracteur, his
packliorse trotting after, lie did it
most weeks; sometimes daily, if mining was
brisk.
“Three cases!” he called to the magazine
boss. “Gelatine! Two on the chestnut;
th’ other across me knees!”
This meant a hundred pounds of the
deadliest, and he got forty-five shillings a
week for the job.
The keeper's wife accompanied the two
to the magazine on the mountain side —even
the advent of the store packer is an event
in the ranges. She carried an infant, a
toddler held her skirt.
Straps were buckled round two cases
and the explosive hung by rope grummets
over the horns of the pack-saddle. Another
long strap encircled cases and horse.
The packer was about to mount his riding
hack ready to take the third 501 b. of sudden
death across his knees and the saddle
pommel, when both men paused as a yell
rang down the mountain side.
On top of the ridge, upon whose side the
magazine perched like a white dovecote, the
Higgs brothers were getting props for the
new aerial tram at the Dead Finish battery.
They' had fallen one directly above the
magazine—a long, straight, stringy-bark
with not a knot or twig from its butt to its
tufted top. It fell up the hill, and Joe
Iliggs climbed and struck off its head
with a couple of swift blows with his axe.
The sap was flowing free, and the crash of
its falling loosened the pole in its thick
hark casd. When Joe severed the head the
slippery polished spar shot from its sheath
butt-first down the hill. It tore through
the low scrub, gathering momentum rapidly.
“Holy Father!” gasped the magazine-
keeper, “it’ll hit the magazine !”
The yellow streak was racing straight for
the wooden building!
The packer whip-chopped his packliorse,
and it started at a canter down the hilly
track, a hundred pounds of devastation
bouncing on its back. The hack broke away
and followed. The two men and the woman
started to run.
If this w r ere fiction the magazine keeper
would pick up the toddler in an effort to
save it first. But he didn’t. He just ran,
selfishly, primitively. The mother gathered
up the astonished toddler by the slack of
his jumper and all three fell blindly down
the pack trade? Up on the hillside the
Higgses could be heard making a frenzied
scramble to escape.
“Bah!” said the packer, bitterly, after a
few paces. “What’s the good o’ runnin’?”
The two men pulled up and turned
stolidly, fatalistically, to watch the flying
tree. The woman flung herself on her
hands and knees, the two children under-
neath her. muttering again and again:
“Mother of God! Mother of God!” The
toddler fought against the flapping white
apron that enveloped him. “Wanta see
out! Wanta see out!” he bawled.
Round the building ran a post-and-wire
cattle-fence about a chain away. The fence
twanged like a bowstring as the tree hit the
No. 6 top wire near a post. It seemed to
halt. Then it rose upright on the wire,
poised a second, turned over, and shot
arrow-wise, point forward, into the roof of
the magazine porch. The three listeners
heard the rending as it tore through the
iron, through the matchboard lining, then—
crash! —through the double wall of the
magazine proper!
It stopped, quivering like a child’s dart.
The two men watched, dazed. The toddler
freed himself from the apron and chirruped
“Dad!” gleefully. Then they went to see.
The spar was held rigid by the two walls
it had transfixed. Its drill-cut point
thrummed not three inches from a stack of
blasting gelatine!
The forty-five-shilling packer took his
third case of fracteur across his knees and
resumed his job placidly.
C. LLEWELLYN GRIFFITUS.
Middle Harbor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
the darkness moved the tide
Sluicing in on every side ;
jSI Pallid, spectral, far withdrawn
Gleamed the stars that herald dawn.
From the distance, down the breeze
Came the sound of snoring seas;
But for these things all was still —
Tree and rock, and cove and hill.
Mystery, with misty hair,
Wove her wonder everywhere.
Touching with her magic hand
Tree and headland, reef and sand.
Throned she was upon the steep,
Mirrored in the velvet deep.
Every tree beneath her spell
Seemed a tall, still sentinel.
Where the silken waters crept
Through the mangrove roots she stept;
And, at times, it seemed that she
Sighed and whispered audibly.
Underneath her wonder power
Slumbered bird and bee and dower.
Then, behold !—her thraldom ceased,
And a grey light tinged the east.
The world, awake, stretched limbs and lay
A moment thinking of the day—
The toil, the task it .might not shirk ;
Then yawned and rose and went to work.
Then shoreward, rowing soft, we went,
Thrilling the while with whole content;
Each rock-pool gleamed a golden well,
And all was as a miracle.
N.SAV.
RODERIC QUINN.
